###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: OT - AS400

- **From:** floydj@netins.net (Floyd Johnson)
- **Date:** 2002-10-01T00:14:07+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<anapcf$422$1@ins22.netins.net>`
- **References:** `<ana3da$d1i$1@ins22.netins.net> <3d989660_3@Usenet.com> <3D98DAC8.40503@sympatico.ca>`

```
Donald Tees (Donald_Tees@sympatico.ca) wrote:
: In fact, if I was a 
: teacher, I'd be searching the globe for toggle switch computers, as 
: well.  You do not understand computers until you have booted one from a 
: blank memory and a set of toggle switches.

Been there - done that - back in California in 1972 (or there abouts).  It 
was truely a joy.  Not only did we have to enter the boot strap loader, we 
also had to write data acquisition routines.  It was a joy!

Do you have an old PDP-8 or PDP-11 that I can play with?  I will add it my 
collection of Apple IIs, Apple c's, TRS-80 (a model I with color graphics 
by the way [:)]), and at least one Commodore Pet.  

When the AS400 was first offered was first made, I was looking at the 
back corner of a black box.  I assumed that I was looking at the top of 
INTEL based server.  It was only as I moved around to the front that I 
realized that box was something more.  My excitement increased when I 
found that I was looking at an AS400.  

It is not a done deal - I should know within the next two months or so.

Thanks for the feedback.

Floyd Johnson
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: OT - AS400

_(thread depth: 4)_

- **From:** Donald Tees <Donald_Tees@sympatico.ca>
- **Date:** 2002-10-01T20:33:26-04:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<3D9A3ED6.9030406@sympatico.ca>`
- **References:** `<ana3da$d1i$1@ins22.netins.net> <3d989660_3@Usenet.com> <3D98DAC8.40503@sympatico.ca> <anapcf$422$1@ins22.netins.net>`

```
Floyd Johnson wrote:
> Donald Tees (Donald_Tees@sympatico.ca) wrote:
> : In fact, if I was a 
…[11 more quoted lines elided]…
> 


I wish I did ... I threw one out many years back.  Got tired of lugging 
it arround.  The PDP-8E (4k SR-33 tape reader punch) was my first home 
computer, circa 1974.  I paid better than 70 grand for it ... 4k, paper 
tape software.

I still have the 110/300 baud modem that went with it ... I used a dial 
up PDP-10 to do most of the programming using the PAL3 cross-assembler. 
  The modem carries the bell telephone logo, and serial number 142.  It 
is almost an exact match in size for my new 1 gig, 1 Mhz motherboard.  I 
could fit, (without enough room for cable or air circulation, granted) 
12 80 gigabyte hard disks in the case.

Damn, what a wonderfull time to live.  Such neat toys.  I always wanted 
my own PDP-10, actually.  I suppose what I have now is better, but 
nowhere near as neat a looking piece of gear.

You know, it would not be hard to make a neat virtual PDP-8, complete 
with a GUI front panel.  I could write an assembler for it in Cobol in 
about two hours ... write the output as a binary "tape" that could be 
dropped onto the SR-33.

Donald
```

    The US Military was still using the FADAC (Field Artillery Automatic
Computer) in 1980.  It's main memory was an 8K fixed head hard disk.
It had the fastest paper tape (read only) system I have ever heard of.

    No RAM at all.  No IC's at all.

    I wonder what they did with them.

How important?  Extrememly.  The AS/400 is a great machine with a
built in relational database.  It is most popular internationally due
to IBM's dilligence in internationalization on the platform.  Its a
solid performer.  Search the job sites - you will find as many
(possibly more) AS/400 jobs using COBOL as IBM Mainframe jobs using
COBOL.

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: TCP Client Sample Code with Winsock

- **From:** "Editor" <Editor@aboutlegacycoding.commm>
- **Date:** 2002-10-04T10:30:12-04:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<3d9da8ad$1_7@news.teranews.com>`
- **References:** `<A1Ll9.40149$8o3.981815@twister.austin.rr.com> <bfdfc3e8.0210010957.58a144f8@posting.google.com> <sgsm9.51345$Fw2.1440029@twister.austin.rr.com>`

```
I'm always amazed how little the download area of About Legacy Coding is
used.
COBOLers just don't seem to share code.

Go to any one of dozens of C++, VB or Web development Community sites and
you will find litterally thousands of code snippets, subroutines, utilities
and other helpful downloads.

Is it that our community is too technically fractured?  (i.e. platforms are
to numerous and different to make sharing code reasonable)
Or does COBOL, JCL, etc. simply not lend itself to the sharing of code?
Or is it that we all work for large corporations who frown on our sharing
even snippets of routines of what they view as Company owned code?
```

I believe that this might be a major part of the restriction on code sharing.

of 6 prior employers of varying size) have proprietary language in their hiring

papers regarding ownership of all code developed by the employee (on or off
the job).

- **From:** Arnold Trembley <arnold.trembley@worldnet.att.net>
- **Date:** 2002-10-05T09:17:02+00:00

- **Message-ID:** `<3D9EADDE.5A5C8FA2@worldnet.att.net>`

```
Sff5ky wrote:
> 
> In article <3d9da8ad$1_7@news.teranews.com>, "Editor"
…[10 more quoted lines elided]…
> the job).

Indeed, every COBOL program in my shop has a copyright notice in the
Identification Division that looks something like this:

       SKIP1
      ****************************************************************
      *SECURITY. 'CONFIDENTIAL                                       *
      *          THIS ITEM CONTAINS INFORMATION AND PROCEDURES       *
      *          WHICH ARE PROPRIETARY TO XXXXXXXXXXXXXXXXXXX        *
      *          INCORPORATED, AND WHICH ARE CONFIDENTIAL.  IT IS    *
      *          PROVIDED WITH THE EXPRESS UNDERSTANDING THAT IT     *
      *          IS TO BE USED ONLY FOR THE BENEFIT OF XXXXXXXX      *
      *          XXXXXXXXXX, AND IS NOT TO BE USED, COPIED OR        *
      *          DISCLOSED FOR ANY OTHER PURPOSE.  ANY AUTHORIZED    *
      *          REPRODUCTION (IN WHOLE OR IN PART) OF THIS          *
      *          MATERIAL MUST BE MARKED WITH THIS LEGEND.'          *
      ****************************************************************

I'm not sure what the legal status would be of any COBOL program I
wrote for my PC at home, completely unrelated to my job.  Do I own
it?  Can I give it away?  I'm not sure.  Curiously enough, I am aware
of at least one instance where my company sold several COBOL source
programs to a competitor.  I doubt they removed the copyright notice.  

There is a similar problem with acquiring free COBOL source code.  If
we didn't write it and didn't pay for it, can we legally use it?  How
would TPTB know whether or not it was any good? 

Obviously, any code that contains a company's business rules can't
easily be shared.  Yet at the same time, my employer has no qualms
about bringing in contract programmers to work on a large project. 
They must learn something about our business, and they might end a
contract with us and take another at a competing company. 
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: TCP Client Sample Code with Winsock

_(thread depth: 6)_

- **From:** "Peter E. C. Dashwood" <dashwood@nospam.enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2002-10-06T22:52:03+01:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<3da0adb0_1@Usenet.com>`
- **References:** `<3d9da8ad$1_7@news.teranews.com> <20021004232935.01003.00002494@mb-mq.aol.com> <3D9EADDE.5A5C8FA2@worldnet.att.net>`

```
This is an interesting point, Arnold.

As a contractor (in the days when I wrote COBOL code for a living) it was
quite usual for each contract to contain a clause stating that all code
written by me on their site remained their intellectual and physical
property. I always crossed it out and explained that I could not sell them
the intellectual property rights to solutions I developed because I would
then be unable to use the same solution anywhere else. Consider something
like a table searching algorithm. You come up with a "smarter" one... you
can only write and use it once, and that would be for the people where you
were currently working. Nowadays, there are many standard algorithms for
doing things, but in the old days we wrote our own and it would have been
impossible not to re-use them. No Company I worked for ever refused to
employ me because I would not sign this clause. As long as they owned the
code I wrote it was fne. As long as I retained the intellectual property to
go and write it again somewhere else, I had no problem with it either. It
was sort of like..."Well, it's yours and you have ownership of it, but I own
it too..."

This has been challenged in Court (in the 1980s in the U.K.) and found to be
a "restrictive practice". A restrictive practice is something that revents a
person from making a living. Such clauses, although still in contracts to
this day, are not legally binding, (at least, my understanding is that they
are not in the U.K...I have no idea what the US position on this would be.).

I would strongly recommend contractors to delete this clause before signing
the contract. (Yes, I know you'll say "but then I won't get the job...".
Don't be such a baby! If everyone resisted unfair clauses like this, they'd
soon disappear from "standard" contracts... Besides, contracting is a two
way street; if you have what they want, they'll bend a little in order to
get it. If you explain the position, make it clear to them that they will
have full rights to whatever you write, but you retain IP, most people are
prepared to be fair. (If they're not, you're in for a rocky ride working for
them anyway...)).

I believe the reason COBOL code is not shared as much as other languages is
because there are few "complex" techniques in COBOL. And it is so verbose
that when you do get sample code it requires a lot of time to wade through
it.

Style in COBOL is a very touchy subject and many people don't want their
coding style exposed to the World for criticism by others; then there are
many COBOL programmers who will only see the style and miss the underlying
technique (especially if they disagree with the style...people turn off when
they see code they don't "like". As Richard Plinston has observed here, most
COBOL programmers don't "like" what is unfamiliar to them).

We have all seen the "Religious Wars" here on style... they can get very
fierce. Personally, I don't mind maintaining someone else's code whatever
style they use, provided the intent is clear and they don't have a
requirement for me to learn their personal "shorthand" (a la Foodman).
During my career I have seen good and bad COBOL and dealt with it. I can't
see the point of making a crusade out of it.

Some people LOVE to have code samples and need them to really understand
what is going on (Jimmy Gavan is an example of this...Jimmy loves to see and
post code samples.) Others are content with a description of a concept and a
few coding clues. I find it tiresome to go through screeds of code when the
concept could be described in a few lines. Neither approach is "wrong".
Everybody's different.

In the case of the TCP Client sample code for use with Winsock, Paul
Raulerson opted to use Thane's sample code. That makes sense, but only if
you are determined to do this at the lowest level, in COBOL. I solved the
same problem by using a component. Not because I COULDN'T get the COBOL to
work (and I posted Paul the details of where I believed his code was
lacking), but because COBOL is NOT the best tool for the job in talking to
Sockets.

(The Sockets API is documented extensively for VB, Java, and C++ and it
works perfectly. Use COBOL you must "translate" all the parameters and the
methods of invoking them; use a component and you don't care what language
it is written in, you just get/set the properties and invoke the Methods...
It is simple, straightforward, and portable. Hard coded COBOL to the API is
not.).

I have many thousands of lines of COBOL code which I would be prepared to
share, but I suspect it has little relevance in today's World. I am slowly
converting the valuable stuff (standard Date routines, input validations,
relational DB utilities...etc.) to Components and these I am NOT prepared to
give away <G>. (Well, maybe, under special circumstances...<G>)

Pete.

- **From:** thaneh@softwaresimple.com (Thane Hubbell)

- **References:** `<3d9da8ad$1_7@news.teranews.com> <20021004232935.01003.00002494@mb-mq.aol.com> <3D9EADDE.5A5C8FA2@worldnet.att.net> <3da0adb0_1@Usenet.com>`

```
Hello Pete, 

I have a slightly different way of handling this.  Your point is well
taken and valid but the client also wants protection, and deserves it,
for things that you do that really ARE HIS.  I have the following
exception clause in my standard contract (I am "VENDOR", client is
"Short Name").

VENDOR may reuse programs and source code that have been previously
developed.  Rights to such programs and source code are granted to
(Short Name) non-exclusively.  In addition, VENDOR may in the course
of development create additional programs, source code, routines
and/or methods that VENDOR may have reason to believe will be useful
in the future.  Any such development must be of a general nature and
not specific to the project being undertaken.  Such items will be
identified by VENDOR and (Short Name) will not be charged for the cost
of such development.

Additionally, such code previously developed may be utilized in the
course of development for this contract.  When such code is used,
rights to such programs and source code are granted to (Short Name)
non-exclusively.  (Short Name) will be charged a reasonable fee for
such code and it's incorporation.

> This is an interesting point, Arnold.

>                 http://www.usenet.com

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: TCP Client Sample Code with Winsock

_(thread depth: 8)_

- **From:** "Peter E. C. Dashwood" <dashwood@nospam.enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2002-10-07T22:45:42+01:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<3da1fd6a_8@Usenet.com>`
- **References:** `<3d9da8ad$1_7@news.teranews.com> <20021004232935.01003.00002494@mb-mq.aol.com> <3D9EADDE.5A5C8FA2@worldnet.att.net> <3da0adb0_1@Usenet.com> <bfdfc3e8.0210070656.799bc2f6@posting.google.com>`

```
Hi Thane,

I like your approach and it sems fair to all concerned.

What do you say when they offer you a "standard contract" that isn't set up
on the basis you describe?

Have you ever had a Company or Agent refuse to accept your alternative?

I'd be interested to know what the variations on this are between the USA
and UK.

In my current job I have to sign contracts for my guys and deal with Agents.
There is a Law in the UK called IR35 that says if a contractor stays more
than 6 months he must be treated as a permanent employee. That means the
Company must pay holidays and withold tax at the standard rate (this is
pretty devastating for most contractors who prefer to deal directly with the
Internal Revenue as Self-Employed people and use every legal means possible
to avoid tax). Some of the contracts I have read recently have gone to
incredibly  imaginative lengths to avoid IR 35. They contain clauses that NO
Company would ever countenance, like the following...

1. The contractor can work from home or on site at his discretion. He may
also use his own equipment but the Company have the right to check it before
allowing connection to their Network.

2. The contractor is not subject to the discipline or control of the Company
but will behave in a Professional manner.

3. The Contractor is contracted to provide service for a specific piece of
work, irrespective of how long it takes.

(there are many more that made me smile)

All of this is designed to show that the Contractor is NOT a de facto
employee of the Company.

When I queried some of these clauses with the various Agents I was hastily
assured that these clauses would never be invoked but were intended purely
for avoiding IR35. The only reason I signed these contracts was because
there is a  clause which gives me (as the empowered representative of the
Corporation) the right to terminate the contract for any reason, and if any
unilateral attempt was ever made to invoke these rights, I would certainly
do so.

It is an interesting example, though, of how bright people will conspire to
get round a stupid law... It is a pity that they have to.

(BTW, I always allow them to retain IP rights to their code, as long as they
accept that we own the instance of it they write for us, and no further
claim on it will be considered <G>. When you are dealing with things that
may not be program code as is understood by COBOL, this whole area becomes
more "fuzzy". For example, If a guy writes an ACCESS macro or SQL Server
stored procedure, or processes some workflows with DTS, can he really
attempt to "copyright" it? What if someone else comes along with the same
requirement and creates the same solution (this COULD happen with COBOL
code, but it is far less likely that the solutions would be identical,
because of the many possibilities and styles of coding...with facilities
like SQL however, there is a much greater chance of accidental duplication.
There might only be ONE way to efficiently join 3 tables, and SQL is SQL; it
is not really subject to style...)

I think your idea of "non-exclusive rights" is a good and fair one. Will
give that some more thought...


Pete.


Thane Hubbell <thaneh@softwaresimple.com> wrote in message
news:bfdfc3e8.0210070656.799bc2f6@posting.google.com...
> Hello Pete,
>
…[23 more quoted lines elided]…
> "Peter E. C. Dashwood" <dashwood@nospam.enternet.co.nz> wrote in message
news:<3da0adb0_1@Usenet.com>...
> > This is an interesting point, Arnold.
> >
> > As a contractor (in the days when I wrote COBOL code for a living) it
was
> > quite usual for each contract to contain a clause stating that all code
> > written by me on their site remained their intellectual and physical
> > property. I always crossed it out and explained that I could not sell
them
> > the intellectual property rights to solutions I developed because I
would
> > then be unable to use the same solution anywhere else. Consider
something
> > like a table searching algorithm. You come up with a "smarter" one...
you
> > can only write and use it once, and that would be for the people where
you
> > were currently working. Nowadays, there are many standard algorithms for
> > doing things, but in the old days we wrote our own and it would have
been
> > impossible not to re-use them. No Company I worked for ever refused to
> > employ me because I would not sign this clause. As long as they owned
the
> > code I wrote it was fne. As long as I retained the intellectual property
to
> > go and write it again somewhere else, I had no problem with it either.
It
> > was sort of like..."Well, it's yours and you have ownership of it, but I
own
> > it too..."
> >
> > This has been challenged in Court (in the 1980s in the U.K.) and found
to be
> > a "restrictive practice". A restrictive practice is something that
revents a
> > person from making a living. Such clauses, although still in contracts
to
> > this day, are not legally binding, (at least, my understanding is that
they
> > are not in the U.K...I have no idea what the US position on this would
be.).
> >
> > I would strongly recommend contractors to delete this clause before
signing
> > the contract. (Yes, I know you'll say "but then I won't get the job...".
> > Don't be such a baby! If everyone resisted unfair clauses like this,
they'd
> > soon disappear from "standard" contracts... Besides, contracting is a
two
> > way street; if you have what they want, they'll bend a little in order
to
> > get it. If you explain the position, make it clear to them that they
will
> > have full rights to whatever you write, but you retain IP, most people
are
> > prepared to be fair. (If they're not, you're in for a rocky ride working
for
> > them anyway...)).
> >
> > I believe the reason COBOL code is not shared as much as other languages
is
> > because there are few "complex" techniques in COBOL. And it is so
verbose
> > that when you do get sample code it requires a lot of time to wade
through
> > it.
> >
> > Style in COBOL is a very touchy subject and many people don't want their
> > coding style exposed to the World for criticism by others; then there
are
> > many COBOL programmers who will only see the style and miss the
underlying
> > technique (especially if they disagree with the style...people turn off
when
> > they see code they don't "like". As Richard Plinston has observed here,
most
> > COBOL programmers don't "like" what is unfamiliar to them).
> >
> > We have all seen the "Religious Wars" here on style... they can get very
> > fierce. Personally, I don't mind maintaining someone else's code
whatever
> > style they use, provided the intent is clear and they don't have a
> > requirement for me to learn their personal "shorthand" (a la Foodman).
> > During my career I have seen good and bad COBOL and dealt with it. I
can't
> > see the point of making a crusade out of it.
> >
> > Some people LOVE to have code samples and need them to really understand
> > what is going on (Jimmy Gavan is an example of this...Jimmy loves to see
and
> > post code samples.) Others are content with a description of a concept
and a
> > few coding clues. I find it tiresome to go through screeds of code when
the
> > concept could be described in a few lines. Neither approach is "wrong".
> > Everybody's different.
> >
> > In the case of the TCP Client sample code for use with Winsock, Paul
> > Raulerson opted to use Thane's sample code. That makes sense, but only
if
> > you are determined to do this at the lowest level, in COBOL. I solved
the
> > same problem by using a component. Not because I COULDN'T get the COBOL
to
> > work (and I posted Paul the details of where I believed his code was
> > lacking), but because COBOL is NOT the best tool for the job in talking
to
> > Sockets.
> >
> > (The Sockets API is documented extensively for VB, Java, and C++ and it
> > works perfectly. Use COBOL you must "translate" all the parameters and
the
> > methods of invoking them; use a component and you don't care what
language
> > it is written in, you just get/set the properties and invoke the
Methods...
> > It is simple, straightforward, and portable. Hard coded COBOL to the API
is
> > not.).
> >
> > I have many thousands of lines of COBOL code which I would be prepared
to
> > share, but I suspect it has little relevance in today's World. I am
slowly
> > converting the valuable stuff (standard Date routines, input
validations,
> > relational DB utilities...etc.) to Components and these I am NOT
prepared to
> > give away <G>. (Well, maybe, under special circumstances...<G>)
> >
…[15 more quoted lines elided]…
> > > > Most places regardless of size (speaking from limited corporate
exposure
> > > > of 6 prior employers of varying size) have proprietary language in
their
> >  hiring
> > > >
> > > > papers regarding ownership of all code developed by the employee (on
or
> >  off
> > > > the job).
…[43 more quoted lines elided]…
> >                 http://www.usenet.com







 Posted Via Usenet.com Premium Usenet Newsgroup Services
----------------------------------------------------------
    ** SPEED ** RETENTION ** COMPLETION ** ANONYMITY **
----------------------------------------------------------        
                http://www.usenet.com

> and UK.

..Snip..

There's a ton of variation.  Some contractacts lean more toward
confidentiality than anything else.  Some are clearly written to avoid
hassles with the tax authorities.

I've never had anyone balk at my ammendment - they all realize that it
saves them time and money, which in the long run is to their benefit.

Presently I operate as an individual, but shortly I intend to
incorporate.  When that is done, the relationship is company to
company and all the questions about "are they an employee or not" go
away.

The IRS here has some simple rules:

1 - the company cannot provide the equipment or tools.
2 - the company cannot say when you work or exactly what you work on.

This is a paraphrase obviously.  Bottom line - it goes down to the
level of supervision.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: TCP Client Sample Code with Winsock

_(thread depth: 7)_

- **From:** "Editor" <Editor@aboutlegacycoding.commm>
- **Date:** 2002-10-07T11:05:46-04:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<3da1a2a1$1_7@news.teranews.com>`
- **References:** `<3d9da8ad$1_7@news.teranews.com> <20021004232935.01003.00002494@mb-mq.aol.com> <3D9EADDE.5A5C8FA2@worldnet.att.net> <3da0adb0_1@Usenet.com>`

```
I think Pete is probably closer to the truth of why COBOL code isn't shared.
The copyright issues are all true, but they are also just as true for C++ or
VB code or even Java - all of which are shared in copious amounts dispite
all these restrictive contract clasues (which I agree are not enforceable).
I'm a web developer by trade and like Pete I've see and scratched out those
clauses too.

However, there doesn't seem to be the same restrictions on using shareware
code on a corporate web site, even a complex, business critical e-commerce
site.  I guess we just don't have the years of version control procedures,
submission to production procedures, QA procedures, etc.  There is more of a
"throw it up and see if it flys" mentality to web development.  I've run
several shops where installing good QA and release procedures required a
small war with the staff.

Paul's point is also well taken - very few people "hobby" code in COBOL.
The compilers (up until Tiny Cobol like compilers) were very expensive.  And
for many people, the enviornments they are comfortable with (Mainframe) just
don't fit in their den.  Most of the code that is shared among JScript, VB
Script or Java programmers is written 'off the clock' so to speak.

Well, It's there, so I'm not going to take the shareware library on About
Legacy Coding down just because it's rarely used.  It costs me nothing to
run and since nobody contributes much, it takes very little of my time.
Perhaps as more 'freeware' compilers come into maturity I'll see more
submissions.
```

I think it is more than only a very tiny "garage" industry in COBOL every developed, due to the steep entry price into COBOL
development. A history of COBOL compilers would make a very interesting article indeed, and would show that all the "professional"
compilers, with just a very few exceptions, have always been list priced over $3K. That used to be a huge chunk of money for an
individual, and is still far from being an insignificant amount even today.

Just try to get another $3K past *my* wife without at least a 51% probability of making a return off that investment. :)

## Thread: Compile error IGYDS1089-S — 6 msgs

### Compile error IGYDS1089-S

- **From:** Mike Murrell <member@mainframeforum.com>
- **Date:** 2002-10-02T00:45:36-05:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<3d9a8800$1_6@news.onlynews.com>`

```
Environment - OS/390 R2.10

Here's the affected area of the source:

01 WS-STATS. 05 WS-TEST PIC S9(9) BINARY VALUE 0.

Receive the following compile error:

IGYDS1089-S "Binary" was invalid

BINARY is changed to COMP and it compiles with CC=0 ??

Thanks for your time.
```

#### ↳ Re: Compile error IGYDS1089-S

- **From:** "William M. Klein" <wmklein@nospam.ix.netcom.com>
- **Date:** 2002-10-02T04:18:44-05:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<anedtu$cr9$1@slb5.atl.mindspring.net>`
- **References:** `<3d9a8800$1_6@news.onlynews.com>`

```
Any chance that your source code had
   B1NARY instead of
   BINARY

(Number 1 instead of letter I)?   "BINARY" is certainly supported in any
compiler that I know of that issues IGY messages.

#### ↳ Re: Compile error IGYDS1089-S

- **From:** Mike Murrell <member@mainframeforum.com>
- **Date:** 2002-10-02T22:15:46-05:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<3d9bb662_1@news.onlynews.com>`
- **References:** `<3d9a8800$1_6@news.onlynews.com>`

```
Update

BINARY is a reserved word that I believe was introduced back in VS Cobol
II - Rel. 3

Our compiler settings for the most part are set by an OEM source mgt.
product - they can be adjusted at the module level.

One of the compiler settings being used is CMPR2. The BINARY description
will drive the compile error mentioned above using CMPR2. In order to
compile with BINARY, one must use compiler option NOCMPR2.

To be straigthforward, I'm not a Cobol programmer. I'm a mainframe
system programmer(CICS and MQ as of late). My coding is done in
assembler. One of our Cobol developers went about the business of coding
some MQ programs. He added SCSQCOBC(MQ cobol copybooks) to his IGYCRCTL
SYSLIB concatenation. Using the "been around forever" compile option
CMPR2, he encountered the IGYDS1089-S "Binary" Invalid errmsg.

I believe we have a ton of COB II style code. If I read the manual
correctly, NOCMPR2 invokes COBOL 85 vs the COBOL 74 used in CMPR2.
Fortunately, this NOCMPR2 will be used in "newly" developed MQ code and
not 20 yr. old legacy code that NOCMPR2 might impact.

Thanks for the interest.
```

##### ↳ ↳ Re: Compile error IGYDS1089-S

- **From:** "William M. Klein" <wmklein@nospam.ix.netcom.com>
- **Date:** 2002-10-02T22:50:47-05:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<angeqs$96e$1@slb4.atl.mindspring.net>`
- **References:** `<3d9a8800$1_6@news.onlynews.com> <3d9bb662_1@news.onlynews.com>`

```
It is true that when you specify CMPR2, that BINARY is *not* a reserved
word.  See:

http://publibz.boulder.ibm.com/cgi-bin/bookmgr_OS390/BOOKS/igya1101/APPENDIX
1.4.2.1

If your application programmers (and/or your OEM source mgt product) isn't
aware of it, the latest releases of IBM's COBOL compilers don't even support
the CMPR2 compiler option.  You should have SOME type of plan on migrating
to NOCMPR2 - if you expect to maintain these applications for the
foreseeable future. (especially if you want to use compilers supported by
IBM).
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Cobol's future

_(thread depth: 4)_

- **From:** "Howard Brazee" <howard.brazee@cusys.edu>
- **Date:** 2002-10-04T13:32:27+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<ank56a$f8k$1@peabody.colorado.edu>`
- **References:** `<662fce7b.0209031713.55336d3d@posting.google.com> <3d766dd5_6@Usenet.com> <anf49k$9dk$1@peabody.colorado.edu> <3d9cb7e4_4@Usenet.com>`

```

On  2-Oct-2002, "Peter E. C. Dashwood" <dashwood@nospam.enternet.co.nz> wrote:

> I'm glad you liked the article. It has certainly prompted some responses. I
> still think the best article I have contributed to this site is "Meet the
> Future"
> (http://www.aboutlegacycoding.com/?AURL=%2FArchives%2FV3%2FV30201%2Easp);

That's IMHO, the best hope for a CoBOL future - although as far as business is
concerned, we could have a replacement for CoBOL to run its functions and
business rules - plugged into a system that uses other languages.

And that's the only reason I see that has a chance of selling OO CoBOL for
mainframe, enterprise work.  Encapsulate some CoBOL business rules in an object
and use them to talk with Java and other tools.

Multiple use of an object appears to have more costs than benefits where
companies require testing of everything that touches a changed module.  (and
CoBOL's biggest strength is in its maintainability).   But no tool is the best
for all purposes.   We can have an isolated CoBOL object used for one function
only - that the user accesses through an XML/Java interface, we only have the
one thing to test - but can pick the best tools for the various portions of the
product.
```

<a id="t-d80cfcee78"></a>
## Thread: data efficiency — 3 msgs

### data efficiency

- **From:** mel5133928@aol.com (MEl5133928)
- **Date:** 2002-10-02T21:56:40+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<20021002175640.14318.00006139@mb-bj.aol.com>`

```
I was wondering whether it is more efficient when comparing numeric items to
ensure they are of the same category e.g zoned v zoned or packed-decimal v
packed-decima?. Similarly, are matching sizes and the joint presence or absence
of signs useful?

Second, when having a record layout as such:

01 WS-YEAR.
    03  WS-CCYY    PIC 9(4).
    03  WS-MM       PIC 9(2).
    03  WS-DD        PIC 9(2).

....if I was to move a PIC 9(8) field containing a value e.g 20021002 to
WS-YEAR, would the fact that WS-YEAR is alphanumeric mean that more efficient
assembler would result if redefining this area as PIC 9(8) and moving here
instead. (I know this would be necessary if the sending field was packed or
binary to ensure conversion to display format.) 

Splitting hairs I'm sure!
```

#### ↳ Re: data efficiency

- **From:** "William M. Klein" <wmklein@nospam.ix.netcom.com>
- **Date:** 2002-10-02T17:42:21-05:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<anfsok$vdg$1@slb3.atl.mindspring.net>`
- **References:** `<20021002175640.14318.00006139@mb-bj.aol.com>`

```
This is a definite "it depends".  The first question is what compiler and
operating system?

If your compiler has a "show the created assembler/object-code" option" -
use that on the specific comparisons and see what code is generated.  OFTEN
(not always) the more instructions, the less efficient.  (OTOH, there are -
on some OS's - some single instructions that are more expensive than
combinations of other instructions).   Also, do NOT assume that any tests
that you do with one set of picture clauses/usages will be the same when you
change to bigger/smaller sizes.

BOTTOM-LINE:
  Unless this comparison is in a "tight loop" in a HIGHLY speed-dependent
environment, this probably will not make a "big" difference in your total
application performance.  This isn't to say that learning "good techniques"
won't help you in the long run.  I can't think of any environments where
comparing "like" fields isn't AS good or better than comparing unlike items.
However, "getting" the fields to be the same MAY cost more than what you
would gain in total performance improvements.
```

#### ↳ Re: data efficiency

- **From:** "JerryMouse" <nospam@bisusa.com>
- **Date:** 2002-10-03T12:50:06+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<22Xm9.36907$bY5.224827@bin4.nnrp.aus1.giganews.com>`
- **References:** `<20021002175640.14318.00006139@mb-bj.aol.com>`

```

"MEl5133928" <mel5133928@aol.com> wrote in message
news:20021002175640.14318.00006139@mb-bj.aol.com...
> I was wondering whether it is more efficient when comparing numeric items
to
> ensure they are of the same category e.g zoned v zoned or packed-decimal v
> packed-decima?. Similarly, are matching sizes and the joint presence or
absence
> of signs useful?
>
…[8 more quoted lines elided]…
> WS-YEAR, would the fact that WS-YEAR is alphanumeric mean that more
efficient
> assembler would result if redefining this area as PIC 9(8) and moving here
> instead. (I know this would be necessary if the sending field was packed
or
> binary to ensure conversion to display format.)
>
> Splitting hairs I'm sure!

How hard is it for you to try this on your compiler and your system. Put the
possibilities in a 100-million iteration loop and time them.

You will find, I'll wager, less than two seconds difference in 100,000,000
comparisons. Now you have to ask whether this difference, a picosecond or
so, is worth the effort in program design.

In virtually every case we've seen, coding (or re-coding) for
micro-efficiency costs far more than it's worth.
```

- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<3D9C6132.60201@sympatico.ca>`
- **References:** `<1886042.1033653004@dbforums.com>`

```
UFREAKING wrote:
> I need help trying to call an external DLL program from power cobol 6.0.
> I am using the following call statement.
…[13 more quoted lines elided]…
> 


You do not call the DLL file, you call a routine in the file. The name 
of the program that you want to call is taken from the program id of the 
source code, the DLL is then compiled, and when it is, it creates a 
FILE.LIB.  That is specified to the linker, and the internal stuff is 
setup during linking.

Donald
```

#### ↳ Re: Power Cobol

- **From:** "JerryMouse" <nospam@bisusa.com>
- **Date:** 2002-10-03T20:54:33+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<d82n9.455435$5r1.19136422@bin5.nnrp.aus1.giganews.com>`
- **References:** `<1886042.1033653004@dbforums.com>`

```

"UFREAKING" <member@dbforums.com> wrote in message
news:1886042.1033653004@dbforums.com...
>
> I need help trying to call an external DLL program from power cobol 6.0.
…[14 more quoted lines elided]…
>

If the external DLL is another PowerCOBOL form (which was linked as a DLL),
use this syntax:

INVOKE POW-SELF 'CallForm' USING  (entryform name) (dllname)

such as:

INVOKE POW-SELF 'CallForm' USING 'MYENTRY' 'SUBPGM.DLL'

If TEMP-1 and TEMP-2 in your examples are data parameters, consider making
them GLOBAL EXTERNALs

On another matter:

You will note I specified "CallForm" using proper casing. Beware! Using all
caps in a Fujitsu PowerCOBOL INVOKE will drop you in the ditch unexpectedly.
For example:

     INVOKE LV-BOX 'ADDSTRING' USING DATANAME

moves up to 64 bytes(!) to the Listview Box. Whereas

     INVOKE LV-BOX 'AddString' USING DATANAME

moves up to 8192 bytes (the length of DATANAME).

What's more insidious are those methods/properties that return a value. For
example:

01  NUM-LIST   PIC S9(7) COMP-5 VALUE HIGH-VALUES.

     MOVE "LISTCOUNT" OF LIST-BOX TO NUM-LIST

could be WAY different from:

     MOVE "ListCount" OF LIST-BOX TO NUM-LIST.

Here's the reason for the above oddities: All CAPS implies 'backward
compatibility mode.' In the first example, ADDSTRING only moves 64 bytes. In
the last example, the return is a 2-byte COMP-5 value (S9(4)) so the last
two bytes of NUM-LIST are unchanged from whatever garbage might be left from
some prior activity.
```

<a id="t-e47c2c3e0e"></a>
## Thread: MicroFocus! Me so angry? — 2 this month / 110 total

_Topics: [`Compilers and vendors`](topics.md#compilers)_

_Thread started [2002-06](2002-06.md#t-e47c2c3e0e)._

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Future of COBOL Re: MicroFocus! Me so angry? Vendors note! (long)

_(thread depth: 8)_

- **From:** "Clark F. Morris, Jr." <cfmtech@istar.ca>
- **Date:** 2002-10-03T19:36:21-03:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<3D9CC665.6E5D3856@istar.ca>`
- **References:** `<a68c53f0.0206272247.f095a13@posting.google.com> <1025263477.52637.0@doris.uk.clara.net> <afhquj$f40$1@hyperion.eu.merant.com> <Pine.LNX.4.21.0207102120010.16224-100000@smarty.smart.net> <Fe8X8.90586$q53.2241815@twister.austin.rr.com> <Pine.LNX.4.21.0207120710460.20531-100000@smarty.smart.net> <25fb80a6.0207121311.5fb73e06@posting.google.com> <3d32c94f_3@Usenet.com>`

```
If the new standard is not widely adopted and there are not quick
follow-on enhancements on an annual or at least semi-annual basis, I
would agree with Pete that the future of COBOL is bleak.  I also agree
that the need for in-house developers is going to continue to shrink. 
However this is for reasons both that are in agreement with Peter says
about Fortress COBOL and for reasons that differ.  The needs will change
with little regard to the language of implementation or existence of
OO.  In the following discussion I am going to cover the reasons for
failure, the benefits of packages, and the real obstacles to OO COBOL.

The high cost and seeming low fulfillment of promise of traditional IT
has little to do with the adoption or lack thereof of OO COBOL.  The
factors are inadequate understanding of what it takes to develop and
maintain a useful system, personality problems, structural problems
within an organization and ideas that were ahead of the capability of
the tools.  Writing code is the simplest part of the task.  Getting a
good design is far harder and setting up the testing and validation also
is far harder and is probably given short shrift in many shops. Many
programmers find it hard to communicate with end users and their brains
are wired to understand and relate to systems rather than people (I fall
in this category).  Documentation for both the system and the use of the
system is given low priority.  Structural problems include barriers set
up by the organization so that designers and modifiers of a system are
isolated from the people who will be using it to do useful work; an
initiation and approval process that requires much effort placed on
paper results (IT may or may not have all of the blame depending on the
organization); failure to understand that things can't be done in
isolation (a change to system A may break system B); and management by
airline magazine.  These factors make the obtaining of a package very
attractive.  The documentation is provided and can be evaluated prior to
purchase.  It is easier to sell an organization on conforming to a
package than it is to an in house system.  The development and testing
is spread over a number of users.  Most companies have been burned by
customizing packages in such a manner that they can't install a new
release so they are willing to being restricted to the change mechanisms
(exits, rules tables, etc.) provided by the vendor rather than changing
vendor code.  Purchasing packages makes a lot of sense even if it puts
COBOL, C/C++ and Pascal programmers out of work.  Note that when a
company buys a package, they  probably don't ask whether it was
developed with object oriented technology.  Also package acquisition
isn't risk free.  If an organization doesn't do the hard work of
determining the business needs including such things as response times
and abilities to handle the business rules, it can have as big a fiasco
as with home-grown, maybe bigger.

OO technology may ease designing somewhat but the support mechanism of a
repository and more intelligent binding mechanisms has far greater
benefits for development and maintenance.  Since my interest and
abilities lie in the enhancement and maintenance, I will actually learn
and understand OO when I have an opportunity to maintain it.  It has
been extremely frustrating reading the literature on Object Oriented
because I think that the proponents are talking about some of the things
we discussed in the SHARE/Guide Language Futures Task Force but in a
completely foreign language.  The final report is in 1984 and probably
available from SHARE (www.share.org, 1-888-5SHARE5).  I am getting
closer to understanding it thanks to some presentations at SHARE by
Steve Ryder.

The main obstacle to adoption of OO COBOL or any other technology is not
the COBOL programmer, it is management.  We have shops that are still
stuck in COBOL 74 thinking because of management edict.  They will
rigidly hang on to old technology then turn around and bet their company
on some relatively small company that has whiz bang technology.  It is
like one of the government agencies that refused to upgrade their 3090
to a more modern, more capable system even though the savings in power
and air conditioning cost would have paid for the upgrade.  The excuse
was that the mainframe was going away.  Similarly, organizations think
that COBOL and existing applications are going to fade away so we don't
have to understand them let alone upgrade them.

"Peter E. C. Dashwood" wrote:
> 
> I've not had time to contribute much lately. However, I downloaded and read
…[455 more quoted lines elided]…
>                 http://www.usenet.com
```

```
Clark,

I read this with interest.

Comments interspersed below...

Pete.

Clark F. Morris, Jr. <cfmtech@istar.ca> wrote in message
news:3D9CC665.6E5D3856@istar.ca...
> If the new standard is not widely adopted and there are not quick
> follow-on enhancements on an annual or at least semi-annual basis, I
> would agree with Pete that the future of COBOL is bleak.

Sadly, the Standard has now lost so much credibility that it will make
little difference.

The maority of sites simply don't care about it and rely on their vendor to
implement what is useful.

What do programmers do when they need to check syntax or look at available
constructs?

Do they find a copy of the Standard and look it up? I don't think so...

They get the vendor supplied manual and see how it is implemented in their
particular environment. As there is less and less transportation of COBOL
code across platforms, there is less requirement for a "standard" language.
These days, if the code needs to be "transported", this is seen as an
opportunity to either buy a package and BPR to comply with it, or go with
Client/Server and rebuild the system with components and quick build tools.
And both these options are usually cheaper than massive COBOL migration. (It
depends on the size and criticality of the system, of course...)

> I also agree
> that the need for in-house developers is going to continue to shrink.
…[7 more quoted lines elided]…
> has little to do with the adoption or lack thereof of OO COBOL.

I agree totally. You may have misunderstood what I have said about the
necessity for OO... OO is necessary for the FUTURE of PROGRAMMING. If you
want COBOL to have a FUTURE, it is necessary for it to embrace OO. "The high
cost and seeming low fulfillment of promise of traditional IT" which you
mention has been going on for years; long before OO was even available.


> The
> factors are inadequate understanding of what it takes to develop and
…[15 more quoted lines elided]…
> airline magazine.

Wow! This is as succinct and accurate a statement of the problems in
Waterfall Development as I have ever seen. I wrote something about this in
March 2001; it was called "Shoot the Rapids; avoid the Waterfall". I
especially liked your statement about "management by airline magazine" <G>.

We have all seen the problems you describe. I believe this has nothing to do
with the programming language, rather it has to do with the Organisational
culture. However, the Waterfall promotes this culture, and COBOL (well,
Fortress COBOL anyway) has traditionally been associated with the Waterfall.

From my own experience, I have seen many of these problems evaporate, and
the culture change, as soon as the Waterfall is removed. The "right"
attitude for an IT shop is to be proud to provide service to the Business.
When the environment is changed so that IT people are able to do this, they
shine, and the whole Organisation benefits. You can never totally eliminate
the personality problems you alluded to, but I can assure you they are less
prevalent and less serious in a "happy" shop, than they are in a place where
everyone is demotivated because, try as they might, they are just unable to
deliver good solutions on time...

I recently inherited an IT team who were very much in this trap. Extremely
bright and capable people who were looking for other jobs and just
completely demotivated by the culture. In the last 8 weeks I have been
thrilled to see this turn around. Relations between the Business and IT are
improving steadily and the same guys who were perceived as being useless and
unable to deliver anything, are now recognised as champions. The single main
factor in this achievement was simply removing the Waterfall and getting
much closer involvement with the Business. (COBOL is not used in this
Company).

Bottom line: COBOL sometimes takes the rap for things which really are not
its fault...The same problems you describe above exist in shops where they
don't even use COBOL.


> These factors make the obtaining of a package very
> attractive.  The documentation is provided and can be evaluated prior to
…[9 more quoted lines elided]…
> developed with object oriented technology.

No, they don't. But it will have been. What this means is that, unlike
Fortress COBOL, it is OPEN. Standard software can interface easily to it.
The COBOL file system precludes this, yet we see never ending posts here
extolling the virtues of ISAM and Mainframe VSAM/KSDS/RRDS in native
mode...the rest of the World has moved to RDB but the Fortress resists... (I
believe this is one of the things that is killing COBOL and I have been
saying so for years...a voice in the wilderness...<G>)


> Also package acquisition
> isn't risk free.  If an organization doesn't do the hard work of
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
>

Nothing is risk free...but there is less risk of being hit by a meteorite
than by a car, when you cross the road <G>. The point is that risks should
be calculated and minimised where possible. The Package solution, in the
long run, minimises the risk.

> OO technology may ease designing somewhat but the support mechanism of a
> repository and more intelligent binding mechanisms has far greater
> benefits for development and maintenance.  Since my interest and
> abilities lie in the enhancement and maintenance, I will actually learn
> and understand OO when I have an opportunity to maintain it.

Clark, you are obviously a bright and dedicated programmer. Can I recommend
to you that you pick up "Java in 24 hours"? It is written by Rogers
Cadenhead (if I remember the name correctly; my copy is back in NZ) and it
is entertaining and informative. You will get more about OO from learning
Java that from sitting in technical lectures or reading papers on it. Once
you have the concepts in place, OO COBOL becomes an open book. (here I would
recommend Wilson Price's outstanding "Elements of Object-Oriented COBOL"
which I take everywhere with me and it is currently sitting on the shelf
above me as I write this.) You are also absolutely correct to want to work
with it as you learn. Learning by doing is by far the best way.

>It has
> been extremely frustrating reading the literature on Object Oriented
…[8 more quoted lines elided]…
> the COBOL programmer, it is management.

Well, let's be fair here...Managers are often guided by the reaction of the
shop floor. If the perception of the programmers is "Hey, it's just Modular
Programming re-invented..." then no Manager is going to race to embrace it.

I believe the resistance is on both sides (Management and Programmers) and
is fostered by the natural Human resistance to change.

> We have shops that are still
> stuck in COBOL 74 thinking because of management edict.

I am certain that there has NEVER been a Management edict that said "All
development will use the State of the Art, as available in 1974."  (except
maybe in 1974 <G>)


> They will
> rigidly hang on to old technology then turn around and bet their company
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
> was that the mainframe was going away.

When does a "Business Reason" become an "excuse"? Doesn't it rather depend
on the preference of the observer?

> Similarly, organizations think
> that COBOL and existing applications are going to fade away so we don't
> have to understand them let alone upgrade them.
>

And that is where we came in... The perception of COBOL is that of a dying
language.

And in many ways, it is correct. The Market has changed, the World has
changed, the technology has changed. The things which made COBOL viable are
no longer so critical and there are alternatives. The World has moved on but
the Fortress hasn't. As I said before:

" The Fortress has not been overrun. It has been bypassed. The "new
technology" hordes, riding lightly armed on their fast little ponies, have
simply swept past it. It will die from attrition over the next 10 years."

For it to have ANY chance in the future it would need to embrace OO. It
hasn't. Draw your own conclusions...

Regards,

Pete.

## Thread: Microfocus Cobol Migration to IBM Cobol for AIX — 6 msgs

### Microfocus Cobol Migration to IBM Cobol for AIX

- **From:** Farhad <member@mainframeforum.com>
- **Date:** 2002-10-03T17:45:40-05:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<3d9cc894$1_4@news.onlynews.com>`

```

Hello Everyone :

I'm new to this forum. Our company is in the process of moving away from
"Microfocus Cobol" to "IBM's Cobol for AIX". I'm not sure if anyone else
has been involved in recent licensing changes Microfocus has
established. But they are too expensive for our clients. IBM's Cobol (
which I was surprised to find out is too, made by Microfocus' ) is
licensed much more affordably.

Has anyone gone thru the migration process from MF Cobol, to IBM
Cobol for AIX ?

If so, can you plz. forward me any comments, notes, documentations ? I
would be very greatful for any help. I will revisit this forum daily to
look for any updates. My experiences with other forums have been less
than desirable. I hope this will change.

#### ↳ Re: Microfocus Cobol Migration to IBM Cobol for AIX

- **From:** "William M. Klein" <wmklein@nospam.ix.netcom.com>
- **Date:** 2002-10-03T17:56:10-05:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<anihug$a22$1@slb7.atl.mindspring.net>`
- **References:** `<3d9cc894$1_4@news.onlynews.com>`

```
Once upon a time and LONG ago, the IBM COBOL compiler for AIX was a
"re-packaged" Micro Focus compiler.  That hasn't been true for MANY years
now.  The IBM COBOL Set for AIX is *entirely* an IBM developed product.  It
is "pretty much" (I am not certain how much any more) the same as the IBM
VisualAge COBOL product (for Windows - and used to be for OS/2).  Its
"front-end" is also *fairly* compatible with the IBM mainframe COBOLs (but
again, I don't think they have been kept in sync - exactly).

There are many, MANY, things (extensions) in the Micro Focus product that
are NOT in the IBM compilers.  I would suggest that you try compiling your
existing COBOL source code with Micro Focus and the "FLAG(COBOL370)"
directive.  This will tell you which extensions you are using in existing
code that will NOT work with the IBM compiler.  This may be large - or it
may be small (depending on exactly how you have coded your programs).

NOTE WELL:
  The FLAG(COBOL370) directive is NOT current with the IBM compiler.  There
may be SOME items that it will flag - that are now supported with IBM COBOL
Set for AIX - for example, COMP-5 may get flagged, but is currently
supported by IBM.  However, this setting should give you a pretty good idea
of the amount of source-code changes that will be involved.  Run-time
changes (such as different results for arithmetic expressions) are another
matter, but usually not TOO bad.
```

## Thread: submit job to run at specified time — 2 msgs

_Topics: [`Jobs, careers, recruiting, salary`](topics.md#jobs)_

### submit job to run at specified time

- **From:** "Charles Hottel" <chottel@cpcug.org>
- **Date:** 2002-10-04T00:18:13+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<01c26b3b$87961e40$f991f243@chottel>`

```
I seem to remember someone on this group having this problem, so I am
reposting from the ibm.main newsgroup:

You may use the command // COMMAND '$T A,T=hh.mm,''$A jobname''' in a
batch job with TYPRUN=HOLD

 or (if you may experience problems with duplicate names), issue
                           via CONSOLE
   '$T A,T=hh.mm,'$A Jnnnn' (obtaining the job number from the
                         SUBMIT command)


The rexx program can be executed any time before the execution time

Regards
```

## Thread: object code error 153 — 1 this month / 3 total

#### ↳ Re: object code error 153

- **From:** vaclav snajdr <vsn@snajdr.de>
- **Date:** 2002-10-04T12:14:26+02:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol

##### ↳ ↳ Re: How to specify a port address in MF COBOL under DOS on PC?

- **From:** "Bob Henderson" <ntfobk@NOSPAMknowt.com>
- **Date:** 2002-10-06T12:32:33+13:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<IEKn9.3828$Os6.534607@news.xtra.co.nz>`
- **References:** `<7ytl9.1074$Os6.178052@news.xtra.co.nz> <3oorpusgpvm73fudf3qoh9rfk5gj7pbgeu@4ax.com>`

```
"Lybush" wrote:
> "Bob Henderson" wrote:
> >The sub-programs with MF Cobol include routines, (CALL X"87" and CALL
> >X"96"), to enter a hardware port and return a one or two byte value where
> >"port" is defined as a PIC 9(4) COMP-X field.
> >I want to access the parallel port. The address of this port is 0378 in
hex.
> >What value do I put into the comp-x field?
> >Thanks in advance for your replies.

> I think I once used PIC 9(5) value 957   and it worked ok.; I think it
> might also have been HEX 3BE

This morning I changed the PIC 9(4) COMP-X field to PIC 9(5) and it worked
perfectly on the first compile. (I am moving 888 decimal to the field for
HEX 0378 which is where DOS says the port is.)

Although it now works, I do not understand why I have to use pic 9(5)
instead of the 9(4) COMP-X as documented in the MF Operating Guide.
Any ideas?

> Hope this helps!

I suspect I would NEVER have discovered this from my own efforts so your
hopes are realised and I am now very happy because I have been pottering
about with this for a couple of weeks. Thank you for sharing your knowledge.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: How to specify a port address in MF COBOL under DOS on PC?

- **From:** "William M. Klein" <wmklein@nospam.ix.netcom.com>
- **Date:** 2002-10-06T00:12:10-05:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<anogne$a0h$1@slb7.atl.mindspring.net>`
- **References:** `<7ytl9.1074$Os6.178052@news.xtra.co.nz> <3oorpusgpvm73fudf3qoh9rfk5gj7pbgeu@4ax.com> <IEKn9.3828$Os6.534607@news.xtra.co.nz>`

```
There are a VAST number of Micro Focus directives that impact how
binary/comp fields are handled.  MOST of them don't impact COMP-5 fields,
but I would check your directives to see if this explained your original
"problem".  Look for directives like TRUNC, IBM-COMP, COMP-5, etc
```

## Thread: OT - Humor (for or against our "mostly male" newsgroup) — 2 msgs

_Topics: [`Off-topic and spam`](topics.md#spam) · [`Meta: FAQs, group policy, charter`](topics.md#meta)_

### OT - Humor (for or against our "mostly male" newsgroup)

- **From:** "William M. Klein" <wmklein@nospam.ix.netcom.com>
- **Date:** 2002-10-04T19:14:17-05:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<anlb50$s8h$1@slb5.atl.mindspring.net>`

```
In the hospital the relatives gathered in the waiting  room, where their
family member lay gravely ill.

Finally, the doctor came in looking tired and somber.
  "I'm afraid I'm the bearer of bad news," he said as he  surveyed the
worried faces.
  "The only hope left for your loved one at this time is  a brain
transplant.  It's an experimental procedure, very risky but it is  the only
hope. Insurance will cover the procedure, but you will have  to pay for the
brain yourselves."

The family members sat silent as they absorbed the  news. After a great
length of time, someone asked,
 "Well, how  much does a brain cost?"

The doctor quickly responded,
 "$5,000 for a male  brain, and $200 for a female brain."

The moment turned awkward.  Men in the room tried not to smile, avoiding eye
contact with the women,  but some actually smirked. A man, unable to control
his curiosity, blurted out  the question everyone wanted to ask,
 "Why is the male brain so much  more?"

The doctor smiled at the childish innocence and  explained to the entire
group,
 "It's just standard pricing procedure.  We have to mark down the price of
the female brains,  because they've  actually been used."

 SEND THIS TO A SMART WOMAN WHO NEEDS A LAUGH AND TO THE MEN YOU THINK CAN
HANDLE IT.
```

##### ↳ ↳ Re: Help to convert COBOL Data

- **From:** "William M. Klein" <wmklein@nospam.ix.netcom.com>
- **Date:** 2002-10-06T00:10:11-05:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<anogjn$tj1$1@nntp9.atl.mindspring.net>`
- **References:** `<3d9e562e$1@news.starhub.net.sg> <tdmdnQDlwpEbPz2gXTWcpw@News.GigaNews.Com>`

```
Actually, this sort-of depends on the compiler and environment.  There are
operating system/compiler combinations where "header" information is placed
into the file - and that these files "only make sense" to a COBOL
application created with the same (or compatible compiler).  There are other
environments where all the ANSI/ISO defined file types (sequential, indexed,
and relative) exist in the "operating system" software and all programming
languages and compilers can access them the same way.

From the fact that this mentions ".DBF and TXT" - my GUESS is that this may
be a Windows environment.  If a COBOL program used the common PC extension
of "Line Sequential" when creating the file, the changes are that it can be
treated as a TXT file without modification (however, if it has any COMP-?
data in it, then this isn't true - but it probably shouldn't have been line
sequential in the first place).
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Help to convert COBOL Data

- **From:** "JerryMouse" <nospam@bisusa.com>
- **Date:** 2002-10-06T15:08:57-05:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<5aednSLo96v8PzygXTWcpw@News.GigaNews.Com>`
- **References:** `<3d9e562e$1@news.starhub.net.sg> <tdmdnQDlwpEbPz2gXTWcpw@News.GigaNews.Com> <anogjn$tj1$1@nntp9.atl.mindspring.net>`

```

"William M. Klein" <wmklein@nospam.ix.netcom.com> wrote in message
news:anogjn$tj1$1@nntp9.atl.mindspring.net...
> Actually, this sort-of depends on the compiler and environment.  There are

be
> treated as a TXT file without modification (however, if it has any COMP-?
> data in it, then this isn't true - but it probably shouldn't have been
line
> sequential in the first place).

I agree.

I've created DBF and CSV files in COBOL (neither are native to COBOL) and
read both into a COBOL program. I've handled the 'header' information from
COBOL variable-length files with other languages and vice-versa. I've dug
data out of COBOL ISAM files with other versions of COBOL.

Data conversion is not trivial! It helps to have the original tools used to
create the files so you can make the conversion easier. Still, it's just
data.
```

##### ↳ ↳ Re: Help to convert COBOL Data

> "pwsource" <sales@pwsource.com.sg> wrote in message
…[8 more quoted lines elided]…

If it's got packed decimal it's a pretty good bet it came from COBOL (or maybe PL/1 if

<a id="t-030a871bbf"></a>
## Thread: OT - Amusement — 17 msgs

_Topics: [`Off-topic and spam`](topics.md#spam)_

### OT - Amusement

- **From:** "Paul Raulerson" <praulerson@hot.NOSPAM.rr.com>
- **Date:** 2002-10-06T17:31:57+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<hs_n9.78316$Fw2.2369990@twister.austin.rr.com>`

```
For y'alls enjoyment, I'll relate this little story. :) 

I'm working on a "block mode" terminal emulator project right now and quickly coded it for proof of concept using COBOL. (Well, once you guys pointed out a sample of how to use sockets from COBOL! Thanks again...! :) 

Well, it was determined that a very small part of this project needed to run inside a browser. Okay, this is a simple talk un Java, right? Just feed a Java program a byte stream, and give it some simple behavior rules, and voila! I should have a simple, easy, display coming up inside a browser. 

Hah! 

First off, Java Strings are not strings of bytes, they are "Unicode" strings. Just *try* and find what a Hex value of say, x'084' is in Unicode!  Testing turned out to be very difficult, since it is about as impossible as it can be to insert a HEX value into a string in Java. 

Ahah, says I - I will simply use an array of Bytes, since they have those things in Java. Since it is an 
array, I can look for or insert values I want in the array and then use the toString() thing to display it. 
Turns out bytes (or Bytes, which are *different* things!) are ALWAYS signed, which means, yep - you guessed it. You have to do signed conversions back and both to figure out what they heck you are looking for. (Negative values.) 

Well, after working through that nonsense, I ran into displaying the data on screen in a reasonable manner. There are (I counted them! ) over 110 (!) different classes that can or do have some effect on just opening a Window and displaying some text. The coding turns out to be simple, but figuring out WHAT to code is worse even than doing it in Smalltalk. (Until now, SmallTalk held my top place for the most capable language no-one can figure out how to operate... :) 

The long and the short of if all this is, even though I do have 3 years of working with Java, in Servlets and integrating stuff to Mainframes and under AIX/Unix, something that is a perfectly simple straightforward task in a procedural language is far far harder to do in Java. With all those objects, you wouldn't think so would you? 

To top it all off, I posted some questions in comp.lang.java.programmers and comp.lang.java.gui asking about particular points. In each group I got one answer of roughly the same quality *this* group puts out as a matter of course. The rest were, at least in some level, insulting and pretty much non-informative. (You should have seen the ones that came directly to my mailbox!) 

God save COBOL, because there certainly is a vast gulf between the expertise and professionalism of people here and people in most other groups. 

-Paul
```

#### ↳ Re: OT - Amusement

- **From:** "Tim Scrivens" <tim.scrivens heavy mung@nz.invalideds.com>

- **Message-ID:** `<anq52l$pj0$1@hermes.nz.eds.com>`

##### ↳ ↳ Re: OT - Amusement

```
Gee Peter - amazingly enough you seem to know more about me and my history than I do. 
Thanks for the tip. By the way, next time you get to pontificating on how Objects are the only 
way that COBOL will survive, check back on some of the work done in the 1980 and 
very early 1990's. I wrote some interesting stuff back then on Software Engineering and
the use of high level languages like Ada in real time defense projects. Even put it into practice in 
projects for the Navy. Found that object oriented coding is most often misleading and results in greater confusion than anything else. Object Oriented DESIGN on the other hand, saves mega dollars. Even when you are implementing something in 390 Assembler. In other words, asserting that a language must support Object Oriented Programming to survive in the future is probably not true. 

Java as used by the 'in crowd' is nothing more than a lot of buzzwords, period. The language is clean
and simple, yes. The implementation of most of the class libraries is less than awe inspiring though.
They are too complex, in many cases, named for obscurity, and in some cases, especially when they are dealing with the display behavior, are even less well designed than Microsoft MFC classes. It requires you to memorize an enormous amount of information, most of which would be better simplified or setup in a manner 
that allows one to quickly find what is needed. None of which is in place with Java right now. That is also not time well spent, not when one has things to do and is not concerned about evangelizing one language over another, or pushing one's pet project for approval or funding. 

But as you say, I am amazingly ill informed.  <grin> I'll correct my ill informed nature if you correct your 
ill mannered one. </grin>
 

  "Peter E. C. Dashwood" <dashwood@nospam.enternet.co.nz> wrote in message news:3da0a166_6@Usenet.com...
  Paul, for someone claiming three years Java you display a severe lack of understanding of it. I am not surprised the Java group were cold with you.

  Stick to COBOL. It's what you know...

  Pete.
    Paul Raulerson <praulerson@hot.NOSPAM.rr.com> wrote in message news:hs_n9.78316$Fw2.2369990@twister.austin.rr.com...
    For y'alls enjoyment, I'll relate this little story. :) 

    I'm working on a "block mode" terminal emulator project right now and quickly coded it for proof of concept using COBOL. (Well, once you guys pointed out a sample of how to use sockets from COBOL! Thanks again...! :) 

    Well, it was determined that a very small part of this project needed to run inside a browser. Okay, this is a simple talk un Java, right? Just feed a Java program a byte stream, and give it some simple behavior rules, and voila! I should have a simple, easy, display coming up inside a browser. 

    Hah! 

    First off, Java Strings are not strings of bytes, they are "Unicode" strings. Just *try* and find what a Hex value of say, x'084' is in Unicode!  Testing turned out to be very difficult, since it is about as impossible as it can be to insert a HEX value into a string in Java. 

    Ahah, says I - I will simply use an array of Bytes, since they have those things in Java. Since it is an 
    array, I can look for or insert values I want in the array and then use the toString() thing to display it. 
    Turns out bytes (or Bytes, which are *different* things!) are ALWAYS signed, which means, yep - you guessed it. You have to do signed conversions back and both to figure out what they heck you are looking for. (Negative values.) 

    Well, after working through that nonsense, I ran into displaying the data on screen in a reasonable manner. There are (I counted them! ) over 110 (!) different classes that can or do have some effect on just opening a Window and displaying some text. The coding turns out to be simple, but figuring out WHAT to code is worse even than doing it in Smalltalk. (Until now, SmallTalk held my top place for the most capable language no-one can figure out how to operate... :) 

    The long and the short of if all this is, even though I do have 3 years of working with Java, in Servlets and integrating stuff to Mainframes and under AIX/Unix, something that is a perfectly simple straightforward task in a procedural language is far far harder to do in Java. With all those objects, you wouldn't think so would you? 

    To top it all off, I posted some questions in comp.lang.java.programmers and comp.lang.java.gui asking about particular points. In each group I got one answer of roughly the same quality *this* group puts out as a matter of course. The rest were, at least in some level, insulting and pretty much non-informative. (You should have seen the ones that came directly to my mailbox!) 

    God save COBOL, because there certainly is a vast gulf between the expertise and professionalism of people here and people in most other groups. 

    -Paul
```

The reason OO programming will probably not be required in the future is the same reason it is not required today, except it a few
very limited cases. Almost any object, data nad behavior, can be implemented in almost any procedural language. (Well, FORTRAN is

There are some astoundingly *good* implementations out there as well. But if you look at the code on say, some of the cable
converters over here, you find that the implementation is both minimal, and reflects a very good design. Hardware details are
encapsulated and well tested.

I'm not a GUI person, so my judgement on GUI program designs is probably too harsh, but still, it is
a snake pit of a design. Far too many classes that inherit far too much behavior from far too many parents
to allow someone to come in and do things that are basically simple.

Now given that, I will say the blasted JTextAreas use a MVC control mechanism that I missed, which
does make it possible to deal with the thing in a relatively easy manner. But why then did no one point that out? Could it be that

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: OT - Amusement

_(thread depth: 5)_

- **From:** dashwood@enternet.co.nz (Peter E. C. Dashwood)
- **Date:** 2002-10-08T09:29:58-07:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<b3638c46.0210080829.67ac75e3@posting.google.com>`
- **References:** `<%F2o9.80056$Fw2.2434684@twister.austin.rr.com> <b3638c46.0210070152.4dc9e7ef@posting.google.com> <nLfo9.114927$121.2529925@twister.austin.rr.com>`

```
"Paul Raulerson" <praulerson@hot.NOSPAM.rr.com> wrote in message news:<nLfo9.114927$121.2529925@twister.austin.rr.com>...
> "Peter E. C. Dashwood" <dashwood@enternet.co.nz> wrote in message news:b3638c46.0210070152.4dc9e7ef@posting.google.com...
> 
> It is indeed a free world. I appreciate your viewpoint, I just don't agree >with it. (Polite) refutation above.

Now THAT is indeed healthy...and sensible <G>

I don't want to labour this because we obviously have views on it that
are Worlds apart.

I believe the future is in Objects. I believe OO Programming will be
required to manipulate these Objects. Although COBOL provides for OO
programming in its latest incarnations, there has been poor uptake by
the COBOL Community for these facilities. It therefore follows that
COBOL will not have much of a future. (Assuming my premises are
correct <G>; I agree it is arguable...).

The main reason we need OO is not for the sake of OO... it is to allow
us to participate in the World of Objects that everybody else will be
participating in over the next few years. The Market place demands
OPEN accessible systems where data can be shared and manipulated by
standard software, with a minimum of "programming".

Having to write a COBOL program every time you need a Report or Query
is no longer acceptable. That's why the COBOL file system is being
replaced by RDB even as I write this. Object Orientation makes Objects
and their Methods and Properties open and accessible. THAT is why any
programming Language that is to have a future, needs Object
Orientation.

History has shown that when the Market place is ignored, other
products rapidly move in to fill the gap...here we are talking about
300,000,000 computer users; they will not accept closed systems.

The above is about as succinct as I can present my argument; I trust
you don't find it too Papal...<G>

It causes me some sadness (after using COBOL for 37 years and having
made a very good living from it) to see that it is not a problem with
the COBOL language that may lead to its demise, but rather a problem
with the User base, who seem unable or unwilling to look at what is
happening in IT, and adapt principles and practices to deal with it.
"We've always done it this way, so it must be right... This new
fangled nonsense is just a fad; COBOL will survive like it always
has..."

This time, it won't...

You say above there is no requirement for OO and there is never likely
to be. I disagree vehemently with that, but all my arguments have been
expressed here and elsewhere so I'll leave the readership to make up
its own mind.

Finally, it was never my intention to offend you (or anyone else <G>).
My comment regarding sticking to COBOL was meant to be stinging in a
positive sense... (Maybe I was a little miffed at your unfair and
gleeful denigration of Java, but it wasn't conscious...) I'm sorry, I
could've done it better...

Cheers,

Pete.
```

- **From:** "Paul Raulerson" <praulerson@hot.NOSPAM.rr.com>
- **Date:** 2002-10-09T02:22:34+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<KpMo9.89457$Fw2.2987293@twister.austin.rr.com>`
- **References:** `<%F2o9.80056$Fw2.2434684@twister.austin.rr.com> <b3638c46.0210070152.4dc9e7ef@posting.google.com> <nLfo9.114927$121.2529925@twister.austin.rr.com> <b3638c46.0210080829.67ac75e3@posting.google.com>`

```

"Peter E. C. Dashwood" <dashwood@enternet.co.nz> wrote in message news:b3638c46.0210080829.67ac75e3@posting.google.com...
> "Paul Raulerson" <praulerson@hot.NOSPAM.rr.com> wrote in message news:<nLfo9.114927$121.2529925@twister.austin.rr.com>...
> > "Peter E. C. Dashwood" <dashwood@enternet.co.nz> wrote in message news:b3638c46.0210070152.4dc9e7ef@posting.google.com...
…[14 more quoted lines elided]…
>

LOL! Well, I guess it depends on what exactly you mean by OO. Is HTML an OO world? (XML?)
And what about PERL and co?  Yes, they have some object handling tendancies, but I sure would
not call them OO languages.  Parsing and using XML is very much an SGML thing, and languages like Omnimark, which are not procedural
(though you can do procedural things in them) handle that better than DOM or SaX. Sometimes I get to the point of thinking that C++
isn't really an OO language either - especially when I find a need to use exception handling in it.

Ada has a lot of OO concepts embedded in it (and is, in fact, my very favorite of all languages - a shameful secret! :)  but it is
also very much procedural. Ada, for those not familiar with it, think of a super-regularlized Pascal, with better compilation and
fantastic exception handling. You can also produce Top Level documentation for a project that compiles and yet reads like English.
Very *very* nice.  Still, it is not OO programming in the same sense as say SmallTalk.  (It was just pointed out to me by the way,
that SmallTalk has very few of the disadvantages of Java, and even though it has more classes, all Java development systems come
with excellent Class Browsers that make like a lot easier. If I could just remember to say object new vice new object! :)

I certainly agree with you that all programs will need to be able to handle objects in some form or another, but I am not at all
sure that means the languages will have to. COBOL for instance, has not problems calling C++ methods and objects; it is just that
the problem domain that COBOL solves has very little need for OO programming solutions, and what needs do exist in that domain are
often best solved with a RDBM and SQL. (i.e. I think CURSORS and such qualify as objects, but that is up for debate.)


> The main reason we need OO is not for the sake of OO... it is to allow
> us to participate in the World of Objects that everybody else will be
…[10 more quoted lines elided]…
>

I've not personally seen a solution where "objects" have solved the solution of writing reports yet. I've seen lots of shops where
people move the report writing to the secretary's desktop with an ODBC connection.
I find that is usually inappropriate, because the people who then select the data and write reports on it, often make fallicious
assumptions that invalidate the conclusions presented in those reports. That is mouthful, but what I mean in plain terms is simple;
they don't analyze the data to find the right information to analyze - usually because they don't know how or what they should be
looking for in the first place. DBA's help a lot in this case, but a LOT of DBAs won't spend the time to do the necessary analysis.
And they probably should not either - that is a programmer's job.

> History has shown that when the Market place is ignored, other
> products rapidly move in to fill the gap...here we are talking about
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
>
Ommm Mani Padre Ommmmmm.... Nope. :) :) :) :) :) :) :) :) :) :)

I do think that the actual trend will be more towards something like the web rather than a traditionally OO program though. I don't
mean exactly like the web today, but I expect that over the next few years, we will all get a lot better at putting encapsulated
information sources, of wildy variant type, on the web for easy access. That was one of the original selling points TBL used to get
the project accepted at CERN wasn't it? That and, I believe, the purchase of a NeXT computer. :)


> It causes me some sadness (after using COBOL for 37 years and having
> made a very good living from it) to see that it is not a problem with
…[13 more quoted lines elided]…
>

I did  - but I think what I meant is more along the lines that there is no need for OO in COBOL for the reasons I gave above. I'm
not entirely sure exactly what OO solves that cannot be solved any other way, but I am sure there are things it solves that nothing
solves better.

Java GUIs *not* being one them though!! I spend hours today just trying to get a JFormattedTextField to underline the text contained
in it. Ah well, maybe I will suceed tomorrow. The solution is NOT to create a FONT with UNDERLINE turned on. <grin>
:)

> Finally, it was never my intention to offend you (or anyone else <G>).
> My comment regarding sticking to COBOL was meant to be stinging in a
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
>

No offense taken at all - I rather enjoy hearing your opinions on things, and I certainly respect your opinions.

Yours,
-Paul

> Cheers,
>
> Pete.
