###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: OT - Amusement

_(thread depth: 7)_

- **From:** "Grinder" <grinder@no.spam.maam.com>
- **Date:** 2002-10-09T14:11:37-05:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<ao1v1a02jng@enews4.newsguy.com>`
- **References:** `<%F2o9.80056$Fw2.2434684@twister.austin.rr.com> <b3638c46.0210070152.4dc9e7ef@posting.google.com> <nLfo9.114927$121.2529925@twister.austin.rr.com> <b3638c46.0210080829.67ac75e3@posting.google.com> <KpMo9.89457$Fw2.2987293@twister.austin.rr.com>`

```
> I've not personally seen a solution where "objects" have
solved
> the solution of writing reports yet.

> I've seen lots of shops where people move the report writing
> to the secretary's desktop with an ODBC connection.
> I find that is usually inappropriate, because the people who
> then select the data and write reports on it, often make
fallicious
> assumptions that invalidate the conclusions presented in
those
> reports. That is mouthful, but what I mean in plain terms is
simple;
> they don't analyze the data to find the right information to
analyze
> - usually because they don't know how or what they should be
> looking for in the first place. DBA's help a lot in this
case, but a
> LOT of DBAs won't spend the time to do the necessary
analysis.
> And they probably should not either - that is a programmer's
job.

I agree with this up to a point.  I should underscore that this
is not an off-handed acknowledgement of your point, before
launching into a refutation.  There are genuine fallacies in
swapping the woodcutter's axe for a chainsaw -- not all of the
resulting stumps will have come from trees.

A past experience of object-oriented design that has assisted,
though not really "solved," the task of report writing may be
in order.

I worked on an application that was new development in the
500-700 k$ range, the was back-ended by an RDBMS.  There were
perhaps 150-200 principal tables, and a similar number of
windows/screens.  I wrote most of the reporting logic, though
the layouts for those reports were done by an associate.

Purely out of laziness on my part, I wrote a set of generic
reporting objects that were more-or-less SQL knock-offs. They
were taught rules about set operation, data validation, and how
to collect schema from the database, as well as the syntactic
basics.  Even though these objects would start with database
schema, I didn't go so far as to extend all of this to the
user's interface.

Instead, it served as a platform form me to code details in a
shorthand fashion.  I could for instance say, for reports on
Customers:

1)  these parameters must be provided to generate this report
2)  these parameters may be provided
3)  here's the list of columns that can be included
4)  here's the list of columns that must be included
5)  here's the list of columns that can be sorted upon
6)  here's the list of columns that can be grouped upon
7)  here's a layout template to use
8)  here's the list of available reports
9)  these columns should be totalled, averaged, min or maxed
10)  this can only be run by Executive or Accounting users
(this was a service industry application)
11) ...

This and more could be detailed for a report (or more
accurately a group of reports considering the parameters to
it) -- all in about the amount of code as text I've used to
describe it.  The user would get some options, but within
constraints imposed by the report's definition.

There are two big benefits to this "wrapper" approach:

1)  Controlled parameterization of the reports allows users
(perhaps selectively) to change format of the report without
having to know the database design.

2)  Encapsulation kept generic byte pushing seperate from
business logic.  99% of post-release development was to the
business logic -- which was concise and easy to maintain.  If,
however, a general enhancement was needed -- say going one to
multiple levels of grouping -- the generic logic could be
extended to the potential benefit of any of the specific report
objects


In the last month of the project, we scored major contract
extensions by being able to add:

1)  Report persistence -- users can save the details of a
query, only the keys, or (of course) the report's data.  (This
was not a decision left up to the user, but rather a
distinction made by the application.)

2)  Report memos -- a user with more flexible reporting
priveleges could "design" and send a report to someone else to
say "take a look at these figures."  Or, if they're convinced
of their genius (and have rights to do so,) they could make it
available to the application.

3)  Report batching -- reports may be generated on a schedule.
If a user goes after the report, the system can either provide
the last generated report or re-run it.  (A little bit of
report annotation was put in as well - a notes field was added
to the persisted report data.)

4)  Report layout editor -- not really a feature of the report
objects, but back-ended by them.  I was not really a fan of
this feature, but editor was fun to write.

> I'm not entirely sure exactly what OO solves that cannot be
solved
> any other way, but I am sure there are things it solves that
nothing
> solves better.

I believe that a system just a capable and extensible could
have been written without OO techniques.

Straight procedural code, however, would have make this task
much more time consuming.  Writing a "SaveReport" method into
hundreds (or even dozens) of reports would have made me belch
blood.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: OT - Amusement

_(thread depth: 8)_

- **From:** Donald Tees <Donald_Tees@sympatico.ca>
- **Date:** 2002-10-09T18:03:17-04:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<3DA4A7A5.8020805@sympatico.ca>`
- **References:** `<%F2o9.80056$Fw2.2434684@twister.austin.rr.com> <b3638c46.0210070152.4dc9e7ef@posting.google.com> <nLfo9.114927$121.2529925@twister.austin.rr.com> <b3638c46.0210080829.67ac75e3@posting.google.com> <KpMo9.89457$Fw2.2987293@twister.austin.rr.com> <ao1v1a02jng@enews4.newsguy.com>`

```
Grinder wrote:
>>I've not personally seen a solution where "objects" have
> solved >>the solution of writing reports yet.
> 


This caught my eye.  Perhaps I am going off on a complete tangent here, 
but having used OOP Cobol for three or four years, I think you are way 
off base ... use the right tool for the right job.

Example ... OOP *DOES* solve the problem of forms wonderfully.  I have 
an object that has nothing to do with the logic.  I do know I am going 
to have to place the answers into two hundred different formats. I do 
something like this.

      INVOKE NEW FORM-CLASS-OBJECT RETURNING INVOICE-FORM.
      MOVE "INVOICE" TO FORM-TYPE OF INVOICE-FORM.
      MOVE CUSTOMER-NAME OF CUSTOMER-RECORD
		TO MAILING-ADDRESS-LINE1 OF INVOICE-FORM.
*** etc.
      INVOKE INVOICE-OBJECT "PRINT-SELF".
      SET INVOICE-OBJECT TO NULL.

All I need to know about the form object is the picture clause and COBOL 
data name of the fields on it.  I do not have to know anything at all 
about location on the page, fonts used, white space, type of printer, 
etc., etc., etc.

I am gradually converting all my forms to standarized objects using 
cobol and the SP2 printer equivalent to their GUI interface.  The nice 
thing about it is that when done, you just take the form names and stick 
them into a pulldown list.  The user then chooses the form they want to 
use. I have several forms integrated to that point now (for example, my 
truck dispatch system has the customer truck ticket types in a pulldown 
list).

A standard "PAGE" can be an object as well, but then once you have a 
form object, there is little point.  My first four or five standard 
forms were all the standard line printer page setups, and added the 
ability to automatically preview pages, fax stuff, etc., to my standard 
old text mode "LPT1:" reports.	

I am now building the next step ... a generic collection of all forms. 
When I get THAT done, I'll be back to the point of having a decent 
spooler, which seems to have beome obsolete along the way.

I keep hearing from the theorists that I am doing things all wrong. 
Apparently, I am supposed to be building tiny little objects that get 
created and destroyed at the speed of light, and do nothing usefull in 
Cobol.  The theories, of course, are written by C programmers, and do 
not work in bussiness.  When they are applied to Cobol, they are not 
that usefull, but since that is the way things are suppose to be done, 
then nothing that is suppose to be done can be done in OOP Cobol. So the 
theorists say.

What the theorists do not seem to realize, is that we did all that stuff 
years ago when we evolved Cobol, with those neat little 
bussiness-oriented-data-objects with name attributes, and the data 
attributes so neatly encapsulated in a picture clause.  The MOVE 
statement was the first object oriented program in computer history, and 
the C programmers are just catching on. The Cobol-data-object is the 
most precisely defined data-object in the computer industry.

So.  Now we can now evolve OOP methods at the high level, instead of 
just at the low level of computer code.  There is a place for bussiness 
objects, as well as for language design objects.  Cobol has evolved just 
for that purpose.  It is just getting started, if we can get our act 
to-gether.

Donald
```

## Thread: Future of COBOL — 98 this month / 100 total

_Topics: [`COBOL's future, legacy, and obsolescence`](topics.md#future)_

### Future of COBOL

```
As a long-time former COBOL project manager/analyst/programmer
I'm sad to see COBOL's protracted demise.

With products like Delphi 7 Pro costing less than high-endCobol
compilers, offering much more power and flexibility, and
no RTLF, who would use Cobol under current conditions?
```

```
"Robert Graham" <rgraham2@nycap.rr.com> wrote in message
news:3DA1845D.7070204@nycap.rr.com...
> Next year?  How about the flavor of the month? Like XML, or
.NET, etc.
> etc.  Let's not forget J++ and J# or even Delphi.  PC based
programming
> languages change as fast as CPU chip speed.  Just as soon as
I learned
> FoxPro, it was replaced by VB.  Just as soon as I learned VB
it was
> replaced by .NET.  I was about to refresh myself in C++ when
C# came
> out.  One hundred years from now, there will still be a need
for COBOL
> programmers, however, I retire in 15 years.

I don't see anything wrong with COBOL, but I don't see what's
wrong with these progressions as well.  You talk as if change
is intrinsically bad.

Many of the things you've mentioned aren't programming
languages:

XML -- universal data format standard.  This is a really good
thing, as there's never been one before.  If it pans out,
though, is yet to be seen.

J++/J# are development environment for a trendy newcomer, Java.
In terms of COBOL, these things are closer to PowerCOBOL and
VisualAge COBOL -- not new languages, but rather
implementations of them.

Delphi is also a development environment that utilises Object
Pascal.  Pascal has been around since 1970, and object pascal
since about 1993.  Object Pascal has grown, but in a controlled
and upwardly compatible manner.  (How many months does it have
to be around before it's no longer the "flavor of the month?")

FoxPro is still around and in use -- it wasn't replaced by VB,
but probably lost some market share to developers wanting a
general development tool, rather than one embedded in a RDBMS.

.NET is not a language, it's a runtime system.  Compilers have
been written for existing languages: VB, C++, Java, Delphi's
object pascal, and other as well, such that those existing
syntaxes may be used with the runtime.

C# does qualify as a language, but in my opinion, only
marginally so.  It's an obvious attempt to rip-off Java, but
also doubles as a well-tuned syntax for the .NET runtime.  I
like it, and will most likely use it, but it has not replaced
C++.
```

- **From:** "Robert Lair" <rlair@asppages.com>

```
Hi All, good discussion...  I am a C#/.NET developer that has recently been
working with COBOL quite a bit because of NetCOBOL.  I can safely say that I
believe strongly that COBOL will never die.  There is so much existing code
that (representing billions of dollars) that have, as David said, been
debugged over the centuries.  At the same time, I believe that COBOL is here
to stay partly because of Fujitsu's NetCOBOL for .NET.  Eventually COBOL
applications are going to need to support newer technologies such as Web
Services, Windows Forms, Web Applications, etc.  Before NetCOBOL, the only
choice was to rewrite in a newer language.  However, now developers can
simply compile their applications with NetCOBOL and immediately make
avaliable all of the features supported by .NET.  This will preserve the
existing COBOL code and eliminate the need to rewrite.  In other words,
COBOL isn't going anywhere.

Just my two cents,
Bob Lair

"David Robinson" <david@nighttime.demon.invalid> wrote in message
```

```
Bob:

While I agree 100% with your statements regarding the continued
longevity of COBOL, I must respectfully disagree with you on one or
two other points.

COBOL sp2 has supported the ability to display GUI screens directly
from a COBOL program since 1989 (13 years) using nothing but COBOL
CALL USING statements.

We introduced support for GUI on the Open Look GUI which ran under the
SUN/OS operating system.  One year later, in 1990, we introduced GUI
support for PC-based COBOL compilers and Microsoft Windows 3.0.  We
have continued to enhance the software over the past 12 years.

We have offered Internet Thin Client enabling technology for over 5
years now and also offer a tool to allow for the creation of Web
Browser User Interfaces directly from a COBOL program.

We're not the only vendor, either.  There are a number of other GUI
tools which specifically support the COBOL language.....we're just one
of them.

While I agree with your assessment of the future of COBOL, I just
wanted to point out that COBOL GUI and Internet tools have been around
for quite some time now.

[snip]



Bob Wolfe
```

```
2002-10-28 14:00:00 MET

> Nothing wrong with change so long as it isn't for the sake of change.
>
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…

But also is true that the implementation of a legacy system never
went a straight line but from Zero to A to B to C and so on to the
state now. A straight way from Zero to the current state
would mean a more slender, consistent and maintainable system.
This situation gets worse with advancing age of a system.

So, at a certain time, it is a wise decision to abandon the old
high-entropy system and write it anew. One *might* decide
for another implementation language then, but that is not the
main issue.

Herwig
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Future of COBOL

_(thread depth: 7)_

- **From:** "Paul Raulerson" <praulerson@hot.NOSPAM.rr.com>
- **Date:** 2002-10-28T13:58:24+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<4obv9.232265$121.6532231@twister.austin.rr.com>`
- **References:** `<3da0b52b$0$9160$5a62ac22@freenews.iinet.net.au> <gg2o9.8633$cG1.57786599@news-text.cableinet.net> <217e491a.0210062150.7806d1af@posting.google.com> <3DA1845D.7070204@nycap.rr.com> <anspl807ol@enews1.newsguy.com> <ofpo9.951$BW6.6363303@news-text.cableinet.net> <apjcf0$kuu$1@dackel.pdb.sbs.de>`

```

"Herwig Huener" <Herwig.Huener@fujitsu-siemens.com> wrote in message news:apjcf0$kuu$1@dackel.pdb.sbs.de...
> 2002-10-28 14:00:00 MET
>
…[13 more quoted lines elided]…
>

There is much truth to this, but there are also systems that are
both well documented and have an expereinced staff to maintain
and enhance them. There are also niche products that are just so
well fitted to their niche that the primary focus of development
and maintenance for the products is efficiency, not maintainability.


> So, at a certain time, it is a wise decision to abandon the old
> high-entropy system and write it anew. One *might* decide
> for another implementation language then, but that is not the
> main issue.
>

Rewrites are always risky; extracting the business rules is NOT a trivial
thing, even for small programs.

Which is why you almost always find a compromise, where new systems are
being layered over legacy systems, and the legacy systems are being phased
our over a long period. It is not unusual for that period to encompass a decade
or longer.

Which of course simply fuels the fires of controversy- COBOL has been around for
decades with only what amounts to evolutionary change. A COBOL programmer from
1962 would be able to quikcly come up to speed with COBOL in 2002; he just wouldn't
have a grip on the support tools such as editors and so forth. A programmer from 1982
would be perfectly comfortable in the COBOL environment of today in very short order.

Now, take the average BASIC progammer from 1982, it would take that programmer
a signifigantly longer period to simply realize that something like Visual Basic *was* basic.
The paradigms have changed far more radically than for COBOL.

The same, in some measure, is true for a C programmer from 1982, a Pascal Programmer from
1982, or even a Java programmer from 1995. In short, the modern languages that do confer
so many benefits evolve much faster than large systems can handle.

In the decade it takes to move a large system to a "modern" language, that modern language will have changed more than COBOL did in
its entire history. The ROI for that is dramatically LOWER than
most people would assume.

Given that COBOL has been evolving as well, and can now do a lot of what the modern languages
can do, well... other than predjudice in the minds of inexpereinced managers, there is little reason
to move off it. It is certainly more efficient than any Java implementation, more understandable than
any C or C++ implementation, and it still does the job.

Some areas are natural points for migration away from COBOL; we services for example, are much better implemented in Java than
COBOL. However, the backend logic that is implemented by web services is
often (though not always) much better implemented in COBOL.

What does this say to me?

We ought to be writing interfaces that allow COBOL to be used by "modern" products as easily as
those same products currently use a database. And those interfaces are pretty much going to need to
be open-source, because no compiler manufactuer is going to be able to keep up with the requirements,
as well as most COBOL manufactuers are reluctant to give up a potential profit center.

That's just some of the thoughts on the subject I've been developing by the way - please don't anyone
take that as scholarly dissertation!!

-Paul


> Herwig
>
>
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Future of COBOL

_(thread depth: 5)_

- **From:** "JerryMouse" <nospam@bisusa.com>
- **Date:** 2002-10-08T18:30:29-05:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<EnWdnVPoBNoJ9z6gXTWc3g@News.GigaNews.Com>`
- **References:** `<3da0b52b$0$9160$5a62ac22@freenews.iinet.net.au> <gg2o9.8633$cG1.57786599@news-text.cableinet.net> <217e491a.0210062150.7806d1af@posting.google.com> <3DA1845D.7070204@nycap.rr.com> <anspl807ol@enews1.newsguy.com>`

```

"Grinder" <grinder@no.spam.maam.com>

> I don't see anything wrong with COBOL, but I don't see what's
> wrong with these progressions as well.  You talk as if change
> is intrinsically bad.

Change IS intrinsically bad. Change requires effort. Change requires
communication. Change requires delay, expense, and uncertainty. Change is
always on the minus side of the cost/benefit ledger.

Only if:

New  > Current + Change

is true, do you even consider change. Change for cosmetic reasons is bad.
Change for the sake of have flames rippling out of the Account Receivable
folder is bad. Change for the purpose of having your POS application play
"Happy Days Are Here Again" every time a sale for more than $100 takes place
is bad. Change for the sole purpose of using a language from a
soon-to-be-bankrupt company is bad.

Only if change is a necessary step for a desired result could change stand a
chance of acceptability. Change for change's sake is bad.

Change is an expense. Without an offsetting increase in revenue, change is
bad.

Change is like brain surgery or nuclear war: you don't do it for fun or to
prove you can.

Change is bad. Eschew change.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Future of COBOL

_(thread depth: 6)_

- **From:** dashwood@enternet.co.nz (Peter E. C. Dashwood)
- **Date:** 2002-10-09T04:30:14-07:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<b3638c46.0210090330.6d305012@posting.google.com>`
- **References:** `<3da0b52b$0$9160$5a62ac22@freenews.iinet.net.au> <gg2o9.8633$cG1.57786599@news-text.cableinet.net> <217e491a.0210062150.7806d1af@posting.google.com> <3DA1845D.7070204@nycap.rr.com> <anspl807ol@enews1.newsguy.com> <EnWdnVPoBNoJ9z6gXTWc3g@News.GigaNews.Com>`

```
Jerry,

this is the most delightful post I have seen on CLC for a very long
time <G>.

Beautifully written and thought provoking. 

Thanks a lot...

(Response below...well, I couldn't let THAT go, now, could I <G>?)

"JerryMouse" <nospam@bisusa.com> wrote in message news:<EnWdnVPoBNoJ9z6gXTWc3g@News.GigaNews.Com>...
> "Grinder" <grinder@no.spam.maam.com>
> 
…[7 more quoted lines elided]…
> 

No it isn't. The STATUS QUO is what's bad. Why? Because it means you
will settle for what you have now, never challenge it, or explore it
or try to improve it. There is NO progress without change. (However,
not all change represents progress...)

"Change requires effort" - So what is wrong with effort? Effort
throughout history has been shown to produce increases in morale,
capability, and achievement. (Edison- "Genius is 10% inspiration and
90% perspiration".) Now if, having expended effort, your reaction is
simply "I'm tired..." then the direction of the effort needs to be
reviewed <G>. Physics teaches us that you can apply as much force
(effort) to something as you like, but, until it moves, no work is
done. So the direction of the effort is important...<G>

"Change requires communication" - That one doesn't count because the
Staus Quo also requires communication. Besides, communication is such
an intrinsically valuable skill it should be developed whether you
intend to change things or not <G>

"Change requires delay, expense, and uncertainty" - Why should
changing something cause delay to it? If changes are incorporated into
the development cycle (as they are with RAD projects) change need
incur no delay whatsoever. Only with the Waterfall does change
inevitably incur delay. Expense? It might be that the change will
cause an overall reduction in cost that justifies the expense many
times over. It is neither fair nor true to say that change ALWAYS
involves expense. Uncertainty? Certainly true if you don't plan the
change. If you run a paper model of the proposed change, how would
there be uncertainty in it? What about the uncertainty over whether it
is possible to maintain the Status Quo indefinitely <G>?

"Change is always on the minus side of the cost/benefit ledger" - I am
currently involved in reviewing proposed changes to our systems. We
prioritise these changes in the first instance by using a
Cost/Benefits matrix. By far the
majority of these changes are Low Cost/Medium to High Benefit. They
certainly are NOT on the negative side of the Ledger (but then, if
they were, we wouldn't do them <G>) The flaw in the above is the use
of "always"... it makes it a sweeping statement and, as such, it is
unsupportable.


> Only if:
> 
> New  > Current + Change
> 
> is true, do you even consider change. 

Yes, that is generally true but not "always" and not "only if".
Sometimes the benefits are not quantifiable; they are nevertheless
tangible.

> Change for cosmetic reasons is bad.

Depends how important ease of use or look and feel are to you. What
may be cosmetic to me, could be ESSENTIAL to a User...

> Change for the sake of have flames rippling out of the Account Receivable
> folder is bad. 

(I loved this one <G>) My personal viewpoint here (and I agree it will
not be everybody's) is that if something makes you smile it is worth
doing. "Morale" is a very intangible thing, but it can dramatically
affect the benefits derived from a system. Humour is a very good
"attitude adjustment" mechanism. I don't think that flames rippling
out if the AR folder is bad; I think I'd like to work in a place where
they do such things...<G>

> Change for the purpose of having your POS application play
> "Happy Days Are Here Again" every time a sale for more than $100 takes place
> is bad. 
(loved this one too <G>) Same argument as above...


> Change for the sole purpose of using a language from a
> soon-to-be-bankrupt company is bad.
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
>
Hmmmm.... this contradicts what you said above... ("Change is bad"
Full stop.)

I'm never sure what people mean by "change for change's sake.." so
I'll leave that one alone.


> Change is an expense. Without an offsetting increase in revenue, change is
> bad.
>

Yes, if money is everything. This depends on the Organisation and
culture, and the specific change. Some organisations may consider the
intangible benefits of a change outweigh the expense of it.
 
> Change is like brain surgery or nuclear war: you don't do it for fun or to
> prove you can.
> 
On that we certainly agree <G>.


> Change is bad. Eschew change.

The alternative (no change, no progress) is worse! Eschew stagnation!

Thanks again, Jerry for a brilliant post (which I suspect was actually
tongue-in-cheek ?),

Pete.
```

```
Peter and Jerry:

I must agree with Doc.

To suggest that change is bad or that the status quo is bad represents
an absolute position which is indeed a generalization.

I would contend that *sometimes* change is bad and *sometimes*
maintaining the status quo is bad, but neither one is *always* bad nor
*always* good.

If we are speaking about re-writing an application in some other
language as a bad or good decision, I would contend that each and
every situation depends upon a multitude of factors each of which must
be properly considered before making the final decision.

From a business perspective, the decision to change or leave the
application alone should be based upon a careful analysis of the real
cost of change versus the real cost of doing nothing.

Quite often, these decisions are made on the basis of what is "new"
versus what is "old" with the mistaken assumption that new is
inherently good and old is inherently bad.

John Denver, may he rest in peace, learned in the last moments of his
life that the "new" experimental aircraft he was flying was probably
not as good as a properly maintained 30 year old Cessna 172.
Interesting enough, the flaw was not based upon design, but based upon
builder modifications to the design.

Conversely, had he chosen a poorly maintained Cessna 172 to fly that
day, he may have been taking an even greater risk.

New vs old...change vs status quo........none of them are absolutely
good nor absolutely bad.  Whether they are good or bad depends upon a
complex set of factors in each and every specific situation.


docdwarf@panix.com wrote:

>In article <b3638c46.0210090330.6d305012@posting.google.com>,
>Peter E. C. Dashwood <dashwood@enternet.co.nz> wrote:
…[38 more quoted lines elided]…
>


Bob Wolfe
```

```
>When that happens, work is done. If the energy is

... is, thereby, simply incorrect, at least according to the American 
Heritage Dictionay citing at http://www.dictionary.com of:

--begin quoted text:

   13.Abbr. w Physics. The transfer of energy from one physical system to
another, especially the transfer of energy to a body by the application of 
a force that moves the body in the direction of the force.

--end quoted text

Notice that although it 'especially (is) the transfer of energy to a body' 
it is primarily defined as 'the transfer of energy'; it was this 'transfer 
of energy I was referring to as an 'expenditure' in that the energy in one 
system (or part thereof) was no longer in that system or part but had been 
(according to the definition cited above) transferred to another system or 
part.

This, then, is the combination of definitions and logic I used to arrive 
at my conclusion that work is done whenever energy is 
expended/transferred; might you be so kind as to cite a source to support 
your assertion that work is not done until energy is 'converted' (your 
term)?
```

```
> --begin quoted text:
>
…[15 more quoted lines elided]…
> expended/transferred;

As always, your argument is scrupulously prepared and your authorities are
impeccable; unfortunately your conclusion, as expressed in the last sentence
above, is simply wrong.Work is done NOT "whenever energy is
expended/transferred",or even applied. Work is done when the object the
force is applied to moves...

> might you be so kind as to cite a source to support
> your assertion that work is not done until energy is 'converted' (your
> term)?
>
 Being of an inherently kind disposition, I shall do so gladly...<G>

There is a real danger in using Dictionary definitions (which are intended
for a wider public) when attempting to discuss the nitty gritty of
Mathematics and Science. Despite what it says in your Dictionary, the
science of Physics has its own definitions which are much more precise.

I was looking for something to read (I like to indulge in this ancient and
nearly forgotten practice for 20 minutes or so before going to sleep) two
nights ago (AFTER I had posted the stuff above about no work being done
until the object moves). Imagine my surprise when a book I had selected at
random from one of the bookcases in the cottage I am currently leasing, fell
open at the section on Physics... (I selected the book on my way upstairs to
bed and didn't even look at it's cover or have any idea what it dealt with;
the object was simply to read SOMETHING before sleep...)

I am quoting verbatim from a delightful volume (1 of 6) called "Practical
Knowledge for All" edited by Sir John Hammerton, in the section on "Physics"
(Volume 1, page 334). (Please note the 1940s English; I find it just
beautiful <G>)

==========================  Begin Quote  ==============================
WORK and ENERGY.

In Physics, work is said to be performed only when a force moves its point
of application.
If  force (F) when applied to a body, moves it a distance (s), in the
direction of the force, the work (W) is given as the product of the force x
distance moved:

W = Fs

Work is therefore measured in units called foot-pounds. [This is, obviously,
an English book, based on the Imperial FPS system, but the same principle
applies in the Metric CGS system...Pete]

If one tries to move a heavy box by pushing at it with all the force one can
command, NO WORK IS SAID TO BE DONE ON THE BOX UNTIL IT MOVES. [My emphasis.
Pete].

 If  by some means a heavy boulder is brought to the edge of a cliff, it is
apparent that, owing to the position it occupies, the boulder could do work
if allowed to fall over the cliff. We express this by saying that the
boulder has POTENTIAL ENERGY. Once the boulder is released, it acquires
velocity and now can do work in virtue of the motion it possesses. The
boulder is now said to have KINETIC ENERGY. This latter is actually measured
by half the product of the mass(m) and the square of the velocity (v):

i.e. kinetic energy = .5 (m(v**2)) [the book shows half mv square, but I
converted it to "computer speak" for the sake of posting plain text. Pete]

It will be seen that  the definition of energy implied in the above is "the
capability of doing work". In one case it is capable of doing work  because
of its position,  and in the other in virtue of its movement.

When the boulder is at the bottom of the cliff it can fall no lower, its
potential energy is then zero, but at that instant the velocity is
greatest - all the energy is now kinetic and is found to be exactly equal to
the potential energy it had at the top of the cliff.

This is a particular example of a general law that if the energy CHANGE ITS
FORM, it does so WITHOUT LOSS.  [again, my emphasis. To me "without loss"
means that "expended" is incorrect. The energy has NOT been "expended" even
by Doc's definition; it has simply changed form (This is what I mean by
"converted", Doc.)  Whether work was done or not during this conversion
depends on whether  the object the force was applied to, moved. Pete.]

At all times there is a conservation of energy... <remainder omitted>

=======================     end of quote ===================================
> >
> >As the sum total of all the energy in the Universe is zero (it has to be;
> >everything came from nothing), energy may just be an illusion
anyway...<G>
>
> As much as anything else, of course... and Space is Time and Time is Money
> so that's why one pays rent.
>
I pay rent because the people who own the cottage would send some of the
boys around if I didn't...<G>
> >
> >
…[8 more quoted lines elided]…
> >Hey, it was Jerry who first used "eschew"; I simply responded in kind
<G>.
>
> A 'Brooklyn Bridge defense... if someone else jumped off the Brooklyn
> Bridge then you would, too?
>
It was NO defense. (I didn't feel attacked). But while we're on the
subject... The "Brooklyn Bridge Defense" defense is a lousy one.  Whether I
would follow someone else's example and jump off the Brooklyn Bridge would
depend on the circumstances. (For example, if remaining on the bridge meant
getting burned to death, there's a fair chance I would jump...)

> >

awful!
```

- **From:** "Grinder" <grinder@no.spam.maam.com>

- **References:** `<3da0b52b$0$9160$5a62ac22@freenews.iinet.net.au> <ao18u6$c2d$1@panix1.panix.com> <3da5762e.355391@news.epix.net> <3da604f8_8@Usenet.com> <ao59tp$22o$1@panix1.panix.com> <3da80a7f_1@Usenet.com>`

```
I don't want to inject myself into the debate, but I think I
can see the source of difference in your respective opinions.


> WORK and ENERGY.
>
> In Physics, work is said to be performed only when a force
moves its point
> of application.
> If  force (F) when applied to a body, moves it a distance
(s), in the
> direction of the force, the work (W) is given as the product
of the force x
> distance moved:
>
> W = Fs


This is the classic definition of the relationship between
transfer of mechanical energy -- often called work, and a
constantly applied force.  This term, work, though is often
comandeered to describe other energy transfers.

For instance, in the model of the photoelectric effect, there
is a "work function" that describes the minimum amount of
energy required to pop an electron from a solid material --
fudge factor would be as descriptive.
```

```
I am going to take issue with that, Bob.  Corporations should ultimately 
improve society.  If they do not then they are worthless.  Capitalism 
may be a means to an end, and may even be the best means. To make it the 
ultimate goal however, is missing the point.

A corporation should have a goal beyond "making money".  How well it 
"makes money" may very well be a good measure of how well it is doing 
the job, but if that is all it is attempting to do, then it is 
ultimately going to fail.

The reason you are having the success you are having, is that you are 
attempting to give programmers a good service.  It is not because you 
are attempting to make a lot of money. A lot of CEO's, particularly in 
the States, seem to have missed that.  Corportaions exist to serve 
humans, not the other way about.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Future of COBOL

_(thread depth: 12)_

- **From:** docdwarf@panix.com
- **Date:** 2002-10-11T10:16:13-04:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<ao6mfd$d6c$1@panix1.panix.com>`
- **References:** `<3da0b52b$0$9160$5a62ac22@freenews.iinet.net.au> <3da604f8_8@Usenet.com> <3da6c0b5.526306@news.epix.net> <3DA6CF07.801@sympatico.ca>`

```
In article <3DA6CF07.801@sympatico.ca>,
Donald Tees  <Donald_Tees@sympatico.ca> wrote:
>Bob Wolfe wrote:
>> "Peter E. C. Dashwood" <dashwood@nospam.enternet.co.nz> wrote:
…[12 more quoted lines elided]…
>ultimate goal however, is missing the point.

Ow... with all due respect, Mr Tees, there seem to be a few assumptions 
going on here of which I do not see as being addressed.

A corporation (in the classic legal sense) is a legal fiction which exists 
solely to limit liability (as noted by such designations as 
'Pitscratching, Nosepicken & Sniffingbotham LLC (Limited Liability 
Corporation)').

What reasons exist for limiting liabilities?  Several... but a common one 
is to decrease the amount for which the principals of the corporation 
might be held liable should an undertaking of the corporation actualise an 
adverse risk.  Why do corporations take risks?  Quite often they do this 
in order to make money.

A comparison might be made between a corporation and a human in that 
making money to one is like eating to the other.  Some folks live to eat, 
sure, and not everyone thinks badly of them - very few people get all 
riled up about gluttons or even food critics the way they get riled up 
about corporations - but if a person does *not* eat then, quite 
frequently, so folks get *so* riled up about this that if the not-eating 
behavior goes on long enough they'll take this non-eater, toss him in a 
hole and throw dirt in his face... because he is dead.

Likewise with a corporation... you may assume that the goal of such is to 
'improve society' (whatever that might be interpreted as... a whole yuther 
ball o' wax) but unless the corporation first makes money it most likely 
won't be around long enough to do much else.

DD
```

```
I'd like to tickle the 'amass wealth' construct. In my view, wealth cannot
be 'amassed;' wealth can only be created or destroyed. If simply piled up -
amassed - the wealth is destroyed. By 'piled up,' I mean gold in the
mattress (which makes it rather lumpy I'm told) and not used in any
productive fashion - not even as collateral.

Your condition of gaining wealth with disregard for all else is well taken,
and I agree. The quest for any goal irrespective of the consequences is
often immoral. By the same token, the quest for wealth, per se, is not evil.
Further, the creation of wealth can be a means to a provident result. Who
gave more to charity last year: Bill Gates or all the 'homeless' in the
country combined?
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Future of COBOL

_(thread depth: 17)_

- **From:** "Paul Raulerson" <praulerson@hot.NOSPAM.rr.com>
- **Date:** 2002-10-18T01:54:35+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<vRJr9.155305$8o3.4413336@twister.austin.rr.com>`
- **References:** `<3da0b52b$0$9160$5a62ac22@freenews.iinet.net.au> <3da604f8_8@Usenet.com> <3da6c0b5.526306@news.epix.net> <3DA6CF07.801@sympatico.ca> <ao6mfd$d6c$1@panix1.panix.com> <3DA6E8B4.7060204@sympatico.ca> <t_KcnaUSYu8LFzqgXTWcpg@News.GigaNews.Com> <3DA7F1BF.3090509@sympatico.ca> <aommin$g77$1@peabody.colorado.edu>`

```
But then again, this *is* Microsoft we are talking about. They are without a doubt, a most un-ethical bunch of cutthroats.
Reference:

Ad Campaign Leaves Pie on Microsoft's Face
People accuse Microsoft of devious tactics all the time. Microsoft generally denies the accusations - after all, they're flanked by
the best lawyers that money can buy.

This week, though, Microsoft gave itself a big, goopy pie in the face. On Oct. 9, the company posted a testimonial on its Web site
called "Confessions of a Mac to PC Convert." It was a first-person account by a "freelance writer" about how she had fallen in love
with Windows XP. She compared the operating system to a Lexus. "I was up and running in less than one day, Girl Scout's honor,"
burbled the attractive, 20-something brunette in the photo.

There was only one problem: She doesn't exist.

A with-it member of Slashdot.org, the popular hangout for articulate nerds, happened to notice that the woman's picture actually
came from GettyImages.com, a stock-photo agency. Associated Press reporter Ted Bridis took it from there. He tracked authorship of
the article to one Valerie Mallinson, a public-relations woman hired by Microsoft to write the story. Microsoft was caught
red-handed.

I was dying to find out how this public-relations fiasco came to pass, but Microsoft spokesman Tom Pilla would speak only in
Officialese. "The article was mistakenly posted to the Microsoft Web Site," is all he would tell me. "Once we realized that it
wasn't part of the Windows XP marketing activities, we pulled it. It's an unfortunate situation, and we take responsibility."

No wonder Microsoft has become a laughingstock online. "Once we realized . . . ?" Hello? Exactly how disconnected are the right and
left hands of Microsoft's marketing organization?

And then there's the feebleness of the ad itself. Not only is it a childish attempt to mimic Apple's "Switch" campaign, but
Microsoft's bogus customer is hopelessly misinformed. "AppleWorks pales in comparison to Microsoft Office XP. There's no equivalent
for the versatility of Microsoft Word, Excel and PowerPoint," she writes, evidently never having heard of Microsoft Word, Excel and
PowerPoint for Macintosh.

Then she makes it worse: "Internet Explorer 6 does more for me than Netscape Navigator ever did. . . . I can name and organize my
Favorites any way I want." First of all, Internet Explorer is on the Mac, too. Second, had Ms. Fictitious ever, in fact, used
Netscape Navigator, she might have realized that it, too, permits naming and organizing bookmarks.

To be sure, the online community is wasting no time in rubbing these gaffes in Microsoft's face. But nobody's mentioning the most
disturbing part of all this: That it's part of a longer string of fraudulent Microsoft marketing efforts.

In 1998, the Los Angeles Times reported that Microsoft, during its antitrust trials, hired PR companies to flood newspapers with
fake letters of support, bearing ordinary individuals' names but actually written by Microsoft PR staff. Payments were funneled
through Microsoft's PR company so that the checks couldn't be traced.

Later, during the antitrust trials, Microsoft attempted to prove the inseparability of Windows and Internet Explorer by showing the
judge a video. There was only one problem: The government's lawyer noticed that as the tape rolled on, the number of icons on the
desktop kept changing. Microsoft sheepishly admitted to having spliced together footage from different computers to make its point.

And now a phony testimonial illustrated by a photo bought from a stock-art agency.

What does all of this say about a company's corporate psyche that it feels the need to fabricate evidence of the public's love?

Maybe Microsoft is jealous of the genuine affection Mac fans seem to exhibit for their machines. Or could it be that the company
somehow feels rejected by the quirky (and as far as anyone can tell, real) people in Apple's "Switch" ads.

But more likely, Microsoft's latest blunder demonstrates neither jealousy nor wounded pride - it's pure arrogance. The company
thinks it can get away with anything. This time, at least, it's wrong.

A screen shot of the original Microsoft ad can be viewed at: http://www.scripting.com/misc/msSwitchAd/lies.jpg.

Visit David Pogue on the Web at DavidPogue.com.
```

```
Peter E. C. Dashwood wrote:
> You have expressed it so eloquently there is no need for me to comment, Don.
> <G>
…[17 more quoted lines elided]…
> 


Thank you. <G>

I agree with Jerry, as you did.  There is nothing intrinsically wrong 
with a desire for wealth.  It is the idea that money is the sole form of 
wealth that irks.  A healthy planet we can live on is also a form of 
wealth, and probably a more realistic form than script issued by 
governments.

The political concept of an open marketplace makes the assumption that 
"money", in a form that can be added up and balanced, is somehow the 
true evaluation of everything.  It is unfortunate that the largest 
political entity on the planet endorses that view.

As a result, we are told that raping and pillaging are "good", as long 
as they are profitable. The fact that it is the future generations we 
are raping and pillaging makes it rather easy.  The idea that it is 
foreigners, and terrorists is more insiduous, but every bit as real.

I am a firm believer in balance.  We need capitalism, for sure.  But it 
has to be balanced with other forms of "wealth", and living in a society 
where we care for each other is a very real position of wealth for me.

At least once the table has food on it.

Donald
```

```
Leaving aside the question of 'tend to be looked down upon by whom?' 
(certainly not those who compose the cover-pages of news-magazines!) the 
motives of individuals are, as it were, secondary to the tool employed.  A 
hammer's intention is the concentrating and redirecting of force, a 
corporation's intention is to shelter its principals from risk.

>

>more" regardless of any other consideration, then I think we are 
>allowing corporations to act in ways that we do not allow individuals to

That is precisely what I had hoped to point out.  Corporations are allowed

considered to be different entities than humans; in like manner (at least
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Future of COBOL

_(thread depth: 14)_

- **From:** rtwolfe@flexus.com (Bob Wolfe)
- **Date:** 2002-10-14T15:48:31+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<3daae125.8105384@news.epix.net>`
- **References:** `<3da0b52b$0$9160$5a62ac22@freenews.iinet.net.au> <3da604f8_8@Usenet.com> <3da6c0b5.526306@news.epix.net> <3DA6CF07.801@sympatico.ca> <ao6mfd$d6c$1@panix1.panix.com> <3DA6E8B4.7060204@sympatico.ca>`

```
Donald Tees <Donald_Tees@sympatico.ca> wrote:

[snip]
>
>I would not disagree with anything you say.  None-the-less, a private 
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
>to be looked down on.

Agreed 100%.

>When the sole ethic involved in capitalism degenerates down to "make 
>more" regardless of any other consideration, then I think we are 
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
>their balance sheets.  The good ones do now.  The others end up like Enron.

Donald:

I don't believe that the sole ethic involved in capitalism denegrates
down to "make more."  It's far more compplicated than that.

Capitalism is the efficient allocation of raw materials and/or human
resources in a manner in which a person or organization is able to
earn a reasonable profit on the risk which is taken to allocate these
materials and human resources.  That says nothing about exploitation
of humans, natural resources or communities.

Making more is the objective of ONLY those capitalists who view that
as the primary objective of capitalism.  I believe that this is an
extremely short-sighted and myopic approach to business and will
ultimately lead to problems.

Long term stability of the business requires a much more intelligent
approach than simply "making more."  Smart capitalists do indeed
include people considerations as part of their day-to-day business
decision making routine.  And those same capitalists are concurrently
watching the bottom line.

I don't think that all capitalists should be stereotyped as greedy and
as only having a single focus of making more money.  I'm sure that
everyone agrees that it is false to suggest that all attorneys as
dishonest, all used car salesmen are dishonest, all politicians as
dishonest and all programmers are geeks.

The smart capitalists optimize......not maximize.  It is possible to
share  the wealth of the organization with employees, vendors,
customers, the community-at-large AND shareholders.  This is
optimization of profits, not the blind ambition of making more for the
sake of becoming rich in the short run.



Bob Wolfe
~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~

Check out The Flexus COBOL Page at http://www.flexus.com
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Future of COBOL

_(thread depth: 12)_

- **From:** rtwolfe@flexus.com (Bob Wolfe)
- **Date:** 2002-10-14T14:32:39+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<3daac255.216661@news.epix.net>`
- **References:** `<3da0b52b$0$9160$5a62ac22@freenews.iinet.net.au> <anspl807ol@enews1.newsguy.com> <EnWdnVPoBNoJ9z6gXTWc3g@News.GigaNews.Com> <b3638c46.0210090330.6d305012@posting.google.com> <ao18u6$c2d$1@panix1.panix.com> <3da5762e.355391@news.epix.net> <3da604f8_8@Usenet.com> <3da6c0b5.526306@news.epix.net> <3DA6CF07.801@sympatico.ca>`

```
Donald Tees <Donald_Tees@sympatico.ca> wrote:

>Bob Wolfe wrote:
>> "Peter E. C. Dashwood" <dashwood@nospam.enternet.co.nz> wrote:
…[12 more quoted lines elided]…
>ultimate goal however, is missing the point.

Donald:

But even the decision to work toward the improvment of society is a
cost/benefit decision.  The local communities which support the
corporate headquarters and manufacturing plants are indeed resources
to the corporation.  Exploitation of that resource is a terrible
business decision.  Nurturing that resource is a smart business
decision.

The company which chooses to support Red Cross Blood Drives, United
Way, local community non-profit associations does so at a cost to the
shareholders.  When employees are allowed to donate blood during
company working hours, solicit United Way contributions and make
arrangements for local community contributions, they are not spending
their time selling product, doing product R & D or manufacturing
product.  That is typically viewed as a cost to shareholders.

But the contributions of time and  money are also seen as a way to
improve the company's public image and make the community a better
place to live for employees and shreholders.  These factors can and do
result in increases in product sales and private investment in the
company.  

In addition, for direct monetray contributions to 501(c)3
organizations (non-profit-charitable) the corporation receives a tax
deduction on their taxable income.

While the "cause/effect" relationship is often impossible to measure,
it is nevertheless a reality, particularly in the category of
tax-deductable contributions to charities.  Corporate altruism is not
simply done out of the goodness of an executive's heart.  It is also
done to benefit the corporation.

Often, people associate the word "capitalism" with "greed" and that is
unfortunate.  While I agree that there are many greedy capitalists,
there are also hundreds of thousands of professional businesspersons
and entrepreneurs who share their corporate wealth and employees time
to improve the local community and society in general.

Take John H. McConnell of Worthington Industries.  His personal
philosophy is "We treat our customers, employees, investors, and
suppliers as we would like to be treated."  Mr. McConnell has built a
highly successful corporation and done so through honesty and good
faith business practices.  He also contributed $7.5 million to develop
the Heart/Health Center at Riverside, part of the OhioHealth
Corporation. Their gift was the largest single gift ever received by
OhioHealth Corporation or Riverside Hospital.  While the contribution
was certainly done out of the goodness of Mr. McConnell's heart, it
also means that his employees and the community in general will enjoy
better health care than perhaps otherwise.  Healthy employees are more
productive employees.

How about Dr. James Goodnight who is CEO and Co-founder of SAS?  They
treat their employees like royalty and yet they have been able to
build a massive software business.  SAS has chosen education as their
contribution to society.  The company donates millions of dollars
every year to this objective in the form of scholarships and grants.
Again, SAS contributes the funds without any expected return, but a
better educated community will provide a larger pool of future
well-educated employees for SAS.  Without a skilled labor force, SAS
may not be quite as competitive.

Countless corporations donate time, money, excess inventory,
equipment, furniture and fixtures on a daily basis to non-profit
organizations.  While it may appear that these contributions are
purely altruistic, there is no question that a community which enjoys
a better quality of life is better equipped to contribute to the
success of a corporation than a community which is struggling.
 
>A corporation should have a goal beyond "making money".  How well it 
>"makes money" may very well be a good measure of how well it is doing 
>the job, but if that is all it is attempting to do, then it is 
>ultimately going to fail.

You are assuming that making money and performing acts of altruism are
mutually exclusive and I suggest that they are not.  I agree that it
is almost impossible to assign a direct "cause and effect"
relationship between the two, I have always contended that corporate
generosity does contribute to the financial success of the
corporation.

Capitalists shouldn't all be lumped into the same category...there are
smart capitalists and there are stupid capitalists.

Stupid capitalists are the ones who think very short-term and try to
squeeze blood from a rock.  They are the capitalists who subscribe to
the philosophy of exploitation.  These are the ones in the past who
owned the company stores and company homes in the coal mining regions.
These are the capitalists who do not negotiate in good faith with
employees, customers or vendors.  They try to squeeze every penny they
can from everyone they can.  They epitomize corporate greed which
everyone despises. 

Don't misunderstand me....I'm the first one to acknowledge that a
massive number of businessmen have made millions by adopting a
short-term mentality...but ultimately these businesses struggle with
one or more personal or business problems directly attributed to their
short-term greed.

Smart capitalists are those who know that the long-term health and
wealth of the corporation is far more complex than efficient
manufacturing practices or effective resource allocation.  They know
that long term business success is best achieved when BOTH parties to
a transaction win.

In contract negotiation BOTH the corporation AND the employees must
win.

In purchase of materials, BOTH the corporation and the vendor must
win.

In the sale of finished goods BOTH the corporation and customer must
win.

...and in every case, BOTH the corporation AND the local community
must win.

This is what makes a corporation sucessful in the long run and success
makes the shareholders happy.

According to Merriam Webster's Collegiate Dictionary, the definition
is capitalism is:

an economic system characterized by private or corporate ownership of
capital goods, by investments that are determined by private decision,
and by prices, production, and the distribution of goods that are
determined mainly by competition in a free market 

The key word here is "investments."

Investing in society is just as important for the corporation as
investing in materials, capital equipment and employees.  The local
community is a key support structure for the corporation.  It supplies
the land, the laws, the employees, the transportation infrastructure,
the education, the health care, in many cases the raw materials and in
many cases the customers and shareholders of the corporation.

Smart companies don't separate community well-being from the corporate
bottom line.  To paraphrase an old saying, "Never take a crap in your
own nest."

>The reason you are having the success you are having, is that you are 
>attempting to give programmers a good service.  It is not because you 
>are attempting to make a lot of money. A lot of CEO's, particularly in 
>the States, seem to have missed that.  Corportaions exist to serve 
>humans, not the other way about.

I'm very fortunate to have a Welsh business partner, Mr. Andy Holyoak.
Many years ago, Andy educated me to the fact that long-term corporate
success is far more important than annual double digit corporate
growth.  It turns out that he was absolutely correct.  It also proved
that you can indeed teach an old dog a new trick.

I believe that your final sentence completely and totally supports my
position that smart capitalists understand that the corporation is
ultimately dependent upon humans and therefore must ultimately invest
in humans in order to be highly successful in the long-term.



Bob Wolfe
~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~
```

```
Bob Wolfe wrote:

> But even the decision to work toward the improvment of society is a
> cost/benefit decision.  The local communities which support the
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
> 

<plus a lot of other stuff that made a lot of sense, that I have clipped 
for brevity>.

I agree.  I am not a believer in 100% altruism any more than I am a 
believer in unbridled capitalism.  In fact, when someone tells me that 
their only interest is pure altruism, my defenses go into high gear.

Enlightened Capitalism (my own term), recognizes that a healthy economy 
requires a healthy society, and works on accomplishing both. In 
addition, as Doc pointed out, you cannot help feed others until you have 
managed to have some food left over.  You cannot do that without at 
least balancing the books.

Capitalism is a great tool, and the marketplace is a great place to 
force the books to balance. What I believe, however, is that "balance" 
is required in other places as well, and one of those is just how far 
you go towards balancing the books, as opposed to balancing the social 
reqirements of the people that use the books.  The two are not the exact 
same thing.

While I think that most small bussinessmen that run their own companies 
are enlightened, I do not think that true of most of our large 
international companies.  I think that most of our large international 
companies are in the control of the super-rich, and that they consider 
"their society" very far removed from the common man in the street. They 
run the corporations for the benefit of "thier" society, and so think 
that giving a million for research to a PHD making $150,000 a year is 
"charity".  It is not.  It may be beneficial their society, but it sure 
is not charity. It also does not solve the problem of poverty and/or 
dead end lives.

For 2000 years after the fall of Rome, we talked about "bread and 
circuses" as the downfall of society.  Now we are back to where giving 
tax breaks to NHL teams is considered chritable work for the good of 
society. People can be very blind.

Donald


Donald
```

```
>least balancing the books.

term intelligent capitalism, but I prefer yours.

For the most part, I believe that we are very much in agreement.

>Capitalism is a great tool, and the marketplace is a great place to 
>force the books to balance. What I believe, however, is that "balance" 
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
>same thing.

I agree with that.  It takes an open minded and intelligent
businessperson to recognize the importance of their responsibility to
contribute to the balance.

>While I think that most small bussinessmen that run their own companies

…[7 more quoted lines elided]…

For the most part I agree with your statement, but if the equipment
and personnel required to run the research lab costs half a million
per year to run and the lab is seriously working on a cure for a
terrible disease or to achieve a greater crop yield, then perhaps it
is society at large who benefits from the contribution.

pockets of the researchers......they are as despicable as the
corporate execs who earn outrageous amounts of money at the expense of
some poor schmuck who invested his life savings only to see the firm
go bankrupt.

>For 2000 years after the fall of Rome, we talked about "bread and 
>circuses" as the downfall of society.  Now we are back to where giving 
>tax breaks to NHL teams is considered chritable work for the good of 
>society. People can be very blind.

I also dispise charities which are do not really serve a charitable
purpose.  The IRS in the US are very stingy about handing out 501(c)3
designations and that's the way it should be.  I DO support corporate
contributions to legitimate charities, because it has a MUCH greater
and more direct impact on the charity than if the corporation were to
pay the tax.

1.  Remember...it is only a tax deduction.  For the million dollar
gift, the corporation only saves the percentage of taxable income
which would be paid in tax.....NOT the entire million.  So the
monetary benefit to the corporation is only about 25% to 35%.

2.  The charity gets the million bucks instead of relying on the
government to allocate it....they would only get a small fraction of
the 25% to 35% because the government would withhold their
administrative costs.

3.  The corporation can choose which charities to fund.  If the
managers make wise charitable contributions, the monies will go to
those charities who truly are involved in works which benefit society
in general.



Bob Wolfe
```

```
Except that there is no either/or choice here.

What's the difference between the two definitions of exploit below?
1 : to make productive use of : UTILIZE <exploiting your talents> <exploit your
opponent's weakness>
2 : to make use of meanly or unjustly for one's own advantage <exploiting
migrant farm workers>

It seems to be a matter of point of view about what is "just".  Each side will
always want a bit more.  The old company town that supplied housing and
everything for its workers is seen nowadays as exploitative.  But it was also
the most nurturing.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Future of COBOL

_(thread depth: 11)_

- **From:** "Peter E. C. Dashwood" <dashwood@nospam.enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2002-10-12T13:34:43+01:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol

- **References:** `<3da0b52b$0$9160$5a62ac22@freenews.iinet.net.au> <anspl807ol@enews1.newsguy.com> <EnWdnVPoBNoJ9z6gXTWc3g@News.GigaNews.Com> <b3638c46.0210090330.6d305012@posting.google.com> <ao18u6$c2d$1@panix1.panix.com> <3da5762e.355391@news.epix.net> <3da604f8_8@Usenet.com> <3da6c0b5.526306@news.epix.net>`

```

Bob Wolfe <rtwolfe@flexus.com> wrote in message
news:3da6c0b5.526306@news.epix.net...
> "Peter E. C. Dashwood" <dashwood@nospam.enternet.co.nz> wrote:
>
…[16 more quoted lines elided]…
>
No Bob, you are correct. I DID state that. However it was a rhetorical
device  (look at it in the context of the quoted original post) designed to
offset Jerry's excellent post which sparked the whole debate. Later on, I
qualified the statement.

>
> I'm not saying that certain factors are difficult to quantify.....I'm
…[8 more quoted lines elided]…
>
OK...

All of the folllowing are real cases I have had experience of at first hand.

1. The Marketing Morale factor. (Implementing this  may motivate/de-motivate
our Marketing people. How Much? Who knows...can't quantify. But it is likely
to affect our bottom line. How Much? Who knows...can't quantify.)

2. The Public perception of our Company. (Say we send out letters to
customers which are produced on a mainframe and we can't put signatures on
them (the MF IT people have never heard  of, or are resistant to, APF <G>).
Our competition send out letters produced by Word Processor. They look
professional and friendly; we look like green lineflo... How damaging is
that in terms of new customers? In terms of retaining our existing customers
(who have a choice to stay or go)?  Who knows...can't quantify. But it is
likely to affect our bottom line. How Much? Who knows...can't quantify.)

3. The time factor. (This enhancement will enable us to do various processes
more quickly. How much is it worth to get invoices out two days sooner; to
process incoming cash say, 4 days more quickly? Who knows...can't quantify.
But it is likely to affect our bottom line. How Much? Who knows...can't
quantify.) The RANGE of this can be quantified (we obviously know the total
value of our invoicing and inwards cash), but we can't quantify  what
percentage will pay early. We know that NONE will if we don't do it <G>)

4. Staff well-being.  (this is akin to the Marketing Morale mentioned above
but not exactly the same). How do we quantify changes that are intended to
make the environment more pleasant (healthy?), make working conditions less
onerous, establish a "culture" within the organisation? Say we move from a
tight hierarchic Management structure to a much more open networked
"no-blame" culture. How much is this worth? Who knows...can't quantify. But
it is likely to affect our bottom line. How Much? Who knows...can't
quantify.)

While it can be argued that if it can't be quantified it should not be
included in the Business case or the Cost/Benefits  Matrix, I believe there
are few reading this who would suggest that none of the above have a bearing
on the bottom line. Taken together they could well be the deciding factor as
to whether the Company survives or not.

My point is that it is short sighted to simply manage everything in terms of
quantifiable factors. (Although you CAN do this and it is certainly better
than not looking at ANY factors and going entirely by gut feel...)

Pete.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Future of COBOL

_(thread depth: 12)_

- **From:** rtwolfe@flexus.com (Bob Wolfe)
- **Date:** 2002-10-14T16:47:08+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<3daae984.10247795@news.epix.net>`
- **References:** `<3da0b52b$0$9160$5a62ac22@freenews.iinet.net.au> <anspl807ol@enews1.newsguy.com> <EnWdnVPoBNoJ9z6gXTWc3g@News.GigaNews.Com> <b3638c46.0210090330.6d305012@posting.google.com> <ao18u6$c2d$1@panix1.panix.com> <3da5762e.355391@news.epix.net> <3da604f8_8@Usenet.com> <3da6c0b5.526306@news.epix.net> <3da81440_4@Usenet.com>`

```
"Peter E. C. Dashwood" <dashwood@nospam.enternet.co.nz> wrote:

[snip]
>
>>
…[16 more quoted lines elided]…
>to affect our bottom line. How Much? Who knows...can't quantify.)

This can be quantified.  Look again at my comments above.  I said that
certain factors ARE difficult to quantify, but that the attempt is
still made.  The numbers are merely estimates, but they are
quantified.  At the end of the year, a more precise analysis can be
made upon the decision.

Using your example.  If we implement this, our marketing people will
be de-motivated.  This will result in an estimated decline in sales of
x%, therefore we anticipate a corresponding decline of y% in net
profit.

>2. The Public perception of our Company. (Say we send out letters to
>customers which are produced on a mainframe and we can't put signatures on
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
>likely to affect our bottom line. How Much? Who knows...can't quantify.)

Sure you can.  If the anticipated impact is trivial, the manager
probably won't even bother to attempt to quantify it because the
impact is viewed as minor.

But is the anticipated impact is significant, the quantification is
accomplished by estimating.  If businesses could predict with absolute
certainty the precise volumes which will be sold next year and the
precise amount of expense which will be incurred, then there would be
no need for budgets and forecasts.  That's not the way it works.
EVERYTHING is essentially an estimate.  It happens every day in the
business world.  They are typically referred to as:

sales forecasts, expense forecasts, budgets, estimates of quarterly
and annual profits, etc.

If a business anticipates a change in the environment in which they
operate......

employees need re-training;
anticipated recession and corresponding sales decline;
necessary replacement of machinery and equipment;
increase/decrease in availability of raw materials;
indictment of senior level executive, resulting in bad publicity;

...then they make adjustments to their estimates.....but their
estimates are still expressed in numbers.....ergo quantities.....ergo
quantifiable.  They will likely not be 100% correct after all is said
and done, but they have to make the attempt to estimate, otherwise
management of the business would be virtually impossible.

>3. The time factor. (This enhancement will enable us to do various processes
>more quickly. How much is it worth to get invoices out two days sooner; to
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
>percentage will pay early. We know that NONE will if we don't do it <G>)

Peter... I DID this when I worked for Consumers Power Company.  We
estimated the increased cash flow to the corporation based upon the
creation of a central bill processing center and the placement of a
scannable line at the top of the payment stub along with the
micro-encoding of the customer's check.

We quantified the impact of the project by making estimates which were
based on historical daily payment processing in all 8 company region
offices.  By centralizing the payment processing, we knew that we
would see a decrease in the time between receipt of payment and the
actual increase in the cash acccount balance of the company.

This WAS based upon our best estimates, but in order to proceed with
the project, we HAD to convince executive management that it was a
worthwhile risk to take.

>4. Staff well-being.  (this is akin to the Marketing Morale mentioned above
>but not exactly the same). How do we quantify changes that are intended to
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
>quantify.)

In the vast majority of typical corporations, an estimate of the
financial impact of a major decision is always made.  Otherwise senior
management will never agree to allow the change.

Quantification doesn't mean that the estimated numbers must precisely
reflect the outcome of the decision.

If raw material for a product costs $100 per unit and it is a
historical fact that exactly 10 finished products can be
manufacturered from a single raw material unit, you can say with great
precision that the material cost per finished product unit is $10.  

But we really cannot say with great precision that the final cost per
finished product unit will be $10.  What if we experience machinery
failure and a batch is ruined and the material cannot be salvaged?  We
throw away a lot of purchased raw materials which we would have used
otherwise.  This causes the average material cost to go up.  We didn't
anticipate machinery failure!  Therefore our original number of $10
per finished product unit cannot be estimated with 100% precision.
Does that mean that we should not make the attempt to quantify the
anticipated cost of raw material per finished product unit?  Of course
not.

You are confusing revenue/expense budgets with the actual
revenues/expenses.  Every business keeps both sets of numbers.
Budgets are always "educated guesses" and quantified.  Actuals are
also obviously quantified.

>While it can be argued that if it can't be quantified it should not be
>included in the Business case or the Cost/Benefits  Matrix, I believe there
>are few reading this who would suggest that none of the above have a bearing
>on the bottom line. Taken together they could well be the deciding factor as
>to whether the Company survives or not.

I believe that all of the above have a bearing on the bottom line and
if management anticipates that the impact of said decision will
significantly effect revenues or expense, an estimate is made which is
a an attempt to quantify the impact os said decision.

>My point is that it is short sighted to simply manage everything in terms of
>quantifiable factors. (Although you CAN do this and it is certainly better
>than not looking at ANY factors and going entirely by gut feel...)

The gut feeling is often the basis for the estimate.

1.  I have a box of apples (not quantified).
2.  I have a box of approximately 20 apples (quantified based upon an
estimate)
3.  I have a box of exactly 22 apples (quantified likely based upon an
actual count of the apples)

Items 2 AND 3 are both quantified.  One is an estimate and the other
is an actual....but they are BOTH quantified.



Bob Wolfe
```

```
Bob,

I read your response and considered it.

Sorry, but we disagree on something fundamental here.

While I accept that an estimate or "best guess" does supply a number, it is
NOT quantified in the sense that the projected costs of a project are
quantified. (based on previous actuals) There is NO way of knowing what
effect a decline in Marketing morale will have on sales, because you cannot
quantify the extent of the decline. Different people will react differently
to the same stimulus.

For me, pulling a number out of the air is a less useful approach than
ignoring the factor completely.

I would not kid myself that I had quantified an intangible, based on my "gut
feel" and then use this quantification as a basis for making Business
decisions. If you find that OK, fine; I won't do it.

And I have no confusion whatsoever regarding Revenues and Budgets or
Projected and Actuals... use these things every day. BUT they are NOT based
on intangibles. What we did last year is very tangible...

So we have to agree to disagree on this.

Cheers,

Pete.

> and annual profits, etc.

>
> If a business anticipates a change in the environment in which they
…[13 more quoted lines elided]…
>
That's where we disagree. There is no need to make the attempt to quantify
if the thing being quantified is intangible. Doing so, in my opinion can
cause more trouble than benefit. My original argument was not that things
should or should not be quantified; it was that intangibles can  (and, very
often, should) be considered when deciding to proceed or not. That can be
done without quantifying. There may be occasions where proceeding is the
right thing (morally) to do. There may be other cases where it isn't. How do
you quantify a moral or ethical dilemma?
I believe there ARE intangibles. I further believe that 3 different people
will give three different numbers when asked to quantify them.
Who is right? Which "estimate" do we go with? In my case, none of them. We
simply consider that there is an intangible that should be considered before
we decide to go or not.

> >3. The time factor. (This enhancement will enable us to do various

And you did so quite correctly based on historical data, not intangibles. In
the case where I did this, no such data was available, but one of the
arguments for proceeding was that if we didn't do it there would certainly
be zero benefit.

> >4. Staff well-being.  (this is akin to the Marketing Morale mentioned
above
> >but not exactly the same). How do we quantify changes that are intended
to
> >make the environment more pleasant (healthy?), make working conditions
less
> >onerous, establish a "culture" within the organisation? Say we move from
a
> >tight hierarchic Management structure to a much more open networked
> >"no-blame" culture. How much is this worth? Who knows...can't quantify.
But
> >it is likely to affect our bottom line. How Much? Who knows...can't
> >quantify.)
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
>
How can you estimate the above? A culture change has to be proceeded with
because there is belief that the culture needs change, people realise that
what they are doing now could be done better. It does not require numbers.
You might do it just because people will feel better. You might suspect that
will cut staff turnover but you cannot make a realistic estimate of by what
amount. If you do, you are kidding yourself and that's the part I consider
dangerous. It would be more sensible to assume that this benefit will not
eventuate (don't make it part of the equation) and then see it as a bonus
if/when it eventuates. And you are wrong to say that Senior Managers will
never agree the change. From my own experience I have seen that the more
enlightened ones WILL do it without quantifiable benefit, because they are
persuaded there will be benefit, even if it is not quantified or
quantifiable.

> Quantification doesn't mean that the estimated numbers must precisely
> reflect the outcome of the decision.
…[21 more quoted lines elided]…

And none of these deal with intangibles.

there

of
> >quantifiable factors. (Although you CAN do this and it is certainly
better
> >than not looking at ANY factors and going entirely by gut feel...)

…[10 more quoted lines elided]…
>
And they are tangible... If they are unripe your "gut feel" could well be
painful <G>... but it is most definitely tangible...

There was a young Swami from Deal
Who said: "Pain isn't real..."
But when I sit on a pin
And it punctures my skin
I dislike what I fancy I feel...

BOTTOM LINE: I oppose the "quantification" of intangibles as being
misleading and dangerous. But, I believe they should be considered when
making decisions that affect people. Something along the lines of: "We can't
quantify this, but we have reason to believe it will affect the process we
are considering. We should attempt to deal with this effect in whatever
solution we come up with." (Going back to the Marketing morale thing, we
might decide to offer a bonus or a boondoggle to the Marketing people to
offset the expected decline in morale... we have considered the effect,
without requiring it to be quantified.)
```
