```
"Peter E. C. Dashwood" <dashwood@nospam.enternet.co.nz> wrote:

>Bob,
>
…[9 more quoted lines elided]…
>to the same stimulus.

Peter, I've never said that that the outcome will be known.  I'm
saying that the outcome will be predicted and the prediction will be
based upon the best data available....both historical data (if it
happened before) as well as analysis from the most powerful computers
in the world....human brains with years of experience in the specific
industry.

The business will then quantify an estimate of the anticipated decline
in sales.  Regardless of how the estimated number was determined...it
nevertheless represents a quantification of next year's forecasted
sales.

It appears to me that you believe that quantification can only occur
if the outcome can be predicted with absolute certainty.  I'm
suggesting that quantification doesn't infer absolute accuracy.
Estimates are quantifications.

If businesspersons only made decisions on numbers which they
absolutely KNOW will occur, then no business decisions would ever be
made.

>For me, pulling a number out of the air is a less useful approach than
>ignoring the factor completely.

But basing a best estimate on historical data, surveys and various
forms of research isn't just "pulling a number out of the air."  

So if adverse weather causes a decline in coffee bean yield in
Columbia, coffee companies should just ignore the fact that there will
likely be an increase in the price of coffee beans?  There is
absolutely no way to precisely predict what the actual price of beans
will be.  They can certainly estimate what the likely price will be.

It sounds to me that you are suggesting that if a business cannot
predict with absolute certainty, then they should ignore the factor.

>I would not kid myself that I had quantified an intangible, based on my "gut
>feel" and then use this quantification as a basis for making Business
>decisions. If you find that OK, fine; I won't do it.

Weather forecasts (as in my example above) are intangible.  Because
weather patterns can change on a day-by-day basis and even an
hour-by-hour basis, it is impossible to achieve 100% precision before
the fact,  Coffee bean yield forecasts are intangible.  Weather, bugs
and other factors affect the actual outcome.

Businesses decisions are not always based upon hard facts and outcomes
which are predictable to 100% accuracy.  That's why most businesses
employ the use of statistical data in order to attempt to predict
outcomes.

>And I have no confusion whatsoever regarding Revenues and Budgets or
>Projected and Actuals... use these things every day. BUT they are NOT based
>on intangibles. What we did last year is very tangible...

Of course what you did last year is absolutely tangible......but what
you budget for next year will almost ALWAYS include a variety of
intangible factors which are impossible to predict with absolute
certainty.

My son is a University Physics Major....Dean's list, straight A's.  I
have similar discussions with him and he harbors the same position as
you.  He, too, is stalwart in his belief that decisions should always
be made with absolute certainty of the outcome.  That's the scientist
in him.

Decisions in science can generally be made on outcomes which can be
predicted with absolute certainty.  At a specific altitude and
barometric pressure, it is possible to predict the precise temperature
at which water will boil.  Given a quantity of pure carbon, it is
possible to predict with 100% certainty the specific gravity of the
element.

Because of the massive number of variables, both known and unknown in
most business decisions, rarely are they ever made based upon factors
which can be predicted with 100% certainty......yet the decisions are
based on estimated numbers.....estimated
quantities.......quantifications.



Bob Wolfe

Bob,

I'm really sorry that you missed the whole point of my argument and decided
that I can only accept 100% accuracy. (That is certainly not the case).

But it isn't so important I intend to debate it further.

If you clear your preconceptions and re-read what I wrote, you will realise
the difference between guesstimates based on tangibles and guesstimates
based on intangibles.

Both are ESTIMATES but one has more validity than the other.

I feel strongly enough about this that I WON'T use intangibles as a basis
for estimating, when I am considering Business cases. (But, like I said
before, I have no problem if you do...).

It sounds to me like your son has grasped this better than you have <G> (but
then, I would say that...<G>)

Certainly, it does my heart good to know that you have this kind of
conversation with your offspring. So many parents these days don't or
can't... You are both lucky men.

_(thread depth: 15)_

- **From:** "Howard Brazee" <howard.brazee@cusys.edu>
- **Date:** 2002-10-17T15:39:27+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<aomlg3$fk0$1@peabody.colorado.edu>`
- **References:** `<3da0b52b$0$9160$5a62ac22@freenews.iinet.net.au> <anspl807ol@enews1.newsguy.com> <EnWdnVPoBNoJ9z6gXTWc3g@News.GigaNews.Com> <b3638c46.0210090330.6d305012@posting.google.com> <ao18u6$c2d$1@panix1.panix.com> <3da5762e.355391@news.epix.net> <3da604f8_8@Usenet.com> <3da6c0b5.526306@news.epix.net> <3da81440_4@Usenet.com> <3daae984.10247795@news.epix.net> <3dac4ef6_5@Usenet.com> <3dad6125.309495@news.epix.net>`

```
One big problem with estimates is that too many people don't know the precision
of the basic assumptions behind the figures.  Maybe they need to practice simple
slide rule practice.

It's like getting precision with the Drake equations - assuming that intelligent
species will use broadband radio and keep using it.  After making a bunch of
WAGs, we can plug in precise numbers and get lots of precision in an answer that
is still a WAG.

The people making the decisions need very good understanding on what is being
assumed.  And they need to consider strategies for what happens when the
assumptions are wrong.   A 20% chance of your CEO telling you to go with his
choice of vendors may be more important than determining whether your best
vendor will get your job in 360 or 365 days.

Precision, WAGs, and significant figures are not well understood by people who
need to make decisions.
```

Impossible to determine as it requires subjective judgement. (A change that

My fervent belief is that change is good and intrinsically it is NOT bad
(mainly for the reasons I outlined in my response to Jerry...no progress
without change...no growth without change...the alternative (stagnation)
would be anathema to me...etc. BUT, all of this is entirely arguable and

that leave me <G>. Are only "good" changes intrinsically good <G>?

It's a bit like the Church of Randanity: "Good things are Good; Bad things
are Bad..." <G>
(http://ourworld.compuserve.com/homepages/pete_dashwood/randity.htm)

In article <01c26fe9$cae71480$5295f243@chottel>, Charles Hottel
<chottel@cpcug.org> writes
>The universe is change.  Eveything is a process.  A chair may seem static
>and unchanging but at the atomic and quantum levels there is contiuous
>change. You cannot avoid it.
>
Q: If you stand in front of a river for one hour, is the river you are
standing in front of at the end of one hour the same river that you
stood in front of at the start of the hour? (This is a big question in
philosophy).

A1: No, the water contained within the river has changed and therefore
it is not the same river.

A2: Yes, the water has little bearing on the matter as the banks and the
contents are largely unchanged.

If the river is the same (and that appears to be the consensus of the
philosophers) then the Universe must be the same, too, regardless of the
changes to structure and content. Therefore:

     plus ca change, plus la meme chose.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Philosophical rivers. was Re: Future of COBOL

_(thread depth: 10)_

- **From:** "Peter E. C. Dashwood" <dashwood@nospam.enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2002-10-11T00:34:33+01:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<3da60c18_6@Usenet.com>`
- **References:** `<3da0b52b$0$9160$5a62ac22@freenews.iinet.net.au> <gg2o9.8633$cG1.57786599@news-text.cableinet.net> <217e491a.0210062150.7806d1af@posting.google.com> <3DA1845D.7070204@nycap.rr.com> <anspl807ol@enews1.newsguy.com> <EnWdnVPoBNoJ9z6gXTWc3g@News.GigaNews.Com> <ao1qp802boi@enews4.newsguy.com> <3da4b1da_3@Usenet.com> <01c26fe9$cae71480$5295f243@chottel> <w7KZtTA6PWp9EwBK@ld50macca.demon.co.uk>`

```

Alistair Maclean <alistair@ld50macca.demon.co.uk> wrote in message
news:w7KZtTA6PWp9EwBK@ld50macca.demon.co.uk...
> In article <01c26fe9$cae71480$5295f243@chottel>, Charles Hottel
> <chottel@cpcug.org> writes
…[8 more quoted lines elided]…
>
Oh, I really like this one, Alistair!

So here I am on the Banks of the Rhein as it flows through Dusseldorf, say,
and one hour later it has suddenly become the Mosel...?! That would only be
true if I took the hour to travel to Koblenz which is at the junction of the
Rhein and Mosel.

I don't think so. I  can stand there as long as I like and it is still the
Rhein I am looking at. The philosophers are unconcerned with the label we
apply to the river, or even with our definitions of what constitutes a
"river"; the question is much deeper...<sorry... <G>>.

I perceive the river. An hour later I still perceive the river. Given that I
have not relocated myself spatially, and only time has elapsed, it must be
the same river. (We must assume that my perceptions are intact and no-one is
out to hoodwink me...)

Now supposing I close my eyes and stand there for 300,000 years. The river
changes its course or eventually dries up. Ice ages come and go and
eventually I open my eyes and find myself looking at a river. It is in the
same place and it looks the same, but it is NOT the same river, irrespective
of what label I put on it.

In computer terms I am looking at a difference instance of the river object.
It is NOT the same instance that was there 300,000 years ago.

The point is that the river can change intrinsically (water molecules can
change their state and location in time and space) but it is still "the
river". The intrinsic changes are properties of this thing called a "river".

> A1: No, the water contained within the river has changed and therefore
> it is not the same river.
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
>

From the above I find myself in agreement with the philosophers on this.  I
like Answer 2 better than Answer 1, though neither of them completely
satisfies me...<G>

> If the river is the same (and that appears to be the consensus of the
> philosophers) then the Universe must be the same, too, regardless of the
> changes to structure and content.

Well, that's really pushing the envelope <G>! However, I can see no logical
objection to such a conclusion.

The State of the Universe is much more difficult to ascertain than the state
of the river. If you subscribe to the Big Bang theory of Cosmology (I do...)
then it is obvious that, regardless of the changes which may occur in the
Universe, it can only remain intrinsically what it is...it came from nothing
and it IS nothing.

Yet the keyboard I'm typing this on seems pretty real and solid...the
explanation for this is that it is composed of just one type of
differentiated energy, the kind that we refer to as "matter", (but which is
really just another aspect of energy).

There is no immediately obvious anti-matter in my keyboard; if there was I'd
have no fingers <G>.

If we took all the different kinds of energy in the Universe and combined
them (under the right circumstances), everything would disappear back to
nothing, as all the various particles and photons cancelled each other out.
On this (greatly over-simplified) basis, I would have to agree that the
changes within the Universe do not affect the instance of the Universe
Object so I guess I can go with the conclusion.

It was a stimulating walk from the Rhein to Relativity...Thanks <G>!

Pete.



 Posted Via Usenet.com Premium Usenet Newsgroup Services
----------------------------------------------------------
    ** SPEED ** RETENTION ** COMPLETION ** ANONYMITY **
----------------------------------------------------------        
                http://www.usenet.com
```

SkippyPB <swiegand@NOSPAM.neo.rr.com> wrote in article
<6899BC09B3568761.A4E5317FEDC93FF7.25077DE1A62203E5@lp.airnews.net>...

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Future of COBOL

_(thread depth: 5)_

- **From:** riplin@Azonic.co.nz (Richard)
- **Date:** 2002-10-09T16:02:44-07:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<217e491a.0210091502.4bdac839@posting.google.com>`
- **References:** `<3da0b52b$0$9160$5a62ac22@freenews.iinet.net.au> <gg2o9.8633$cG1.57786599@news-text.cableinet.net> <217e491a.0210062150.7806d1af@posting.google.com> <3DA1845D.7070204@nycap.rr.com> <anspl807ol@enews1.newsguy.com>`

```
"Grinder" <grinder@no.spam.maam.com> wrote 

> XML -- universal data format standard.  This is a really good
> thing, as there's never been one before.  If it pans out,
> though, is yet to be seen.

I suspect that you are over-optimistic about XML.  XML is just a
tidied up form of SGML, which has been around since the 80s.  The
problem with XML is that it is only a framework within which
'standard' data formats may be defined, it does nothing in itself to
be a 'good thing'.

It is no better than EDIFACT as a 'standard data format', except that
XML can be visually understood somewhat better.  For XML to be useful
it needs an 'XML application' to be defined in which the tag names and
aattributes and the structure is enforced.  This is much the same
situation as EDIFACT format.

> .NET is not a language, it's a runtime system.  Compilers have
> been written for existing languages: VB, C++, Java, Delphi's
> object pascal, and other as well, such that those existing
> syntaxes may be used with the runtime.

Existing syntaxes may only be used as long as they map to C#.  That
is: the .NET languages are just C# with another syntax and not an
implementation of that language for the CLR.  See NetCobol for .NET,
it is not full Cobol.
```

```

"Richard" <riplin@Azonic.co.nz> wrote in message
news:217e491a.0210091502.4bdac839@posting.google.com...
> "Grinder" <grinder@no.spam.maam.com> wrote
>
> > XML -- universal data format standard.  This is a really
good
> > thing, as there's never been one before.  If it pans out,
> > though, is yet to be seen.
>
> I suspect that you are over-optimistic about XML.  XML is
just a
> tidied up form of SGML, which has been around since the 80s.
The
> problem with XML is that it is only a framework within which
> 'standard' data formats may be defined, it does nothing in
itself to
> be a 'good thing'.

I hear what you're saying -- but don't feel that I'm overrating
XML.  (Thusfar my praise for it has been slight.)

> It is no better than EDIFACT as a 'standard data format',
except that
> XML can be visually understood somewhat better.  For XML to
be useful
> it needs an 'XML application' to be defined in which the tag
names and
> aattributes and the structure is enforced.  This is much the
same
> situation as EDIFACT format.

The addition of standards such as xpath, xsl, and xml-schema
makes for a great way to characterize (and manipulate) your
data.  I've got no illusions about anyone defining good
universal schemas, but if they at least write their schemas
down in a common language, they'll be able to communicate
better.

It is hard to see EDIFACT going away because of XML.  XML is so
fluffy and EDIs are typically designed to be economical.

> > .NET is not a language, it's a runtime system.  Compilers
have
> > been written for existing languages: VB, C++, Java,
Delphi's
> > object pascal, and other as well, such that those existing
> > syntaxes may be used with the runtime.
>
> Existing syntaxes may only be used as long as they map to C#.
That
> is: the .NET languages are just C# with another syntax and
not an
> implementation of that language for the CLR.

That's not quite, but essentially true.  (There has been a lot
of noise about VB's "normalization" to .net -- unofficially
referred to as B# instead of vb.net.)

All .net compilers must map their languages to an intermediate
language, MSIL.  If you want your code to be interchangeable
with modules in other languages, you additionally have to be
CLR compliant.  Oddly, there are some C# features that aren't
CLR-compliant.  C++.net, in particular, retains a large set of
non-compliant features.

This approach works for a number of languages (descendants or
analogs of Fortran) without wiping out their "style" or
capabilities.  This has probably been made easier by the
relatively uniform additions of classes to the procedural forms
of those languages.

> See NetCobol for .NET, it is not full Cobol.

I did take a look at this, though not with any great depth or
authority.  I don't doubt it, but I'm curious -- what's
missing?
```

- **From:** "William M. Klein" <wmklein@nospam.ix.netcom.com>
- **Date:** 2002-10-09T21:30:29-05:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<ao2oo8$hb1$1@slb2.atl.mindspring.net>`
- **References:** `<3da0b52b$0$9160$5a62ac22@freenews.iinet.net.au> <gg2o9.8633$cG1.57786599@news-text.cableinet.net> <217e491a.0210062150.7806d1af@posting.google.com> <3DA1845D.7070204@nycap.rr.com> <anspl807ol@enews1.newsguy.com> <217e491a.0210091502.4bdac839@posting.google.com> <ao2jhk02vpk@enews3.newsguy.com>`

```
"Grinder" <grinder@no.spam.maam.com> wrote in message
news:ao2jhk02vpk@enews3.newsguy.com...
>
> "Richard" <riplin@Azonic.co.nz> wrote in message
> news:217e491a.0210091502.4bdac839@posting.google.com...
> > "Grinder" <grinder@no.spam.maam.com> wrote

>
> > See NetCobol for .NET, it is not full Cobol.
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
>

I don't know the CLR or MSIL (?) structure well enough to know how much of
this is "intrinsic" to any future COBOL for .NET, but I do know that the
current Fujitsu product is missing LOTS of funcitonality from the ANSI'85
Standard *and* from comparison with other workstation COBOL's.  The
following is the (I think incomplete) list from the Fujitsu documentation
that I have:


a.. Print files (ASSIGN TO PRINTER) are not supported. If you want to use
print files we recommend that you compile your print programs using Fujtisu
COBOL V6 and call this unmanaged code from your NetCOBOL for .NET managed
code. See Interacting with Other Languages .
a.. The SCREEN SECTION is not supported. Your NetCOBOL for .NET applications
should use WinForms or WebForms for interfacing with your users.
a.. SORT/MERGE is not supported. As with print files compile your SORT/MERGE
programs with the Windows COBOL compiler (e.g. Fujitsu COBOL V6) and call
the unmanaged code from your NetCOBOL for .NET managed code. See Interacting
with Other Languages.
a.. The COBOL REPORT WRITER module is not supported.
a.. Nested programs are not supported.
a.. Pointer data items (USAGE POINTER) are not supported.
a.. TYPEDEF STRONG is not supported.
a.. ENTRY statements are not supported. Code that relies on ENTRY points
should be restructured so that each separate ENTRY point becomes a method.
a.. ALTER statements are not supported. ALTER was declared obsolete in the
1985 COBOL standard.
a.. A GO TO statement without a procedure name (used as the target of an
ALTER statement) is not supported.
a.. The "STOP literal" statement, which would display a message to an
operator and pause execution pending a response, is not supported.
a.. Exception handling in .NET has some differences to standard OO COBOL
exception handling. .Net exceptions continue to be propagated up the call
chain until a matching exception handler is found. In standard OO COBOL the
handler needs to be defined in the source unit in which the exception is
raised.
a.. NetCOBOL for .NET only supports the COBOL file system. It does not
support other file systems such as the external file system, or Btrieve.
a.. You cannot build assemblies within the Visual Studio .NET IDE. This is a
VS .NET limitation not a COBOL limitation.
```

_(thread depth: 7)_

- **From:** ___computerNOSPAMconsultant@mindspring.com___ (nospam)
- **Date:** 2002-10-10T07:26:30+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<3da51e9f.386979947@nntp.mindspring.com>`
- **References:** `<3da0b52b$0$9160$5a62ac22@freenews.iinet.net.au> <gg2o9.8633$cG1.57786599@news-text.cableinet.net> <217e491a.0210062150.7806d1af@posting.google.com> <3DA1845D.7070204@nycap.rr.com> <anspl807ol@enews1.newsguy.com> <217e491a.0210091502.4bdac839@posting.google.com> <ao2jhk02vpk@enews3.newsguy.com>`

```
In responce to the post:
 On Wed, 9 Oct 2002 20:01:38 -0500, "Grinder"
<grinder@no.spam.maam.com> stated...and I replied:
>
>"Richard" <riplin@Azonic.co.nz> wrote in message
…[10 more quoted lines elided]…
>> tidied up form of SGML, which has been around since the 80s.
snip
>> It is no better than EDIFACT as a 'standard data format',
>except that
…[6 more quoted lines elided]…
>> situation as EDIFACT format.

I'm not quite sure either of you know what you're talking about.

From what I know of these things: first, XML is not a "tidied up
version of SGML"  it is a subset of SGML (just like HTML is a subset
of SGML).  XML is that portion of SGML that allows you to define a
data structure...any data structure you want, in any format, for any
purpose (that makes it completely UNstructured until someone defines
the data in an XML schema).  This makes it nothing near a
"standard"...no more than the last database schema I wrote was a
"standard".

EDIFACT is the European version of EDI, which are both completely
defined data formats and allow for NO variations to format, structure,
or purpose outside of that for which it (the specific EDI form) was
defined.  Each EDI/EDIFACT form is specifically designed for the
data/use (even to the point of having EDI forms defined specifically
for say, railroad shipping or automotive sales).  To even imply that
XML is much the same as EDIFACT would be tantamount to saying that the
alphabet and the dictionary are the same, just because they use the
same letters, without regard to the order of the letters or structure
of the language.

As far as I'm concerned, XML is nothing more than another way to
"define" data.  Definately not a "standard", but well worth using for
new types of network transactions.

EDI/EDIFACT forms are a standard.  They are specific, predefined data
formats that are quite old.  And well worth using for atypical
electronic business transactions (like invoices/money
transfers/product catalogs...).

Let's not blow ANYTHING out of proportions, except maybe paychecks!!

-
One of many Computer Consultants in the USA
http://drshell.home.mindspring.com/
30 years in the computer field, 20 with Unisys.
-
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Future of COBOL

_(thread depth: 8)_

- **From:** riplin@Azonic.co.nz (Richard)
- **Date:** 2002-10-10T11:53:17-07:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<217e491a.0210101053.1b090d9@posting.google.com>`
- **References:** `<3da0b52b$0$9160$5a62ac22@freenews.iinet.net.au> <gg2o9.8633$cG1.57786599@news-text.cableinet.net> <217e491a.0210062150.7806d1af@posting.google.com> <3DA1845D.7070204@nycap.rr.com> <anspl807ol@enews1.newsguy.com> <217e491a.0210091502.4bdac839@posting.google.com> <ao2jhk02vpk@enews3.newsguy.com> <3da51e9f.386979947@nntp.mindspring.com>`

```
___computerNOSPAMconsultant@mindspring.com___ (nospam) wrote 

> I'm not quite sure either of you know what you're talking about.

That's OK, because I don't think you know what I'm talking about
either.

> From what I know of these things: first, XML is not a "tidied up
> version of SGML"  

I actually said 'form' of SGML, not 'version'.

I don't mind being criticised for what I _said_, but I do object to
being criticised for someone's imagination.

> it is a subset of SGML (just like HTML is a subset
> of SGML).  XML is that portion of SGML that allows you to define a
> data structure...

These are 'subsets of SGML' only in so far as SGML extends to become a
superset of them.  It is not like XML was previously entirely
contained within SGML and only needed trimming down a bit.

> any data structure you want, in any format, for any
> purpose (that makes it completely UNstructured until someone defines
> the data in an XML schema).  This makes it nothing near a
> "standard"...no more than the last database schema I wrote was a
> "standard".

XML has a defined standard, just as Cobol language has a defined
standard.  To be useful Cobol has to be written into a program.  For
XML to be useful it has to be written into an 'application'.  You are
just re-iterating what I said:

>> For XML to be useful it needs an 'XML application' to be defined 
>> in which the tag names and aattributes and the structure is
enforced.

In fact you may well have used a 'standard language' to write your
database schema.

> EDIFACT is the European version of EDI, which are both completely
> defined data formats and allow for NO variations to format, structure,
> or purpose outside of that for which it (the specific EDI form) was
> defined. 

EDIFACT has a definition of the way that the data is presented, the
separation of the fields and values, the collection of these into
segments and the grouping into messages including how each message is
controlled.  This is equivalent to how the XML standard defines how
XML messages are laid out.

> Each EDI/EDIFACT form is specifically designed for the
> data/use (even to the point of having EDI forms defined specifically
> for say, railroad shipping or automotive sales).  

EDIFACT _also_ defines specific message types with the segments and
the data
items they are to contain.  This is equivalent to XML applications.

> completely
> defined data formats and allow for NO variations to format, structure,
> or purpose outside of that for which it (the specific EDI form) was
> defined. 

There is, in fact, no prohibition at all in using the EDIFACT data
format for any new purpose whatsoever.  There is no restriction to
only using those that are predefined applications, nor is there any
requirement for "NO variation". These matters are agreed between the
partners who are interchanging data.

Now it may be that in specific cases a 'partner' may require that a
form be followed exactly.  In other cases a completely new form
following the EDIFACT standard for layout may be defined from scratch.
 I am aware of the process having been involved for some years.

> To even imply that
> XML is much the same as EDIFACT would be tantamount to saying that the
> alphabet and the dictionary are the same, just because they use the
> same letters, without regard to the order of the letters or structure
> of the language.

What I _actually_ said was "It is no better than EDIFACT as a
'standard data format'".  EDIFACT defines, in the first part, a method
of laying out data - a standard for formatting data.  XML defines a
method of formatting data.  Both of these require 'applications' to
define what data is used and where it goes.  In this respect XML is no
better than EDIFACT in solving data interchange problems.

There are applications defined in both EDIFACT and in XML which do
help solve specific data interchange problems.

> As far as I'm concerned, XML is nothing more than another way to
> "define" data.  Definately not a "standard", but well worth using for
> new types of network transactions.

What I said.

> EDI/EDIFACT forms are a standard.  They are specific, predefined data
> formats that are quite old.  And well worth using for atypical
> electronic business transactions (like invoices/money
> transfers/product catalogs...).

You are confusing EDIFACT forms (applications) with the EDIFACT data
format. This is much the same confusion that applies to XML.  Those
that promote XML as a 'standard' seem to imagine that the data format
is enough to ensure understanding (probably because they can read it
while EDIFACT is all :?+') when what is actually required is standard
applications.

For example some word processors are storing their documents in XML
'standard' format.  This does not mean that these can be used by other
programs directly in spite of the optimism.  It may, however, make it
easier to write a converter because, at least, the layout is
standardised.
```

```
Well, I'll try that interspersed format people seem to like. Edited comments below:

"nospam" <___computerNOSPAMconsultant@mindspring.com___> wrote in message news:3da60d9a.448159018@nntp.mindspring.com...
> In responce to the post:
>  On 10 Oct 2002 11:53:17 -0700, riplin@Azonic.co.nz (Richard)
…[7 more quoted lines elided]…
> Symantics again

Not so. Earlier in the thread, one of you referenced HTML as being a "form" of SGML as well.

HTML is a DTD (Document Type Description).  People think of it like a language in and of itself, but it is not. There are many DTD's
out there, and the job they perform is to describe the content structure of a document. A DTD is not a language, nor is it a "form"
of SGML, it is simply the set of rules and definitions of how a document is formed, ordered, what elements it may contain, and what
the possible attributes of those elements are.

The fancy graphics and such that people tend to associate with HTML actually have not a single thing to do with the HTML; they are
how the presentation software chooses to present the content of the HTML document. That may seem like a trivial difference, but if
you spend a little time thinking hard about it, you will realize that it is a really tremendous amount of difference.

XML, on the other hand, is an --> extension <-- of SGML, and actually includes new fucntionality. SCHEMA's replacing DTDs (which, by
the way, XML still supports) are the major innovation. They pretty much provide the reason for the 'eXtensionable' part of the XML
name.  Like SGML, XML has documents that each have a specific and well defined document type. Unlike SGML, that document type may be
defined in a DTD *or* in a Schema.


> >XML has a defined standard, just as Cobol language has a defined
> >standard.  To be useful Cobol has to be written into a program.  For
…[10 more quoted lines elided]…
>

No - a standard is a set of rules that are provided by an committe or other organization with the authority to set standards for a
particular subject matter.

In the sense that you refer to the organization and content of an XML document being 'standard' - there is no standard. The DTD or
Schema defines that.

In the sense that any XML document must be able to be parsed and validated against it's DTD, there are a good solid set of
standards. Again, XML is a superset of SGML and acts and behaves as such.

I edited out the rest of the "discussion" below, but let me point out the difference between traditional EDI solutions and XML.

XML, by definition, carries with itself the description of it's document. In a way, it is a self defining document.

That does not mean that it describes the meaning of each element in the document or the meaning of the content of an element; just
where the element may be, how many times it may appear, what the attributes for that element are, and other similar information.

The parties exchanging information don't need to agree on the structure of the document, since they can always determine the
structure of the document on the fly.

The parties exchanging information DO need to have a  pre-existing agreement on the meaning represented
by each element in the DTD or schema. In other words, they have to agree on a standard as to exactly what  a particular element or
attribute of an element means before they can exchange any meaningful communications. They simply do NOT have to have pre-existing
knowledge the structure of the document exchanged (though it would rare to find someone exchanging XML documents that does *not*)
since the document, by definition, carries that information along with it.

Hope that helps a bit guys.
```

"Version of" (which I did not say) implies form and functionality. 
"form of" (which I did say) only implies the format and not [all] the

XML is a "form of" SGML in that it retains the form but not [all] the
functionality.

Whether it is 'tidier' is subjective and a matter of opinion.

There is no requirement for 'NO variation' from the pre-defined forms.

> No, EDI definitions were defined by DISA...XML definitions exist

EDIFACT forms were written by individuals too, there is no 'higher
authority' demanding conformance, it is an issue between partners
agreeing.

If, for a particular group of partners, they have agreed that a
particular definition is to be followed exactly then this is to be
done.  If they agree to varying some published definition and
documenting it somewhat differently then they are free to do so.  As
long as they are only exchanging data between themselves then this is
not a problem at all.

> >> completely
> >> defined data formats and allow for NO variations to format, structure,
…[10 more quoted lines elided]…
> corner and NOT go to jail.  :/

The EDIFACT 'rules', just as the rules of a game, are agreed between
partners (or players). EDIFACT acts for its members (who are the
partners in exchanging data) in order to formalise and document what
is agreed.  If a change is required by the partners then they can do
this informally, but agreement between themselves, or through EDIFACT
with a new form or a new version.

> If it's a completely new form, it's not in the EDIFACT standard.

Again you are confused.

I quite carefully explain that there is a 'standard', a document that
lays out how EDIFACT data exchange should be done in terms of
arranging data in fields and segments and then say that a new form can
follow this standard.

But 'whoosh' it passes completely above your head and you blindly come
back with  "If it's a completely new form, it's not in the EDIFACT
standard.".

No, I will write this slowly for you: I t  _ u s e s _   t h e   E D I
F A C T   s t a n d a r d .

The EDIFACT data exchange standard defines the way that data can laid
out in fields and segments and these assembled into messages and
groups.

BAPLIE, for example, _uses_ the EDIFACT data exchange standard to
define how to write data about Container Ship loadings.  This, in
itself, is another standard and is defined by a working group of
members/partners within EDIFACT.

I could write a new 'form' that used the EDIFACT data exchange
standard format but dealt with completely novel data.  It could still
be defined as 'Standard EDIFACT format'.

You are still confused about form and function.

> I've
> been "involved" in data transfer since before EDI/EDIFACT. And,
> written full blown EDI applications (following the form structures)
> for the Navy.

Working for the Navy may explain why you have this difficulty of
understanding.  You seem to have been trained that all 'standards'
come from a higher authority, somewhere above 'god', and must be
followed exactly with no room for thinking.

> Now, do you want to compare penis size?

Why ? Is this your usual way of resolving arguments ?
```

Ref: Websters New International Dictionary: 1922
Based on the International Dictionary of 1890 and 1900
the latest authentic quarto edition of the Merriam series.

Stand'ard  n.  [Of estandart, F. e'tendard, of Teutonic origin]
1. Something elevated as a signal or beacon.
4. That which is set up and established by authority as a rule for the
measure of quantity, weight, extent, value, or quality;  esp., the
original specimen weight or measure sanctioned by Government as the
standard pound, gallon, yard, meter, or the like.
5. That which is established by authority, custom, or general consent,
as a model or example; criterion; test.

is?) from the Navy.  Being Teutonic (originating from a group of

very background you attempt to defile.  These people were mostly sea
faring folks (navy men).

standards, this place would be full of those (like yourself) who
believe they are of a high enough authority to establish standards.

EDI/EDIFACT, does not make it an EDI/EDIFACT standard.  This is
probably why we have so many variations of different programming
languages...arrogance.

within it in a standard way.

#### ↳ Re: [HELP] OCCURS

- **From:** riplin@Azonic.co.nz (Richard)
- **Date:** 2002-10-08T20:44:36-07:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<217e491a.0210081944.1c3d15e2@posting.google.com>`
- **References:** `<antkq2$feo$1@hfc.pacific.net.hk>`

```
"       j  " <hide@X.jp> wrote 

>   01  TABLE-STATE-RECORD OCCURS 50 TIMES
>          INDEXED BY STATE-INDEX.
…[8 more quoted lines elided]…
> So, how could I handle it?

An OCCURS clause is invalid on an 01 or 77 level.

      01  Table-State-Table.
        03  TABLE-STATE-RECORD        OCCURS 50 TIMES
                                      INDEXED BY STATE-INDEX.
         05  TABLE-STATE-CODE         PIC XX.
         05  TABLE-STATE-NAME         PIC X(20).

Some compilers allow OCCURS on an 01 (but not 77) as an extention, but
this can lead to anomolies.  For example if this was an FD what would
the 'record size' for the file be ? 22 ?

There may also be issues with 'slack bytes'.  A compiler may start
each 01 and 77 level on, say, an 8 byte boundry.  How much space would
your table take ?  1100 bytes ?  1200 bytes ?  1198 bytes ?

What if you had had (in the original) 

     01  Table-State-REDF REDEFINES Table-State-Record.

Are you redefining just one 01 level or the whole 1100/1200/1198 bytes
?
```

that has an OCCURS clause (for the same reason as below).

The rules of  "uniqueness of reference" really do pose serious problems when
this extension exists.

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: [HELP] OCCURS

- **From:** "Chuck Stevens" <charles.stevens@unisys.com>
- **Date:** 2002-10-09T14:29:35-07:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<ao273v$kbr$1@si05.rsvl.unisys.com>`
- **References:** `<antkq2$feo$1@hfc.pacific.net.hk> <217e491a.0210081944.1c3d15e2@posting.google.com> <ao1c5q$nl9$1@peabody.colorado.edu> <ao1mrl$4ol$1@slb7.atl.mindspring.net>`

```
A slight, and admittedly obscure, exception:  Unisys MCP/AS COBOL(68)
allowed OCCURS on 01-level items.  What it did on its target system was
produce a multidimensional table in which the first subscript addressed an
array of descriptors, each of which described memory.  Thus, only the table
elements associated with first subscripts that were actually used in the
program had memory allocated for them, and only when an element of the table
with that first subscript was "touched".   The mechanism thus made use of
the hardware's implementation of "true" two-dimensional arrays:  an array of
descriptors for the first dimension and memory spaces for the second (and
subsequent).

This feature was troublesome both in implementation and in execution, and
had some insoluble semantic anomalies when the user neglected to code a
second dimension.  The feature was not carried forward as a feature into
MCP/AS COBOL74 (or COBOL85, for that matter), and users were urged to use
"soft" multidimensional arrays -- i.e., "normal" COBOL-style ones -- rather
than attempt to use the array-of-descriptors approach.  Unisys MCP/AS
COBOL(68) is no longer officially supported but still has a few users by
special arrangement.

    -Chuck Stevens

"William M. Klein" <wmklein@nospam.ix.netcom.com> wrote in message
news:ao1mrl$4ol$1@slb7.atl.mindspring.net...

bytes

>
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: [HELP] OCCURS

- **From:** riplin@Azonic.co.nz (Richard)
- **Date:** 2002-10-09T11:48:31-07:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<217e491a.0210091048.7a66dc6@posting.google.com>`
- **References:** `<antkq2$feo$1@hfc.pacific.net.hk> <217e491a.0210081944.1c3d15e2@posting.google.com> <ao1c5q$nl9$1@peabody.colorado.edu>`

```
"Howard Brazee" <howard.brazee@cusys.edu> wrote 

> > Some compilers allow OCCURS on an 01 (but not 77) as an extention, but
> > this can lead to anomolies.  For example if this was an FD what would
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
> limitation elsewhere.

So, you would be happy with it being inconsistent ?

What if it was in linkage section ?

> > There may also be issues with 'slack bytes'.  A compiler may start
> > each 01 and 77 level on, say, an 8 byte boundry.  How much space would
> > your table take ?  1100 bytes ?  1200 bytes ?  1198 bytes ?
> 
> The compiler designer could pick one.

The Cobol _designer_ 'picked' one option: No OCCURS on level 01.  The
whole point of a standard is that the compiler writers should not need
to 'pick' anything.  Having OCCURS on 01 levels can lead to many
problems.  For example if it is used in a CALL USING then the called
routine would need to know how the compiler dealt with slack bytes.

> And we could still move the occurs down if we didn't like the default.

What is your problem with 'moving the occurs down' anyway ?  Is it
that you now have lots of code that would fail a complete check
because you didn't know that this rule existed ?

> Right now we don't have any choice.

Why do you want 'choice' in this issue.  01 OCCURS have never been in
standard Cobol and have no useful function.
 
> What if you had
>       05  Table-State-REDF-05 REDEFINES Table-State-Record-05.
> 
>     Are you redefining just one 05 level or the whole 1100/1200/1198 bytes  ???

What does the standard say about it ?

Actually, what it says is that you cannot redefine a data item
containing an occurs clause.  Redefining records (01 levels) should
always be valid.
 
> I fail to see your point.

Yes.
```

#### ↳ Re: [HELP] OCCURS

- **From:** jacksleight@hotmail.com (Jack Sleight)
- **Date:** 2002-10-08T23:00:05-07:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<8a2d426e.0210082200.71919bd4@posting.google.com>`
- **References:** `<antkq2$feo$1@hfc.pacific.net.hk>`

```
Hi,

Try:

01  STATE-TABLE.
    03  STATE-ENTRY OCCURS 50 TIMES
         INDEXED BY STATE-INDEX.
        05    TABLE-STATE-CODE        PIC XX.
        05    TABLE-STATE-NAME        PIC X(20).

Regards, Jack.

## Thread: VSAM File Structure — 8 msgs

_Topics: [`VSAM, files, sorting`](topics.md#files)_

### VSAM File Structure

- **From:** "Robert Lair" <rlair@asppages.com>
- **Date:** 2002-10-08T00:59:05-04:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<3da2656b$0$38605$9a6e19ea@news.newshosting.com>`

```
Hi All,

I am in need of accessing legacy COBOL applications from Microsoft .NET.
One of the things I am running into however, is accessing VSAM files.  I am
considering writing a VSAM data provider for .NET but I need to know more
about VSAM files to do this.  Can anyone tell me where I can get information
about the structure of a VSAM file?  Basically I will need to access the
file manually in the same way you would need to do so in C++.  Any
information you can provide me with would be greatly appreciated.

Thanks!
Bob
```

#### ↳ Re: VSAM File Structure

- **From:** "William M. Klein" <wmklein@nospam.ix.netcom.com>
- **Date:** 2002-10-08T00:29:59-05:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<antqgu$qq3$1@slb2.atl.mindspring.net>`
- **References:** `<3da2656b$0$38605$9a6e19ea@news.newshosting.com>`

```
If your .NET application runs on a PC (or Linux) the ONLY places that I know
of where .NET is currently implemented, then something is odd in your
request.  VSAM is an IBM *mainframe* implementation of an "indexed
sequential access method" (aka ISAM).  What are you REALLY trying to do?

 - Do "remote" file access from a .NET environment to mainframe situated
files?

 - Access files that were created in a COBOL (or other) application that was
PORTED from a mainframe environment to the PC (or Linux)?

 - Access COBOL "ISAM" files on the PC (or Linux) created by some other
COBOL compiler?

Any (all) of these CAN be done (with existing software) - but we need to
know exactly WHAT you want to do - before helping more.

P.S. There was (well maybe still is) a PC version of something
"sort-of-like" VSAM - part of the "SmartData Utilities" products.  I may be
mistaken on this, but I don't know of anyone actually USING it (or using it
much).  If you want more information on it, you might want to start at:
  http://publibfp.boulder.ibm.com/epubs/pdf/dd0wvmas.pdf

or any of the other "SMARTData" books at:
 http://www-3.ibm.com/software/ad/cobol/va/library/
```

##### ↳ ↳ Re: VSAM File Structure

- **From:** "Robert Lair" <rlair@asppages.com>

- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<3da273c1$0$38579$9a6e19ea@news.newshosting.com>`
- **References:** `<3da2656b$0$38605$9a6e19ea@news.newshosting.com> <antqgu$qq3$1@slb2.atl.mindspring.net>`

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: VSAM File Structure

- **From:** "William M. Klein" <wmklein@nospam.ix.netcom.com>
- **Date:** 2002-10-08T01:18:42-05:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<anttca$4l$1@slb7.atl.mindspring.net>`
- **References:** `<3da2656b$0$38605$9a6e19ea@news.newshosting.com> <antqgu$qq3$1@slb2.atl.mindspring.net> <3da273c1$0$38579$9a6e19ea@news.newshosting.com>`

```
If you have an existing COBOL CICS application that has been ported to the
PC - is it STILL a "COBOL CICS" application on the PC?  (This is possible
with a VARIETY of COBOL products and a few CICS products).  If so, that
should give us (me) a clue as to what the VSAM files "are".

Some PC COBOL CICS applications use C-ISAM, some use IBM-proprietary ISAM
files, some use Micro Focus or Fujitsu proprietary ISAM files.

Again, if the application that is CREATING this files runs on the PC, you do
*not* have "VSAM" files.  If you are trying to create a program on the PC to
*replace* a mainframe COBOL CICS program, that can be done too.

Will the PC application still be in COBOL?  If so, use a "native" PC COBOL
compiler and you will "auto-magically" get a VSAM compatible file system
(but not the same from compiler vendor to vendor).

If you are just trying to create a NEW (replacement) application that will
access "indexed files" - but not from COBOL, then I would suggest that you
look at C-ISAM or BTRIEVE, both of which have "full" VSAM compatible
support.  (I don't know if either C-ISAM or BTRIEVE have announced ".NET"
versions - but I would check with those vendors.)

Mainframe COBOL CICS application programs do *not* get into the "structure"
of VSAM, therefore, I strongly recommend that you don't either.

  ***

If you really want to get  into "VSAM" - a couple of online books you might
want to look at are:
 "z/OS V1R3.0 DFSMS: Using Data Sets"
at

http://publibz.boulder.ibm.com/cgi-bin/bookmgr_OS390/BOOKS/DGT2D410/CCONTENT
S

    or

 "/OS V1R3.0 DFSMS Macro Instructions for Data Sets"
at

http://publibz.boulder.ibm.com/cgi-bin/bookmgr_OS390/BOOKS/dgt2d510/CONTENTS

    or
 "z/OS V1R3.0 DFSMSdfp Diagnosis Reference"
at

http://publibz.boulder.ibm.com/cgi-bin/bookmgr_OS390/BOOKS/dgt2r110/CONTENTS


    ***

If you don't currently know IBM mainframe access methods (I can't tell from
your note if you are coming from a PC or Mainframe background), you could
start out with:

 "z/OS V1R3.0 DFSMS Introduction"
at

http://publibz.boulder.ibm.com/cgi-bin/bookmgr_OS390/BOOKS/DGT2DI10/CCONTENT
S

has something called Distributed File Management.

## Thread: C Datatype BYTE in COBOL — 3 this month / 6 total

_Thread started [2002-08](2002-08.md#t-353ee6d77e)._

#### ↳ Re: C Datatype BYTE in COBOL

- **From:** "Robert M. Pritchett - RMP Consulting Partners LLC" <Sending-Email-To-This-Address-Constitutes-A-Promise-To-Send-One-Thousand-Dollars-To-RMPCP@rmpcp.com>

- **References:** `<3d64f29f_2@news5.nntpserver.com>`

```
*** post for FREE via your newsreader at post.newsfeed.com ***

Not directly, but you can manipulate it as half of a pic xx redefined as
a binay # e.g. pic s9999 comp and initialized to 0. But beware of the
byte order on the particular machine it's running on.
```

##### ↳ ↳ Re: C Datatype BYTE in COBOL

USAGE BINARY-CHAR

the standard implemented is if there is user demand.

As a user - make a demand.

- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol

```
    Some compilers do allow it.  Microfocus calls it PIC X COMP-X.

    You can also use various combinations of "9", so
long as you use COMP-X.  COMP-X implies unsigned.

    I think that it allows single byte COMP as well.

    Some compilers may have various flavors of "BINARY" as well.

    Multibyte items can also be created.  The exact picture really
depends on the exact compiler, etc.

<a id="t-4f24537094"></a>
## Thread: packed field handling - no sign — 3 this month / 12 total

_Topics: [`Language features and syntax`](topics.md#syntax)_

_Thread started [2002-08](2002-08.md#t-4f24537094)._

#### ↳ Re: packed field handling - no sign

- **From:** "Robert M. Pritchett - RMP Consulting Partners LLC" <Sending-Email-To-This-Address-Constitutes-A-Promise-To-Send-One-Thousand-Dollars-To-RMPCP@rmpcp.com>
- **Date:** 2002-10-08T02:43:51-05:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<3da28c34@post.newsfeed.com>`
- **References:** `<3d6c1e7c.0208270821.5802dbbe@posting.google.com>`

```
*** post for FREE via your newsreader at post.newsfeed.com ***

Redefine it as a 9(9) comp (binary) field, then multiply it by 16 to
shift it left 4 bits, then add 12 to or in the x'0c' sign nybble, then
redefine that as a big enough (5 byte) comp-3 field S9(9). If not exact,
this should be a good starting point.
```

##### ↳ ↳ Re: packed field handling - no sign

- **From:** Jeff Raben <jraben@cascinc.com>
- **Date:** 2002-10-08T11:47:41-04:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<n8v5qu0uabs4ajltn74hru4la4n47gd59h@4ax.com>`
- **References:** `<3d6c1e7c.0208270821.5802dbbe@posting.google.com> <3da28c34@post.newsfeed.com>`

```
Easier way:
 define a decimal field as s9(9) comp-3 and split it as follows.
01  bigfield pic s9(9) comp-3 value zero.
01  fff redefines bigfield.
    05 fourbytes pic xxxx.
    05 onebyte   pic x.
Move the unsigned packed field (that was defined PIC XXXX to FOURBYTES
and use BIGFIELD as a data source.   

QED.(quite easily done)

Jeff


"Robert M. Pritchett - RMP Consulting Partners LLC"
<Sending-Email-To-This-Address-Constitutes-A-Promise-To-Send-One-Thousand-Dollars-To-RMPCP@rmpcp.com>
wrote:

>*** post for FREE via your newsreader at post.newsfeed.com ***
>
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
>this should be a good starting point.

and stir with a Runcible spoon...
```

##### ↳ ↳ Re: packed field handling - no sign

- **From:** "WOB" <wobconsult@sprynet.com>
- **Date:** 2002-10-08T20:01:57-04:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<anvr26$4ph$1@slb3.atl.mindspring.net>`
- **References:** `<3d6c1e7c.0208270821.5802dbbe@posting.google.com> <3da28c34@post.newsfeed.com>`

```
The BINARY solution won't work. Here's an alternative:

03  WS-PACKED PIC  9(08)V9 PACKED-DECIMAL.
03  WS-PACKED-X REDEFINES WS-PACKED PIC  X(05).
03  WS-HEX-DATE PIC  X(04) VALUE X'20020517'.
03  WS-DISPLAY PIC  9(08).

MOVE WS-HEX-DATE TO WS-PACKED-X (1:4).
MOVE X'0F' TO WS-PACKED-X (5:1).
MOVE WS-PACKED TO WS-DISPLAY.

HTH....

Bill

"Robert M. Pritchett - RMP Consulting Partners LLC"
<Sending-Email-To-This-Address-Constitutes-A-Promise-To-Send-One-Thousand-Do
llars-To-RMPCP@rmpcp.com> wrote in message
news:3da28c34@post.newsfeed.com...
> *** post for FREE via your newsreader at post.newsfeed.com ***
>
…[38 more quoted lines elided]…
>
```

## Thread: OT - Humor (was: Is 12:00 Noon considered AM or PM? — 93 msgs

_Topics: [`Off-topic and spam`](topics.md#spam)_

### OT - Humor (was: Is 12:00 Noon considered AM or PM?

- **From:** "William M. Klein" <wmklein@nospam.ix.netcom.com>
- **Date:** 2002-10-09T14:10:58-05:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<ao1v07$lst$1@slb6.atl.mindspring.net>`
- **References:** `<CA0B96E96ED3D411ACEB0008C7918E10D30783@gmmcexcisc02.gmmc.ghs.org>`

```
I just had to share this note from the IBM-MAIN newsgroup.  (They are
discussing whether noon is A.M. or P.M.  and got a reference to the

- **From:** sanghabum@aol.comjunkless (Sanghabum)

```
Just to go serious for a few moments, there is a perfectly good international
standard (ISO 8601) for avoiding potential am/pm confusions. It also clears up
other such problems with dates.

Here's one reference, and a qiote from it.

http://www.cl.cam.ac.uk/~mgk25/iso-time.html

<<It is not clear, how 00:00, 12:00 and 24:00 are represented. Even
encyclopedias and style manuals contain contradicting descriptions and a common
quick fix seems to be to avoid "12:00 a.m./p.m." altogether and write "noon",
"midnight", or "12:01 a.m./p.m." instead, although the word "midnight" still
does not distinguish between 00:00 and 24:00 (midnight at the start or end of a
given day).>>

Colin.
```

- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol

- **References:** `<CA0B96E96ED3D411ACEB0008C7918E10D30783@gmmcexcisc02.gmmc.ghs.org> <ao1v07$lst$1@slb6.atl.mindspring.net> <3DA4825D.3070608@sympatico.ca> <3DA48DC9.4A3154BC@mb.sympatico.ca> <3da528da.389599654@nntp.mindspring.com> <ao4pms01028@enews3.newsguy.com> <eNsp9.16910$ue4.1011487@bgtnsc04-news.ops.worldnet.att.net> <3da81b83_4@Usenet.com>`

```
On Sat, 12 Oct 2002 14:03:10 +0100, "Peter E. C. Dashwood"
<dashwood@nospam.enternet.co.nz> wrote:

>The argument on whether 1 is prime or not is NOT circular. 1 is prime.

Says who?

1 is primus, the first number, but not a prime number

I agree that my thesis in mathematics is nearly 25 years old, and the
lectures I heard on this topic were even further in the past, and my
memory is not getting any better, but who has "decreed" that 1 is a
prime?  Of course one could "define" a prime in such a way that 1 is
"defined" as a prime, but that would break things, and thus makes such
a definition less sensible:

If 1 were a prime, one of the fundamental theorems would be flawed:
Any number > 1 can be uniquely (except of order) expressed as a
product of primes:  Like 24 = 2 * 2 * 2 * 3, and there is no other
representation.  If 1 were a prime, then 24 could be expressed as
1*2*2*2*3, or 1*1*1*2*2*2*3  or an infinite other number of products.
Not good.  Either we drop the fundamental theorem, or think about the
definition of "prime" again.  No, Sir, 1 is as much prime as PI is 3

The link is back, but it's a short comment:


Prime FAQs and Facts
A FAQ is a `Frequently Asked Question', and Mathematics is full
of them. Here are some of the most frequent FAQs about prime
numbers. Readers are invited to submit their FAQs (on primes,
or any other part of Mathematics), and we'll do our best to
answer them in future articles.

Is 1 a prime?

Like any good question, this can be answered at several levels.
The first simple answer is that the generally accepted
definition of a prime number is `a positive integer greater
than 1 which has no divisors other than 1 and itself.'

An equivalent definition is to specify that a prime number is a
positive integer which has exactly two divisors. Since 1 has
exactly one divisor, it is not prime.

A composite number is then defined to be a positive integer
with three or more divisors.

If, on the other hand, you define a prime number as a positive
integer which has no divisors other than 1 and itself, then 1
must be regarded as a prime number.

Which is the correct definition? Both have been used over the
years, and it really depends on how you want to develop the
discussion of prime numbers. Mathematicians are nowadays in
general agreement that it is more convenient to use a
definition which excludes 1 from the set of prime numbers.

The prime reason (if you will pardon the pun) is that one of
the first results in number theory is The Fundamental Theorem
of Arithmetic, which states:

Every positive integer greater than 1 is expressible as the
product of prime numbers, and, except for the order of the
factors in the product, such an expression is unique.

Thus 1001 = 7 x 11 x 13, and there is no other way of
expressing 1001 as a product of prime numbers, except by
changing the order of the factors. However, if you allow your
definition of prime to include the number 1, you could write
1001 in many different ways:

1001 = 1 x 7 x 11 x 13 = 1 x 1 x 7 x 11 x 13, etc.
So the Fundamental Theorem of Arithmetic would have to be
changed if your definition of prime included the number 1, to:
Every positive integer greater than 1 can be expressed as a
product of prime numbers greater than 1, and, except for the
order of factors in the product, such an expression is unique.

Thus, if you allow 1 to be a prime number, you must immediately
exclude it from your first major theorem about prime numbers.
That is enough justification for agreeing not to consider 1 to
be a prime. Note that it is not wrong, in any moral, legal or
mathematical respect, to regard 1 as a prime number. But if you
use your own personal definition for a concept which is not how
others view that concept, you are liable to be misunderstood.
```

> 01:00 PM

AM is ante meridian and PM is post meridian, so we should write 12:00M
for midday i.e. 12:00 meridian. However, at 12:00:01 we are now post
meridian.

Using the 24-hour clock avoids AM/PM confusion altogether.

a.m. = ante meridian = before the sun is directly overhead = before noon
p.m. = post meridian = after the sun is directly overhead = after noon

Noon is neither a.m. nor p.m.

Midnight is both 12 hours before noon (a.m.) AND 12 hours after noon (p.m.)

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ ZERO (was: OT - Humor (was: Is 12:00 Noon considered AM or PM?

_(thread depth: 8)_

- **From:** "William M. Klein" <wmklein@nospam.ix.netcom.com>
- **Date:** 2002-10-14T15:01:29-05:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<aof83p$qb$1@slb5.atl.mindspring.net>`
- **References:** `<4v3q9.117988$8o3.3536140@twister.austin.rr.com> <20021013161553.11578.00001134@mb-cp.aol.com>`

```
Actually the issue of "positive and negative zero" is a serious issue in
migrating from older to newer IBM COBOL's.  See:

  "Numeric data changes"
at
 http://publibz.boulder.ibm.com/cgi-bin/bookmgr_OS390/BOOKS/igy3mg10/4.1.7

which says in part,

"For example, Enterprise COBOL will not generate a negative zero result,
while OS/VS COBOL could."

NOTE:
  This has NOTHING to do with the "underlying" z/OS structure which still
supports both negative and positive zero.  It simply indicates what the
current COBOL compiler does.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Noon - AM or PM (was: OT - Humor (was: Is 12:00 Noon considered AM or PM?

_(thread depth: 10)_

- **From:** "William M. Klein" <wmklein@nospam.ix.netcom.com>
- **Date:** 2002-10-16T14:33:56-05:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<aokevm$8g1$1@slb0.atl.mindspring.net>`
- **References:** `<CA0B96E96ED3D411ACEB0008C7918E10D30783@gmmcexcisc02.gmmc.ghs.org> <ao1v07$lst$1@slb6.atl.mindspring.net> <fvacnVz0W_wHdDugXTWcqw@News.GigaNews.Com> <7xgd5KAc1vp9EwR7@ld50macca.demon.co.uk> <YPGcnfuChIZRGTqgXTWcqg@News.GigaNews.Com> <Dt21oIAG6Aq9Ewft@ld50macca.demon.co.uk> <Y8mcnS6v-J2YJTWgXTWcqA@News.GigaNews.Com> <jzp7pAA3vdq9Ewq5@ld50macca.demon.co.uk> <Wu-dnYhE8faDWTegXTWcpg@News.GigaNews.Com> <MeXgCHAPBwq9Ew8p@ld50macca.demon.co.uk> <217e491a.0210151957.7d7e934@posting.google.com>`

```
A couple of references for the USE (not the accuracy) of 12:00 P.M. being
noon:

http://www.faqs.org/faqs/astronomy/faq/part3/

"In strict English construction, 12:00 cannot be given either an A.M. = ante
meridiem or P.M. = post meridiem designation, but it has become common to
use 12 A.M. to mean midnight and 12 P.M. to mean noon.  In traditional
English, 12 M. = meridies means _noon_; nowadays one is just as likely to
see 12 M. = midnight and 12 N. = noon."

    ***

http://bartleby.org/61/80/P0388000.html

"USAGE NOTE:
  By definition, 12 a.m. denotes midnight, and 12 p.m. denotes noon, but
there is sufficient confusion over the meanings of a.m. and p.m. when the
hour is 12 to make it advisable to use 12 noon and 12 midnight where clarity
is required."

    ***

Having said that, I want to make it clear that I agree that the correct (not
usage) is to AVOID A.M. and P.M. for both noon and midnight.  This certainly
can be done via the 24-hour clock (the current ISO and many national
Standard way of doing this) or by avoiding "12:00" and using "12:01" or
similar "close" times).  Personally, I would also AVOID using "12:00m" which
TECHNICALLY is noon - but as indicated several places on the web MAY be
understood as "12:00 Midnight" as oppose to "12:00n" for noon).

If you are going to continue the argument on "commonly (mis-)understood"
phrasing, I would ask for *ANY* evidence of the "commonly accepted" use of
12:00 A.M. as noon.  As far as I can tell, that simply doesn't exist - while
the 12:00pm usage is (fairly) well documented.

    ***

Finally, I did NOT mean to start a "serious" thread on this issue.  If you
go to the beginning of the "humor" thread, it was simply talking about a
group with "rotational" in its official name.
```

```
I've been following this for a long time (too long?) and now want to add my
$.02. While I agree that the instant that the sun crosses the meridian is
referred to as noon, and is neither AM nor PM, many other things influence
the use of PM for noon. First, virtually nowhere does the sun cross overhead
at 12:00 (am, pm, m, n, noon...) due to time zones, etc. Second, while 12:00
may mean an instant with no measurable length, I believe that no one has
pointed out that it also often means the 60 seconds betwen 12:00:00.0000...
and 12:00:59.9999.... I hope that we can agree that MOST of this minute is
PM. Thus, I am willing to accept that noon is 12:00 PM. I would never accept
it to be 12:00 AM.

"William M. Klein" <wmklein@nospam.ix.netcom.com> wrote in message
news:aokevm$8g1$1@slb0.atl.mindspring.net...
> A couple of references for the USE (not the accuracy) of 12:00 P.M. being
> noon:
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> "In strict English construction, 12:00 cannot be given either an A.M. =
ante
> meridiem or P.M. = post meridiem designation, but it has become common to
> use 12 A.M. to mean midnight and 12 P.M. to mean noon.  In traditional
…[10 more quoted lines elided]…
> hour is 12 to make it advisable to use 12 noon and 12 midnight where
clarity
> is required."
>
>     ***
>
> Having said that, I want to make it clear that I agree that the correct
(not
> usage) is to AVOID A.M. and P.M. for both noon and midnight.  This
certainly

```
To continue the saga of 12.00 midight and noon, I agree that while
a.m. and p.m. are not strictly/officially applicable, they are often
used such that midight is 12.00 a.m. and noon is 12.00 p.m. 
Presumably, this is because all the times from 00.00 to 00.59 can also
be represented as 12.00 a.m. to 12.59 a.m. and similarly for 12.00
p.m. to 12.59 p.m.
```

```

On 19-Oct-2002, Alistair Maclean <alistair@ld50macca.demon.co.uk> wrote:

> Perhaps you should add the UK into that. How often have you walked a
> mile and can't remember how many barleycorns, inches, feet, yards, rods,
> poles, perches, chains and furlongs go into it?

There is only one time where I have cared.  I student taught physics one year,
and as an exercise (in learning to plug in units and multiply by one until you
had the correct units), I had them calculate the speed of light in furlongs per
fortnight.



Hmmm.   I can see an argument that says a more difficult conversion can make
people better at math.  Did Brits of my age mess up counting their change back
before they simplified their currency?   Not bloody likely.   They just had to
be better at doing arithmetic in their heads.   Not that anybody (hardly
anybody) wants to have this extra work - but did it really hurt them to have to
think?
```

- **From:** Volker Bandke <vbandke@bsp-gmbh.com>
- **Date:** 2002-10-19T18:52:42+02:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<4j23ru0vvv7jtsqjlthqts8u66e5v477e0@4ax.com>`
- **References:** `<MeXgCHAPBwq9Ew8p@ld50macca.demon.co.uk> <aopc56$88c$1@peabody.colorado.edu> <217e491a.0210181654.f3fbea6@posting.google.com> <ucp1ruc7li659tduc8l3nvaeuvhp9ephpl@4ax.com> <aormvb$i6h$1@panix1.panix.com> <73t2rukqp2dig3lim92iqma7gtpcheekv2@4ax.com>`

```
On Sat, 19 Oct 2002 08:13:40 -0700, Ralf A. Quint 
>
>My boss's son does in 10th grade math that would be 7th grade stuff in
>Germany, Austria or France, just as a small example...
>
>Ralf

Ralf,

PMFJI, but I have seen both sides of the coin.  I went to school in
Germany for 13 years, and then attended the senior class in a High
School (Loomis-Chaffee in Windsor, Ct, if anyone cares)

What was done in mathematics was about the same stuff I had in the
"Unterprima" (one year before graduation in Germany).  Well, the
pupils were also one year younger than I was - The US school system
takes roughly twelve years to get you int college as opposed to the 13
years we have in Germany.

When I told my teacher that is math course (calculus II) this was all
"old news"  (and he found out soon enough that that was true), he
placed me into a special group (of 2) students with our own stuff
(which, as I discovered later), covered the stuff od the first two
semester in Linear Algebra when I was studying mathematics later.

A similar thing happened in Physics (group of 5 students)  and
Chemistry (group of 4), and to keep me happy/busy I got access to the
school computer that were connected to Dartmouth college and had my
first exposure to Computing in general and BASIC in particular  (that
was 1971, BTW)

On other topics:  Geography was somewhat neglected, sports was
overemphasized, History lessons were very biased, leaning very much to
the US view of things. There were topics I had never heard before
(Humanities), others which were done on a purely voluntary basis
(music)

Saying the education was below par is not being fair.  Did you hear
about the PISA study on school education???

>overemphasized, History lessons were very biased, leaning very much to

is one of the main reasons why most US Americans (including
presidents) have such a biased view of /everything/ and then wonder
why the "rest of the world" tries to remind them about the
consequences of their (the US) actions...

>(Humanities), others which were done on a purely voluntary basis
>(music)

No, i didn't. All I mentioned is purely out of everyday personal

Ralf

```
Oh my - contrary to my normal mistake, which is a consequence of being a very bad typist, 
my use of the word was below is perfectly correct. 

The word were, when in the subjunctive mood is the only form of the verb to be in a adverb clause or a noun clause that makes a statement contrary to fact. However, that use of were occurs chiefly in formal writing; informal writing substitutes was.  While there may be some dispute over the exact mood I used the word in, subjunctive or conditional, there can be no dispute that I used the word correctly. Yes, I am aware some
people, mostly those with particular types of limited educational experiences, tend to object to any type of informal writing.

None the less, I consider this type of discussion informal. I produce formal writing only when paid to do so -- not from a lack of ability to do so.  I would suggest that the defense, instead of resting, should engage in a refresher course for writers. 

And "in the vernacular" - these folks are my buddies, and it pleases me to "talk" with them in a informal mode. Mostly because I like everyone here even though I have not physically met more than a half dozen of 'em. Besides, I am an engineer and I have a certain image to maintain! (So there!  :)

P.S. Note to those unable to receive HTML based e-mail. This is in HTML format because I italicized words in the top part of this message, rather than "improperly" enclosing them in quotes. <grin> 

-Paul

simply counting his fingers, as the vast majority still has all 10,
but very few only have 16 or 12.

"Ralf A. Quint" <ralf_quint@hottmail.com>

> And I personally can't understand why the US government is not
> enforcing the switch to metric/international standards much more. In
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
> but very few only have 16 or 12.

##That one's easy. The US government does require (virtually) all business
conducted with the government to be in metric. But the government does not
impose metrication on private transactions. Individuals in the US are -
mainly - free to fail or prosper by their own devices. There is no
government agency that, by force of law, mandates what words can appear in
the language - as there is in France, or bifurcated educational systems as
in Germany. In the UK, even though the monetary system was 'metricized' some
time back, one still hears 'quid.' (which I guess is one pound, five
pence?).

> And using a "regular" 24h/day time representation would eliminate a
> lot of troubles for me when highly educated customers complain about
> the hours charged on my job, simply because the can't properly
> subtract AM starting times from PM ending times...

## Hint: Your customers who cannot compute hours difference are not 'highly
educated.' They are illiterate barbarians. But you knew that.

>
> But this thread is drifting off far too much for this group, so i
> would suggestion we let it either die or take it elsewhere...

## Conversations die when everybody agrees. Do I take it that you agree with
the concept that the English system of weights and measures contributes
mightily to American hegemony and therefore peace, stability, and freedom
from want?

> > but I asked then who used 24 hour
> > clocks, and didn't get a response.
…[8 more quoted lines elided]…
> am/pm.

Those are subsets of all countries.   Which countries don't use 12 hour watches
and clocks?

> But then America can't even show dates in a rational way.

If we picked a "rational" way, it would be year/month/day.    Month/day/year or
day/month/year are equally irrational.   Some people say October 21, others say
The 21st of October.    Programmers prefer to be able to do a quick compare to
see which date is later.

There is a reason for US Military 21-OCT-2002 though, as it separates numbers
with words for clarity.
```

I almost hate to reply to this but I have an example of this being 
applied in law:

One beach area in New Jersey had parking signs that said something like:
PARKING ALLOWED BETWEEN 8AM AND 12AM.

One man was given a ticket for parking in the afternoon.  He took it to 
court and won because 12AM was midnight and the sign clearly stated that 
parking was allowed between 8AM and midnight.  I believe they changed 
the signs to read NOON.

```

Howard Brazee <howard.brazee@cusys.edu> wrote in message news:ap9j2b$i68$1@peabody.colorado.edu...
>
> On 24-Oct-2002, Robert Graham <rgraham2@nycap.rr.com> wrote:
…[11 more quoted lines elided]…
> opinion that he should have won if it had said 12PM as well.

The Royal Observatory, Greenwich, agrees with your reasoning.

From the "FAQs about the Observatory and Time"
at http://www.rog.nmm.ac.uk/museum/faq.htm

-------------------------------------
"Is noon 12 a.m. or 12 p.m.?
12 noon is neither a.m. nor p.m.

To avoid confusion, the correct designation for twelve o'clock is 12 noon or 12 midnight.
Alternatively, the twenty-four-hour-clock system may be used.

The abbreviation a.m. stands for ante-meridiem (before the Sun has crossed the line)
and p.m. for post-meridiem (after the Sun has crossed the line).

At 12 noon, the Sun is at its highest point in the sky and directly over the meridian.
It is therefore neither "ante-" nor "post-"."
-------------------------------------

I think that the RGO should be an authoritative enough source to settle the debate.
```

<a id="t-8cee6da54f"></a>
## Thread: Comparing time in microfocus Cobol — 2 msgs

_Topics: [`Compilers and vendors`](topics.md#compilers)_

### Comparing time in microfocus Cobol

- **From:** samhunt90@hotmail.com (Sam)
- **Date:** 2002-10-10T14:58:00-07:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<e20518e0.0210101358.1b206a24@posting.google.com>`

```
Hi

What's the best way to compare times in cobol?  For example, the
program should only initiate a process if the time of day is before a
certain "cutoff" time.  That "cutoff" time is retrieved from an oracle
database, where it is stored as a date field.  The date (mm/dd/yy)
will be ignored, only the time (hh:mm) will be compared to the current
system time.

Thanks,
Sam90
```

#### ↳ Re: Comparing time in microfocus Cobol

- **From:** jnjsle@aol.com (Jnjsle)
- **Date:** 2002-10-11T18:31:51+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<20021011143151.29550.00010951@mb-ml.aol.com>`
- **References:** `<e20518e0.0210101358.1b206a24@posting.google.com>`

```
Hi Sam,

If your compiler supports intrinsic functions, you can try this:

move function current-date(9:4) to curr-hh-mm

if curr-hh-mm >= oracle-hh-mm ....

The current-date function returns a 21 pos A/N field  that contains the
date/time to 100ths fo a sec as well as  +/- offeset data relative to GMT. The
example above just uses the hhmm of the time substring.

HTH, Jack.
```

## Thread: micro focus file handler (extfh) to read variable length indexed files IDXFORMAT"3" from a C program — 1 msgs

_Topics: [`Compilers and vendors`](topics.md#compilers) · [`VSAM, files, sorting`](topics.md#files)_

### micro focus file handler (extfh) to read variable length indexed files IDXFORMAT"3" from a C program

- **From:** owen@uvsoftware.ca (Owen Townsend)
- **Date:** 2002-10-10T17:36:28-07:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<9123ab55.0210101636.21cf8f75@posting.google.com>`

```
My Situation:
I am a software developer/consultant helping mainframe
customers convert to UNIX systems using Micro Focus
COBOL. I have a set of utilities written in 'C' which
replace the mainframe utilities on the UNIX system.
You can see these utilities described on my web site www.uvsoftware.ca
These utilites call D-ISAM (C-ISAM compatible)
file handlers to access fixed length indexed files which
are compatible with MF Cobol fixed length indexed
files. These are IDXFORMAT"1" as described on the Micro Focus COBOL
web site in 23.3.5.1 'types of indexed files' in the File Structures manual.
This D-ISAM solution (IDXFORMAT"1") has worked
well for my previous customers.

My Problem:
A current customer requires support for VARIABLE length
indexed files which is IDXFORMAT"3" in 23.3.5.1.
This variable format is not compatible with the variable
format supported by C-ISAM. I tried to get answers from Micro Focus
COBOL but no luck since I am not a direct user of Micro Focus COBOL.

My Questions:

1. Can I call the MF Cobol file handler (ExtFH) from my
    'C' program to access IDXFORMAT"3" files ?
    Please see 10.1.3 in the Callable File Handler manual
    which mentions support for IDXFORMAT"4"

2. Are IDXFORMAT"4" & IDXFORMAT"3"
    compatible ? If EXTFH supports 4 does it also
    support 3 ?

3. Is there as sample 'C' demo program illustrating the
    calls to EXTFH ? 10.3.2 shows the call as:
    EXTFH(op-code,&fcd);

4. Exactly how do I link the EXTFH file handler into my
    C program ? I would have expected it to be in a UNIX
    library archive such as cobol/coblib/libcobol.a
    But I dont see any extfh.o components ???
    What is the name of the archive that contains the
     EXTFH file handler ???

Hoping somebody out there has some experience in this area,
Thank You, Owen Townsend
```

## Thread: MQSeries question. — 3 msgs

### MQSeries question.

- **From:** zalekbloom@hotmail.com (Zalek Bloom)
- **Date:** 2002-10-11T09:32:20-07:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<e84e5f2f.0210110832.7dc189af@posting.google.com>`

```
I am reading MQ manual - it says the messages can be retrieve as FIFO
or by priority, the exact quote is:
"The MsgDeliverySequence attribute of the queue determines whether
messages on the queue are stored in FIFO (first in, first out)
sequence, or in FIFO within priority sequence."
My question: 
can a COBOL program change this MsgDeliverySequence attribute of the
queue, or this change is done only during a queue definition by the
system people?

Thanks,

Zalek
```

#### ↳ Re: MQSeries question.

- **From:** "Peter E. C. Dashwood" <dashwood@nospam.enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2002-10-12T13:57:57+01:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<3da81b76_4@Usenet.com>`
- **References:** `<e84e5f2f.0210110832.7dc189af@posting.google.com>`

```
In OO COBOL you have an instance of the Queue object and this is just one of
its properties, so, yes, you can change it. In Standard COBOL I have no idea
how you would do it or even if it is possible.

Pete.
Zalek Bloom <zalekbloom@hotmail.com> wrote in message

> or by priority, the exact quote is:

```

#### ↳ Re: MQSeries question.

- **From:** "Leahy, Don" <zzdlf@tdbank.ca>
- **Date:** 2002-10-15T09:02:43-04:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<UlUq9.3331$q83.569387@news20.bellglobal.com>`
- **References:** `<e84e5f2f.0210110832.7dc189af@posting.google.com>`

```
MsgDeliverySequence is a queue definition property.  Even if the definition
is changed on the fly, the messages currently in the queue are not
reordered.

You can influence the order in which messages are retrieved from the queue
by using the Priority field in the message descriptor when the message is
inserted.   Check the manuals for details.

"Zalek Bloom" <zalekbloom@hotmail.com> wrote in message
news:e84e5f2f.0210110832.7dc189af@posting.google.com...

> or by priority, the exact quote is:
…[10 more quoted lines elided]…
> Zalek

#### ↳ Re: Case stmt with Host variable

- **From:** "William M. Klein" <wmklein@nospam.ix.netcom.com>
- **Date:** 2002-10-11T15:33:20-05:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<ao7cih$hqk$1@slb2.atl.mindspring.net>`
- **References:** `<44515df0.0210111143.42a83374@posting.google.com>`

```
Do you know how to "declare" SQL host-variables?  Do you know how to code in
'85 Standard COBOL?  If so, this should be pretty easy, simply code

 EVALUATE host-variable-name-1  ALSO host-variable-name-2
    When   "Value X" Also ANY
        Perform Something-Procedure
     When   ANY Also Some-Other-Data-Item
        Perform Something-Else
      When   3      Also 4
         Perform   Another-Procedure
      When Other
         Perform Nothing-So-Far
  End-Perform


If you  don't know how to declare host-variables or don't know how to code
in '85 Standard COBOL, then give us a clearer idea of what you need to learn
(and what you already know).
```

##### ↳ ↳ Re: Case stmt with Host variable

- **From:** "Michael Mattias" <michael.mattias@gte.net>
- **Date:** 2002-10-11T21:18:57+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<4fHp9.2229$qW4.888219@newssrv26.news.prodigy.com>`
- **References:** `<44515df0.0210111143.42a83374@posting.google.com> <ao7cih$hqk$1@slb2.atl.mindspring.net>`

```
I think he means use a host variable in the SQL 'CASE' function.

No reason you could not, provided...
1. The DBMS and driver support CASE
2. You are not trying to use a host variable for a table name or column name
or ORDER BY object, because those change the statement such that it must be
re-PREPAREd. Of course, if you do totally dynamic SQL from within your COBOL
program, you can do just about anything; but the table name, column name and
order by are not 'host variable items' under any circumstances.
```
